Semantics of the Predicate: Why Concept Alone
Author clarifies that predicate's intended meaning is its concept, not its individuals, to avoid falsehood or triviality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the predicate in a categorical proposition must be taken by its concept (mafhum) and not its individuals (masduq).

Core Idea
Taking the predicate extensionally leads to either falsehood or triviality, so it must be taken by concept.

The predicate of a proposition does not work the same way as the subject. Why can't we say that 'every human is an animal' means that the individuals of human are the same as the individuals of animal? The author explains that this would either be false (because animal includes non-humans) or trivial (if the individuals are identical). Therefore, the predicate is always taken by its concept. This lesson walks through the reasoning to internalize the rule.

Guided Path
1
Assume the predicate is taken extensionally
Start with the hypothesis that the predicate refers to its individuals, just like the subject. For example, in 'Every human is an animal', assume 'animal' means the set of all individual animals.
2
Consider two cases for the relationship of individuals
The individuals of the predicate (animal) can either be different from the individuals of the subject (human) or the same. The passage examines both.
3
If different, the proposition becomes false
If the individuals of 'animal' are different from those of 'human', then 'Every human is an animal' would be false, because a human would not be an animal (if the sets are disjoint). But we know the proposition is true, so the individuals cannot be different.
The passage says: 'if its referent is different, the predication would be false because the individuals of animal are distinct from those of human, since animal is true of humans and other beasts.'
4
If identical, the proposition becomes trivial
If the individuals are exactly the same, then the proposition would be like saying 'all humans are humans', which is uninformative. Predication would lose its benefit.
The passage gives the example: if 'human' in 'every writer is human' is taken extensionally, it becomes 'Zayd, Amr, etc. are Zayd, Amr, etc.', which is pointless.
5
Conclude that the predicate must be taken by concept
Since both extensional interpretations lead to either falsehood or triviality, the only consistent interpretation is that the predicate refers to its concept (mafhum), not its individuals.
This is the 'known and determined view' as stated in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات: الأَوَّلُ: المَقْصُودُ مِنَ المَوْضُوعِ مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ: أَفْرَادُهُ؛ بِدَلِيلِ قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ، إِلَّا الطَّبِيعِيَّةَ فَيُقْصَدُ بِهِ فِيهَا مَفْهُومُهُ، وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَفْهُومُهُ، لَا مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَعْرُوفُ وَالمُتَعَيِّنُ. قوله: (مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ: أَفْرَادُهُ) اعلم: أنَّ الألفاظ المستعملة عندهم هنا: المصدوق، والماصدق، والذات؛ وكلُّها بمعنى واحدٍ؛ أي: الأفراد التي يصدقُ عليها الشَّيء، فمصدوق الموضوع وماصدقاتُهُ وذاتُهُ: أفراده كـ «زيد، وعمرو» في: «كل إنسان كاتب». والماصدقُ: بعضهم ينطق به ساكن الصَّاد مجموعاً فيه الساكنان على غير حد الجمع، وبعضهم بفتحها وهو منقول من «ما» الموصولة الاسمية وصنتها؛ إذ أصلُه: «ما صدق عليه الشَّيء». قوله: (بِدَلِيلِ قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ) فلو قصدنا بإنسان في قولنا: كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان الحقيقة من حيث هي؛ أعني: المفهوم الذي هو الحيوان الناطق، لم يصح دخول كل عليه؛ إذ الحقيقة من حيث هي شيء واحد لا تعدُّد فيه، ويلزم أيضاً: أن لا يصدق «كل إنسان شخص». قوله: (وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَفْهُومُهُ) أي: لأنا لو أردنا الماصدق: فإما أن يكون مصدوقه غير مصدوق الموضوع، فيبطل الحمل للكذب؛ لأنَّ أفراد «الحيوان» في قولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان مباينةٌ لأفراد الإنسان؛ لصدقها بأفراد الإنسان وغيره من العجماوات. وإن كان مصدوقه هو مصدوق الموضوع، لزم انتفاء الفائدة في الحمل، فإذا أردنا بـ «إنسان» في قولنا: كلُّ كاتب إنسان: زيد وعمرو ونحوهما صدقاً، قلنا: زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم هم زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم، وذلك خال عن الفائدة.
Notices: The first: The intended meaning of the subject is its referent (masduq), i.e., its individuals, as evidenced by the acceptance of quantifiers, except for the natural universal (tabi'iyya) in which case its concept is intended. And the intended meaning of the predicate is its concept, not its referent. This is the known and determined view. His saying: (its referent, i.e., its individuals) Know that the terms used here are: al-masduq, al-masadiq, and al-dhat; all have the same meaning, i.e., the individuals of which the thing is true. So the masduq of the subject, its masadiq, and its dhat are its individuals, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' in 'Every human is a writer'. As for al-masadiq, some pronounce it with a sukun on the sad, combining two sukun against the pattern of plural, and some with a fatha, and it is derived from the relative pronoun 'ma' and its particle; its original is 'ma sadaqa 'alayhi al-shay'. His saying: (as evidenced by the acceptance of quantifiers) For if by 'human' in 'Every human is an animal' we intended the quiddity as such, i.e., the concept 'rational animal', it would not be correct to apply 'every' because the quiddity as such is one thing with no multiplicity, and then 'every human is a person' would not be true. His saying: (and the intended meaning of the predicate is its concept) i.e., because if we intended the referent, then either its referent is different from the referent of the subject, in which case the predication would be false because the individuals of 'animal' in 'Every human is an animal' are distinct from the individuals of 'human', since 'animal' is true of humans and other beasts; or if its referent is the same as the referent of the subject, then predication would be pointless, for if by 'human' in 'Every writer is human' we meant Zayd, Amr, etc., then we say 'Zayd, Amr, Khalid, etc. are Zayd, Amr, Khalid, etc.', which is devoid of benefit.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the semantics of subject and predicate in quantified propositions. The author states that the intended meaning of the subject is its individuals (masduq), as shown by the fact that quantifiers like 'every' apply to them. The exception is the 'natural universal' (al-tabi'iyya), where the concept itself is intended. For the predicate, the intended meaning is always its concept, not its individuals. The commentary then explains the terminology: 'masduq', 'masadiq', and 'dhat' all refer to the individuals of which a term is true. With an example, 'Every human is a writer', the masduq of 'human' is Zayd, Amr, etc. The reason for this is given: if we intended the quiddity (concept) of human in 'Every human is an animal', then the quantifier 'every' could not apply because the quiddity is one thing without multiplicity. Additionally, if the predicate's intended meaning were its individuals, then either the individuals differ (making the proposition false, as 'animal' includes non-humans) or they are identical (making the proposition trivial, like saying all writers are themselves). Therefore, the predicate must be taken by concept to avoid falsity or triviality.

Key Terms
مصدوق
مفهوم
طبيعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The proposition becomes true
B) The proposition becomes false
C) The proposition becomes necessary
D) The proposition becomes trivial

Key Takeaway
The predicate must be interpreted intensionally (by concept) to preserve meaningful and truthful predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the theological proposition 'Every contingent thing is created' (kullu hadithin makhluqun). If the predicate 'created' were taken extensionally, what would be the consequence? Show that it leads to falsehood or triviality, following the passage's method.

The Arabic Copula 'Huwa' and Binary vs Ternary Propositions
Explains non-Arabic copulative words and the Arabic choice of 'huwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an Arabic proposition is binary (thuna'iyya) or ternary (thulathiyya) based on the presence of an explicit copula.

Core Idea
The copula is the logical backbone of a proposition, and Arabic adopted 'huwa' from everyday usage.

The structure of a proposition in logic can include an explicit copula—the word that links subject and predicate. Non-Arabic languages like Persian and Greek have dedicated copula words, and Arab philosophers chose 'huwa' to serve this role in Arabic. Understanding this distinction helps you recognize how logicians formalize Arabic sentences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in an Arabic statement
Take a simple Arabic sentence like 'زيد قائم' (Zayd is standing). The subject is 'زيد' and the predicate is 'قائم'. Note whether a linking word is present between them.
2
Determine if the copula is explicit
If the sentence includes a word like 'هو' (huwa) between subject and predicate, it has an explicit copula. Otherwise, the copula is implicit (omitted).
Possessive or equational sentences often omit the copula in Arabic.
3
Classify the proposition as binary or ternary
If the copula is omitted, the proposition is called 'thuna'iyya' (binary). If the copula is present, it is 'thulathiyya' (ternary).
This terminology is specific to logic texts.
4
Explain why 'huwa' was chosen as the Arabic copula
Philosophers chose 'huwa' because it was already used in Arabic in contexts where Persian 'hast' appears, e.g., 'هذا هو زيد' (This is Zayd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا غَيْرُ العَرَبِ، فَلَهُمْ أَلْفَاظ أَخَرُ تَكُونُ رَابِطَةٌ، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ عِنْدَ حَذْفِ الرَّابِطَةِ : تُنَائِيَّةٌ، وَعِنْدَ ذِكْرِهَا : ثُلاثِيَّةٌ. قوله : (وَأَمَّا غَيْرُ العَرَبِ، فَلَهُمْ أَلْفَاظُ أَخَرُ ... إلخ) المستعمل عند الفلاسفة أصالة في الربط : «هست» في الفارسية، و: «استبر» في اليونانية. ولما احتاج الفلاسفة الذين يتكلمون بالعربيَّة أوّل الوضع لفظاً يقوم مقام ذلك، التمسوا في لغة العرب لفظاً ينقلونه إلى ذلك، فاختار بعضهم لفظة: «هو» ؛ لأنَّها قد تستعمل في بعض الأمكنة التي تستعمل فيها لفظة: «هست»؛ كما في قولنا : «هذا هو زيد، وهذا هو الشّاعر».
And as for non-Arabs, they have other words that serve as a copula. The proposition when the copula is omitted is called 'binary', and when it is mentioned it is called 'ternary'. He said: (And as for non-Arabs...) The term used originally by philosophers for the copula is 'hast' in Persian, and 'est' in Greek. When philosophers who spoke Arabic first needed a word to replace that, they sought in the Arabic language a word to transfer to that meaning, so some chose the word 'huwa' because it is sometimes used in places where 'hast' is used, as in our saying: 'This is Zayd, and this is the poet'.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage defines a key distinction about the structure of propositions. The main text states that non-Arabic languages have explicit copulative terms; when the copula is omitted, the proposition is called 'thuna'iyya' (binary), and when it is present, 'thulathiyya' (ternary). The commentator then elaborates by giving examples: Persian uses 'hast' and Greek uses 'est' as the primary copula. He explains that when Muslim philosophers first began writing in Arabic, they needed a term to function similarly. They selected 'huwa' because it was already used in Arabic in similar contexts, such as 'this is Zayd' and 'this is the poet'. This shows awareness of cross-linguistic logical terminology and the adaptation of Arabic to serve logical purposes. All examples are from the Arabic language itself.

Key Terms
رابطة
ثنائية
ثلاثية
هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The copula is the logical link in a proposition; Arabic adopts 'huwa' based on pre-existing usage, showing the integration of ordinary language into technical logic.

Subject Extension vs. Predicate Concept in Propositions
Clarifies subject and predicate term intentions in propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the intended reference of subject and predicate terms in a categorical proposition, and explain the exception for natural propositions.

Core Idea
The subject refers to individuals, the predicate to the concept; mixing them destroys meaning.

In Islamic logic, the role of subject and predicate terms is not arbitrary. The classical logicians determined that the subject term of a proposition (except in natural propositions) refers to its extension—the individuals that fall under it—while the predicate term refers to its concept—the meaning itself. This distinction, proven by the acceptance of quantifiers and the need for informative predication, is essential for evaluating the truth and utility of statements in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for the subject term
The text states that the intended meaning of the subject is its extension (masduq), i.e., its individuals, as evidenced by its ability to take quantifiers like 'every' or 'some'.
Example: in 'Every human is a writer,' the subject 'human' refers to individuals like Zayd and Amr.
2
Understand the evidence from quantifiers
If the subject referred to its concept (the quiddity), a quantifier like 'every' could not be applied because the concept is one unified meaning without multiplicity. This shows the subject must refer to its individuals.
The commentator argues that if 'human' in 'Every human is an animal' meant the concept 'rational animal', then 'every' would be meaningless since the concept is singular.
3
Recognize the exception for natural propositions
In natural propositions (tabi'iyya), where the subject is taken as a quiddity without quantification, the subject intends its concept, not its extension.
For example, 'Human is rational animal' considers the essence, not individuals.
4
Learn the rule for the predicate term
The predicate always intends its concept (mafhum), not its extension. This is the well-known position.
In 'Every human is an animal', 'animal' intends the meaning of animalness, not the set of all animals.
5
Grasp the proof from predication informativeness
If the predicate intended its extension, two problems arise: (1) if the extensions differ, the proposition is false (e.g., 'animal' includes non-humans); (2) if extensions are identical, the predication is trivial and uninformative (e.g., 'Every writer is human' would just say 'Zayd is Zayd').
Thus, the predicate must give new information about the subject's concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأَوَّلُ : المَقْصُودُ مِنَ المَوْضُوع مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ : أَفْرَادُهُ؛ بِدَلِيلِ قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ، إِلَّا الطَّبِيعِيَّةَ فَيُقْصَدُ بِهِ فِيهَا مَفْهُومُهُ ، وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَنْهُومُهُ، لَا مَصْدُوقُهُ ؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَعْرُوفُ وَالمُتَعَيِّنُ. قوله : (مَصْدُوقُهُ؛ أَيْ: أَفْرَادُهُ) اعلم : أنَّ الألفاظ المستعملة عندهم هنا: المصدوق، والماصدق، والذات؛ وكلُّها بمعنى واحدٍ؛ أي: الأفراد التي يصدقُ عليها الشَّيء، فمصدوق الموضوع وماصدقاتُهُ وذاتُهُ : أفراده كـ «زيد ، وعمرو» في : «كل إنسان كاتب». والماصدقُ : بعضهم ينطق به ساكن الصَّاد مجموعاً فيه الساكنان على غير حد الجمع، وبعضهم بفتحها وهو منقول من «ما» الموصولة الاسمية وصنتها؛ إذ أصلُه : «ما صدق عليه الشَّيء». قوله : (بِدَلِيل قَبُولِ السُّوْرِ) فلو قصدنا بإنسان في قولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ حَيَوَان الحقيقة من حيث هي؛ أعني : المفهوم الذي هو الحيوان الناطق، لم يصح دخول كل عليه ; إذ الحقيقة من حيث هي شيء واحد لا تعدُّد فيه، ويلزم أيضاً : أن لا يصدق «كل إنسان شخص». قوله : (وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ مَفْهُومُهُ) أي : لأنا لو أردنا الماصدق : - فإما أن يكون مصدوقه غير مصدوق الموضوع، فيبطل الحمل للكذب؛ لأنَّ أفراد «الحيوان» في قولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان مباينةٌ لأفراد الإنسان»؛ لصدقها بأفراد الإنسان وغيره من العجماوات . - وإن كان مصدوقه هو مصدوق الموضوع، لزم انتفاء الفائدة في الحمل، فإذا أردنا بـ «إنسان» في قولنا : كلُّ كاتب إنسان»: زيد وعمرو ونحوهما صدقاً، قلنا : زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم هم زيد وعمرو وخالد ونحوهم»، وذلك خال عن الفائدة.
The first [point]: The intended meaning from the subject is its extension, i.e., its individuals; evidenced by its acceptance of quantifiers, except in natural [propositions] where its concept is intended. And the intended meaning from the predicate is its concept, not its extension. This is the well-known and determined [view]. He said: (its extension, i.e., its individuals) Know that the terms used here are: al-masduq, al-masadiq, and al-dhat; all have the same meaning, i.e., the individuals of which the thing is true. So the extension of the subject and its veridicals and its essence are its individuals, like Zayd and 'Amr in 'Every human is a writer'. As for 'al-masadiq', some pronounce it with a silent sad and with two silent letters contrary to the rule of plural, and some with a fatha, and it is derived from the relative clause 'ma sadaqa 'alayhi al-shay'. He said: (by evidence of accepting the quantifier) For if we intended by 'human' in our saying 'Every human is an animal' the quiddity as such, i.e., the concept which is 'rational animal', it would not be correct for 'every' to enter upon it, since the quiddity as such is one thing with no multiplicity, and it would also entail that 'Every human is a person' is not true. He said: (and the intended meaning from the predicate is its concept) That is because if we intended its extension, then either its extension is different from the extension of the subject, in which case the predication becomes false because the individuals of 'animal' in our saying 'Every human is an animal' are distinct from the individuals of 'human', since it applies to individuals of human and other non-speaking beings; or its extension is the same as the extension of the subject, which would eliminate the benefit of the predication. For if we intended by 'human' in 'Every writer is human' Zayd, 'Amr and the like, we would be saying 'Zayd, 'Amr, Khalid, and the like are Zayd, 'Amr, Khalid, and the like', which is devoid of benefit.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage explains the crucial distinction between the extension (masduq) and intension (mafhum) of terms in a proposition. The main text states that in a proposition, the subject term is intended to refer to its extension (individuals), except in natural propositions where the concept is intended. The predicate term, however, always refers to its concept, not its extension. The commentator expands by clarifying terminology: 'masduq', 'masadiq', and 'dhat' are synonyms for 'individuals'. He provides an example: in 'Every human is a writer', the subject 'human' refers to individuals like Zayd and 'Amr. He then proves why the subject must refer to extension: if it referred to the concept (the quiddity), then quantifiers like 'every' cannot apply, because a concept is a single entity and cannot be partitioned. Moreover, the predication 'Every human is a person' would fail. He then proves why the predicate must refer to concept: if it referred to extension, then either the extensions differ (causing falsehood) or they are identical (making the statement trivial). For instance, if 'animal' in 'Every human is an animal' referred to its extension, it would be false because animal includes non-humans. And if it referred to the same individuals as human, then 'Every writer is human' would reduce to an identity statement with no informative content. This reasoning is foundational for understanding the semantics of categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
مصدوق
مفهوم
طبيعية
سور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the predicate's extension cannot be intended in a proposition like 'Every human is an animal'?
A) Because the extension of 'animal' is outside the extension of 'human'.
B) Because the extension of 'animal' includes both humans and non-humans, making the proposition false.
C) Because the predicate's concept is always the same as the subject's concept.
D) Because the quantifier 'every' only applies to concepts.

Key Takeaway
In a categorical proposition, the subject term refers to its individuals (except in natural propositions), while the predicate term always refers to its concept—a distinction essential for meaningful predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every fard [obligatory act] is rewarded by Allah.' Explain why the subject 'fard' must refer to its extension (individual obligatory acts) and the predicate 'rewarded' to its concept. What would be the problem if both referred to their extensions?
?
Identify a natural proposition from the Islamic sciences (e.g., 'Worship is obedience' or 'Iman is affirmation') and explain why the subject here intends its concept rather than its extension.

The Title (Unwan) of the Subject: Definition and Types
Defines the 'unwan' and explains its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of 'unwan' (title) of the subject in a categorical proposition and classify examples accordingly.

Core Idea
The title of the subject is the concept that identifies the individuals, not the individuals themselves.

In logic, when we say 'Every human is an animal', the subject term 'human' picks out individual humans through a concept. This concept is called the 'unwan' (title). It can be the complete essence, a part, or an accidental attribute. Understanding this clarifies what exactly is being predicated.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition of 'unwan'
The passage states: 'العنوان هو وصف الموضوع ومفهومه' – the title is the description and concept of the subject. Note that 'wasf' and 'mafhum' are synonymous here.
Do not confuse the concept with the individuals (dhat).
2
Identify the three possibilities
The title can be: (1) the complete quiddity (تمام الماهية), e.g., 'human' for humans; (2) a part of the quiddity (جزء الماهية), e.g., 'animal' for humans; (3) external to the quiddity (خارج عنها), e.g., 'laughing' for humans. Each example is given in the text.
The third type is an accident, not part of the essence.
3
Distinguish concept from individuals
The individuals (أفراد) are the actual particulars that the concept applies to. The 'unwan' is the concept by which they are known, like a book's title identifies its content.
The subject of predication is the individuals, but they are referred to via the concept.
4
Apply to new examples
Take a proposition like 'Every khamr is haram'. The unwan is the concept 'khamr', which is an accidental attribute (since intoxication can be absent). This is an example of the third type.
Use Islamic examples from fiqh or kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خِلَافاً لِمَا فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ
وَالعُنْوَانُ هُوَ وَصْفُ المَوْضُوعِ وَمَفْهُومُهُ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ تَمَامَ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، أَوْ جُزْءَهَا، نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ»، أَوْ خَارِجاً عَنْهَا؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ ضَاحِكٍ إِنْسَانٌ».
قوله : (خِلَافاً لِمَا فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ) أي : حيث قال : الموضوع يحتمل منه أربع مفهومات: الأوَّل: ذاتُهُ وحقيقته. الثَّاني : أفراده. الثالث: الموصوف به. الرابع : ماصدق عليه من غير اعتبار أحدهما. وذلك لما فيه مِنَ الفساد، وذلك أنَّه أطلق الذات على الحقيقة، والمعروف في هذا المقام إطلاقها على الأفراد، وأيضاً فإنَّ قوله : «الثَّالث الموصوف به تكرار مع ما قبله؛ إذ هو عينه؛ إذ الموصوف بالإنسانية فى قولنا : الإنسان حَيَوان مثلاً أفراده.
قوله : (وَالعُنْوَانُ هُوَ وَصْفُ المَوْضُوعِ وَمَفْهُومُهُ) لو قال : ومفهوم الموضوع هو وصفه وعنوانه لكان أنسب .
وأشار الشارح بهذا الكلام إلى أنَّ وصف الموضوع وعنوانه ومفهومه ألفاظ تستعمل عندهم هنا، وهي بمعنى واحد وهو حقيقةُ الشَّيء ومدلوله المطابقي، وأنَّ العنوان يكون تمام ماهيه الذات، ويكون جزءاً لها، ويكون خارجاً عنها؛ فمفهوم «إنسان» في المثال الأول، ومفهوم «حيوان» في الثاني، ومفهوم «ضاحك» في الثَّالث هو المسمَّى بعنوان الموضوع. والأفراد التي صدق عليها هذا العنوان هي المسماة : ذات الموضوع. وسمي المفهوم : عنواناً؛ لأنَّ الأفراد تعرف به كما يعرف الكتاب بعنوانه، فالمحكوم عليه الأفراد، لا من حيث كون مفهوم الموضوع حقيقتها، وإلا لخرج «كلُّ ضاحك إنسان»، ولا من حيث كونه صفةٌ عارضة لها، وإلا لخرج «كلُّ إنسان حيوان»، بل من حيث كونه صادقاً عليها، فتكون جزئيات له؛ سواء كان حقيقتها، أو جزء حقيقتها، أو وصفاً لها.
Contrary to what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. And the 'title' (unwan) is the description of the subject and its concept; whether it is the complete quiddity of its individuals, such as 'Every human is an animal', or a part of it, such as 'Every animal is a body', or external to it, such as 'Every laughing thing is a human'. His statement: (Contrary to what is in Sharh al-Mukhtasar) means: where he said: The subject admits of four concepts: first: its essence and reality; second: its individuals; third: the thing described by it; fourth: what it applies to without considering either. This is because of the corruption in it, that he applied 'essence' to reality, while the known usage in this context is its application to individuals. Also, his statement: 'third: the thing described by it' is repetition with what preceded; for it is the same; for the thing described by humanity in our statement 'Human is an animal' is its individuals. His statement: (And the title is the description of the subject and its concept) — if he had said: 'And the concept of the subject is its description and its title', it would be more appropriate. The commentator indicated by this speech that the description of the subject, its title, and its concept are terms used by them here, and they are synonymous: the reality of the thing and its true (mutabiqi) signification; and that the title can be the complete essence of the individuals, or a part of it, or external to it. So the concept of 'human' in the first example, the concept of 'animal' in the second, and the concept of 'laughing' in the third is what is called the title of the subject. And the individuals that this title applies to are called the 'essence' (dhat) of the subject. And the concept is called a 'title' because the individuals are known by it, just as a book is known by its title. So the subject of predication is the individuals, not insofar as the concept of the subject is their reality, otherwise 'Every laughing thing is a human' would be excluded; nor insofar as it is an accidental attribute of them, otherwise 'Every human is an animal' would be excluded; rather insofar as it is true of them, so they are particulars of it, whether it is their reality, or part of their reality, or a description of them.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that defines the term 'عنوان' (unwan/title) of the subject in a proposition. It begins by stating that the title is the description and concept of the subject, and it can be the complete essence (like 'human' in 'Every human is an animal'), a part of the essence (like 'animal' in 'Every animal is a body'), or external to the essence (like 'laughing' in 'Every laughing thing is a human'). Then it criticizes the view in Sharh al-Mukhtasar for having four confused concepts, and clarifies that the 'title' is synonymous with 'description' and 'concept', and that the individuals are the true subject of predication, not the concept itself. The commentary uses the analogy of a book title to explain how the concept identifies the individuals.

The 'unwan' of the subject is the concept that applies to the individuals, falling into three categories: complete essence, part of essence, or accidental attribute.

Refining the Subject Concept: Critique of the Fourfold Division
Critiques the fourfold division of the subject from Sharh al-Mukhtasar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two errors in the fourfold division of the subject from Sharh al-Mukhtasar and explain the correct threefold classification.

Core Idea
The fourfold division confuses the concept with the individuals and contains redundancy.

A previous commentator in Sharh al-Mukhtasar proposed that the subject admits four concepts: essence, individuals, the thing described, and what it applies to. Our author criticizes this as confused and redundant. This lesson unpacks that critique and reinforces the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourfold division
According to Sharh al-Mukhtasar, the subject can be considered as: (1) its essence/reality, (2) its individuals, (3) the thing described by it, (4) what it applies to without considering either.
The division attempts to capture different aspects of the subject term.
2
Identify the first error: misuse of 'dhat'
The author says the first concept 'essence' uses 'dhat' incorrectly; in this context, 'dhat' should refer to the individuals, not the reality. The correct term for reality is 'haqiqa' or 'mahiyya'.
This is a terminological shift from earlier usage.
3
Identify the second error: redundancy
The third concept 'the thing described by it' is actually the same as the second (individuals). For example, in 'Human is an animal', the thing described by 'human' is the individuals of human.
Thus the fourfold division collapses to three: reality, individuals, and what it applies to (without consideration).
4
Accept the correct threefold classification
The author's view is that the 'unwan' (concept) can be the complete essence, a part, or external. The individuals are the 'dhat' (subject of predication). There is no need for a fourth category.
This aligns with the standard logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Modality of Subject Truth: Actual vs Possible
Debates whether the subject's truth applies actually or possibly to its individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual (bi-al-fi'l) and possible (bi-al-imkan) modality of the subject's truth and identify which opinion the author supports.

Core Idea
The subject's truth is actual not possible, or definitions collapse.

In logic, how we understand the subject of a proposition matters for truth and inference. This lesson explores a key debate between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi: does the subject's description apply to its individuals in actuality or merely in possibility? The passage clarifies these concepts with examples and defends the actuality view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
The passage mentions three opinions on the modality of the subject's truth: actual, possible, or following the predicate. Focus on the first two.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's 'actuality'
Ibn Sina holds that the subject's truth is actual, meaning the individuals really possess the description in external reality, not hypothetically.
Actuality here does not require necessity or perpetuity; it just means actual affection.
3
Understand al-Farabi's 'possibility'
Al-Farabi's possibility is interpreted as potentiality (quwwa) or as not-impossible. The passage distinguishes these two meanings.
The author sides with Ibn Sina, rejecting the potentiality interpretation.
4
Apply the concepts to an example
Consider 'Every white thing is a body'. Under actuality, a black person is not included because he is not actually white. Under possibility (as not-impossible), he could be included if whiteness is possible for him.
This shows how the modality affects the extension of the subject.
5
Identify the author's stance
The author explicitly follows Ibn Sina and the majority of later scholars, affirming actuality over possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحَقُّ : أَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ بِالفِعْلِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا، لا بِالإِمْكَانِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ الفَارَابِيُّ ؛
قوله : (كَيْفِيَّةَ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ ... إلخ) اعلم : أنَّ وصف المحمول هو محض القصد، وإليه ينصرف التصديق والتكذيب، وغير ذلك من لوازم الخبر كالمطابقة وعدمها، وضرورة النسبة وغيرها من سائر الجهات، ووصف الموضوع وإن كان خارجاً عن القصد ؛ إلَّا أنَّهم لم يهملوه على الجملة، بل اختلفوا في كيفية صدقه على أفراده على ثلاثة أقوال: بالفعل، أو بالإمكان، أو هو تابع لوصف المحمول في فعليَّته وإمكانه .
قوله : (بِالفِعْلِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا) المراد بـ «الفعل» على القول باعتباره: اتصاف الذات بالعنوان حقيقةً من غير تعرُّض لكون ذلك الاتصاف واجباً؛ أي بالضرورة، أو غيره دائماً، أو غيره؛ فإذا قلنا مثلاً : «كلُّ أبيض جسم» دخل فيه : الزَّنجي على القول بالإمكان؛ لإمكان اتصافه بالبياض، ولم يدخل فيه على القول باعتبار الفعل؛ لأنَّه لم يتصف به خارجاً .
وليس المراد بـ «الفعل»: حصوله فرضاً لا خارجاً، حتّى يدخل الزنجي في قولنا : «كلُّ أبيض جسم» إن فرضه الذهن أبيض، كما ذكره السَّعد؛ إذ الأوّل هو الذي يشهد له استعمال العرب؛ قاله الهلالي .
وتبع ابن سينا على هذا القول المتأخّرون، فهو قول الأكثر، وابن سينا هو الرئيس الفاضل الماهر المعروف بـ «الشَّيخ» في ألسنة القوم إذا أطلق، وهو أبو علي الحسن بن عبد الله بن سينا - من تحت، فنون، فألف مقصورة، له التاليف المشهورة، وتوفي بهمذان سنة ثمان وعشرين وأربع مئة .
قوله : (لَا بِالإِمْكَانِ) المراد به : ما يقابل الامتناع، فيصدقُ بالفعل، وليس المراد به: «ما يقابل الفعل»، وهو القوَّة، وينبئ عليهما دخول النطفة في الإنسان إذا قلنا : «كلُّ إنسان حيوان» إذا أريد بالإمكان القوَّة؛ لأنَّ النُّطفة إنسان بالقوة، دون ما إذا أردنا به ما هو أعمُّ مِنَ الفعل؛ إذ يمتنع النطفة بالإنسانية، فليست داخلةً في الموضوع؛ إذ ليست إنساناً بالإمكان الذي هو أعم مِنَ الفعل . و حاصل ما تقدَّم : أنَّا إذا قلنا مثلاً : «كل كاتب ضاحك :
The truth is that the modality of its truth regarding its individuals, when absolute, is actual (bi-al-fi'l) as Ibn Sina says, not possible (bi-al-imkan) as al-Farabi says. His statement: (the modality of its truth regarding its individuals... etc.) Know that the description of the predicate is the core intention, and to it assent and denial are directed, and other necessary concomitants of the proposition such as correspondence and lack thereof, the necessity of the relation, and other aspects. And the description of the subject, although outside the intention, they did not neglect it entirely. Rather they differed regarding the modality of its truth regarding its individuals into three opinions: actual, or possible, or it follows the description of the predicate in its actuality and possibility. His statement: (actual as Ibn Sina says) What is intended by 'actual' according to the opinion that considers it: the affection of the essence (dhat) by the title (unwan) in reality without engaging whether that affection is necessary (i.e., necessarily) or other, constant or other. For instance, when we say 'Every white thing is a body', it includes the black person on the opinion of possibility because of the possibility of his being white, but it does not include him on the opinion considering actuality because he is not actually white. And it is not intended by 'actual': its occurrence hypothetically not externally, such that the black person would enter into our statement 'Every white thing is a body' if the mind assumes him white, as Sa'd mentioned; for the first (i.e., actual external affection) is what the usage of the Arabs testifies to, as al-Hilali said. And the later scholars followed Ibn Sina in this opinion, so it is the opinion of the majority. And Ibn Sina is the excellent, virtuous, skilled chief, known as 'al-Shaykh' in the tongues of the people when absolute, and he is Abu Ali al-Hasan bin Abd Allah bin Sina, and his famous works, and he died in Hamadhan in the year 428. His statement: (not possible) What is intended by it is that which contrasts with impossibility, so it applies actually, and it is not intended by it 'that which contrasts with actuality', which is potentiality. And both are indicated by the inclusion of the sperm-drop in 'human' when we say 'Every human is an animal' if by possible we mean potentiality; because the sperm-drop is human potentially, unlike when we mean by it what is more general than actuality; for the sperm-drop is impossible of humanity, so it is not included in the subject because it is not human by the possibility which is more general than actuality. And the sum of what preceded: that if we say, for example, 'Every writer is laughing:' (incomplete).
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the modality of the subject's truth regarding its individuals. The author declares that the correct view is that when we affirm a predicate of a subject in an absolute proposition, the subject's truth must be actual (bi-al-fi'l) — meaning the individuals actually possess the description — not merely possible (bi-al-imkan) as al-Farabi held. The commentary elaborates: the predicate is the main focus, but the subject's truth is also considered. Three opinions exist: actual, possible, or following the predicate. The actuality is defined as real affection without considering necessity or duration. An example: 'Every white thing is a body' includes a black person only if we allow possible whiteness, but not under actuality because he is not actually white. The text clarifies that 'actual' means external actuality, not hypothetical. It then notes that later scholars followed Ibn Sina, and gives a brief biography of Ibn Sina. Then it discusses 'possible': it means not impossible, so it applies actually; it does not mean potentiality. It gives the example of a sperm-drop in 'Every human is an animal': if 'possible' means potential, the sperm-drop is potentially human and included; but if 'possible' means merely not impossible (i.e., actual possibility), the sperm-drop is not human at all, so excluded. The passage ends mid-sentence with an example.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in the proposition 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is haram', the subject 'intoxicant' must be considered as:
A) Actually intoxicating (bi-al-fi'l)
B) Possibly intoxicating (bi-al-imkan) as potentiality
C) Possibly intoxicating as not-impossible
D) Irrelevant

Key Takeaway
For absolute propositions, the subject's truth is actual (bi-al-fi'l), not potential or merely possible, as upheld by Ibn Sina and the majority of later logicians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every human is a rational animal'. According to the author's view (actuality), should a sperm-drop be considered human? Explain why or why not using the logic from the lesson.
?
In the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram', if we follow al-Farabi's possibility (as not-impossible), would unfermented grape juice be included? Compare with Ibn Sina's actuality.

Propositions

The Modality of Subject Predication in Unqualified Propositions
Author explains three views on how the subject term applies to its individuals in unqualified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three views on the modality of subject predication in unqualified propositions and justify the preference for actual attribution based on Arabic usage.

Core Idea
Subject terms apply actually, not possibly, in unqualified propositions.

When we say 'Every writer is laughing,' does 'writer' include only those who actually write, or also those who could possibly write? Ibn Sina and al-Farabi disagreed on this point, and a third view ties the subject's modality to the predicate. This lesson unpacks these positions, clarifies their implications with examples, and examines the linguistic evidence that supports Ibn Sina's actuality view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Find the three opinions on how the subject term ('عنوان') applies to its individuals when the proposition is unqualified: actual (بالفعل), possible (بالإمكان), and following the predicate.
2
Define 'actual' and 'possible'
According to the commentator, 'actual' means real external attribution, not hypothetical mental attribution. 'Possible' is defined as what is not impossible, which includes actuality, but al-Farabi's view is sometimes interpreted as potentiality (القوة).
Distinguish the two senses of 'imkān'.
3
Test with the white/body example
Apply the views to 'Every white thing is a body.' Under Ibn Sina's view, a black person is excluded because not actually white. Under al-Farabi's possibility view, the black person is included because he could possibly be white. Understand why each view includes or excludes.
The example shows the practical difference.
4
Apply the Quranic test
Analyze the verses on theft and adultery: the command applies only to those who actually committed the act ('بالفعل'), not to everyone who could possibly do so. This supports Ibn Sina's actuality view over the possibility view.
Linguistic usage is the decisive criterion.

وَالحَقُّ : أَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةً صِدْقِهِ عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ بِالفِعْلِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا، لا بِالإِمْكَانِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ الفَارَابِيُّ .
قوله : (كَيْفِيَّةَ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ ... إلخ) اعلم : أنَّ وصف المحمول هو محظ القصد، وإليه ينصرف التصديق والتكذيب، وغير ذلك من لوازم الخبر كالمطابقة ،وعدمها، وضرورة النسبة وغيرها من سائر الجهات، ووصف الموضوع وإن كان خارجاً عن القصد ؛ إلَّا أنَّهم لم يهملوه على الجملة، بل اختلفوا في كيفية صدقه على أفراده على ثلاثة أقوال بالفعل، أو بالإمكان، أو هو تابع لوصف المحمول في فعليَّته وإمكانه .
قوله : (بِالفِعْلِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا) المراد بـ «الفعل على القول باعتباره: اتصاف الذات بالعنوان حقيقةً من غير تعرُّض لكون ذلك الاتصاف واجباً ؛ أي بالضرورة، أو غيره دائماً، أو غيره؛ فإذا قلنا مثلاً : كلُّ أبيض جسم» دخل فيه : الزَّنجي على القول بالإمكان؛ لإمكان اتصافه بالبياض، ولم يدخل فيه على القول باعتبار الفعل؛ لأنَّه لم يتصف به خارجاً .
وليس المراد بـ «الفعل»: حصوله فرضاً لا خارجاً، حتّى يدخل الزنجي في قولنا : كلُّ أبيض جسم إن فرضه الذهن أبيض، كما ذكره السَّعد ؛ إذ الأوّل هو الذي يشهد له استعمال العرب؛ قاله الهلالي .
وتبع ابن سينا على هذا القول المتأخّرون، فهو قول الأكثر ، وابن سينا هو الرئيس الفاضل الماهر المعروف بـ «الشَّيخ» في ألسنة القوم إذا أطلق، وهو أبو علي الحسن بن عبد الله بن سينا - من تحت، فنون، فألف مقصورة ، له التاليف المشهورة، وتوفي بهمذان سنة ثمان وعشرين وأربع مئة .
اتِّصافُ
قوله : : (لَا بِالإِمْكَانِ المراد به : ما يقابل الامتناع، فيصدقُ بالفعل، وليس المراد به: «ما يقابل الفعل»، وهو القوَّة، وينبئ عليهما دخول النطفة في الإنسان إذا قلنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان» إذا أريد بالإمكان القوَّة؛ لأنَّ النُّطفة إنسان بالقوة، دون ما إذا أردنا به ما هو أعمُّ مِنَ الفعل؛ إذ يمتنع النطفة بالإنسانية، فليست داخلةً في الموضوع؛ إذ ليست إنساناً بالإمكان الذي هو أعم مِنَ الفعل . و حاصل ما تقدَّم : أنَّا إذا قلنا مثلاً : «كل كاتب ضاحك : 
فعلى قول ابن سينا معناه كل شخص متّصف بالكتابة بالفعل ماضياً، أو حالياً، أو آتياً فهو ضاحك.
وعلى قول الفارابي يكون معناه : كلُّ فرد يمكن انصافه بالكتابة ماضياً، أو حالياً، أو آتياً . - وعلى القول الثالث وهو لحفيد ابن رشد تكون جهة صدق الكاتب على الأفراد كجهة صدق الضاحك، فإن أردت أنَّه ضاحك بالفعل، فالمراد بالموضوع الكاتب بالفعل، وإن أردت أنه ضاحك بالإمكان فالمراد بالكاتب من يمكن اتصافه بالكتابة.
هذا عند الإطلاق، فإن قيد نحو : كلُّ كاتب بالفعل فهو كذا»، فلا نزاع.
قوله : (يَشْهَدُ لَهُ الِاسْتِعْمَالُ) أي : كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا [المائدة: ٣٨] ، و الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا ﴾ [النور: ۲] ؛ أي: كلُّ مَن ثبت له السَّرقة بالفعل والزنا بالفعل، وإلّا أمر بقطع يد كلّ إنسان؛ لإمكان ،سرقته، وجلد كل لإمكان زناه.
واعترض بعض المحققين ترجيحهم القول بالفعل بهذا : بأنَّ الإمكان المراد به ما هو أعمُّ مِنَ الفعل ؛ إذ المراد : الإمكان العام المعدود في مواد القضايا، وهو الإمكان فى نفس الأمر ؛ أي: كون فيصدقُ بالفعل، ومهما صدق الأخصُّ صدق الأعم، فكلام الفارابي الشَّيء غير ممتنع، صادق بالآيتين، لكنَّ الشَّارح أشار إلى ترجيح مذهب الشَّيخ الذي يفرض الاتصاف بالفعل؛ بأنَّ الاستعمال إنَّما جاء على ذلك، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار الإمكان العام.
قوله : (وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَتَارَةٌ... إلخ) أشار بهذا إلى ردّ ما قيل: إنَّ القضيَّة الخارجيَّة لا تأتي على مذهب الفارابي القائل بالإمكان في صدق العنوان؛ لأنَّهم اعتبروا في الخارجيَّة الوجود خارجاً، وذلك يستلزم الصدق بالفعل .
ووجه الرَّدُ : أَنَّ الخلاف بينهما في صدق العنوان على الأفراد ؛ سواء اعتبرنا وجودها في الخارج أو في التقدير، ولا يلزم من اعتبار وجود الأفراد خارجاً أن يصدق عليها العنوان بالفعل، كما زعمه القائل .
The truth is that the modality of its (the subject's) truth concerning its individuals when unqualified is actual, as Ibn Sina says, not possible, as al-Farabi says. (The commentator says:) Know that the description of the predicate is the very intention, and to it assent and denial are directed, and likewise other concomitants of the statement such as correspondence and its absence, the necessity of the relation, and other aspects. And the description of the subject, though it is outside the intention, they did not neglect it entirely; rather they differed concerning the modality of its truth concerning its individuals in three views: actual, possible, or it follows the description of the predicate in its actuality and possibility. (The commentator says:) By 'actual' as Ibn Sina says is meant: the attribution of the essence to the description in reality without addressing whether that attribution is necessary (i.e., by necessity) or perpetual or otherwise. So if we say, for example, 'Every white thing is a body,' the black person enters on the view of possibility because of the possibility of his being described with whiteness, but does not enter on the view of actuality because he is not described with it externally. And by 'actual' is not meant its occurrence hypothetically rather than externally, such that the black person would enter in our saying 'Every white thing is a body' if the mind supposes him white, as al-Sa'd mentioned; for the first is what the usage of the Arabs testifies to, as al-Hilali said. The later scholars followed Ibn Sina on this view, so it is the view of the majority. Ibn Sina is the excellent, virtuous, skilled master known as 'the Sheikh' in the tongues of the people when unqualified, and he is Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Abd Allah ibn Sina, ... (biography). He died in Hamadan in the year 428. (The commentator says:) By 'not possible' is meant what is opposed to impossibility, and it actually holds true; it is not meant by it what is opposed to actuality, which is potentiality. Both meanings are indicated by the inclusion of the sperm in 'human' if we say 'Every human is an animal' if possibility means potentiality, because the sperm is a human potentially; unlike when we mean by it what is more general than actuality, for the sperm is prevented from humanity, so it is not included in the subject because it is not a human by the possibility that is more general than actuality. The summary of what preceded is that if we say, for example, 'Every writer is laughing': on Ibn Sina's view it means every person described with writing actually, past, present, or future, is laughing. On al-Farabi's view it means every individual that can be described with writing past, present, or future. And on the third view, which is that of Ibn Rushd's grandson, the modality of the truth of 'writer' concerning individuals is like the modality of the truth of 'laughing'; if you mean he is laughing actually, then what is meant by the subject is the writer actually, and if you mean he is laughing possibly, then what is meant by 'writer' is the one who can be described with writing. This is when unqualified; if restricted, such as 'Every writer actually is such-and-such,' then there is no dispute. (The commentator says:) By 'usage testifies to it' is meant like His statement, the Exalted: 'The thief, male and female, cut off [their hands]' (Quran 5:38), and 'The adulteress and adulterer, flog each of them' (Quran 24:2); i.e., everyone for whom theft is established actually and adultery actually; otherwise it would command cutting off the hand of every human being because of the possibility of theft, and flogging everyone because of the possibility of adultery. Some investigators objected to their preferring the view of actuality with this, saying that possibility meant here is what is more general than actuality, since what is meant is the general possibility counted among the matters of propositions, which is possibility in reality, i.e., something not impossible; it is true actually, and whenever the more specific is true the more general is true, so al-Farabi's statement is not impossible and is true with respect to the two verses. However, the commentator indicated the preference of the Sheikh's view, which presupposes the attribute actually, because usage came accordingly, so there is no need to consider general possibility. (The commentator says:) By 'And on every...' he indicated a refutation of what was said: that the external proposition does not occur according to al-Farabi's view that holds possibility in the truth of the title, because they considered in the external proposition existence externally, and that necessitates truth actually. The refutation is that the disagreement between them is concerning the truth of the title concerning individuals, whether we consider their existence externally or hypothetically, and from considering the existence of individuals externally it does not follow that the title is true of them actually, as the claimant claimed.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the modality of the subject term's truth value when used in an unqualified proposition. The commentator explains the correct position—that the subject applies to its individuals actually (bi'l-fiʿl), as Ibn Sina held, contrary to al-Farabi's view that it applies possibly (bi'l-imkān). Three opinions are enumerated: actuality, possibility, and a third view attributed to Ibn Rushd's grandson, where the subject's modality follows that of the predicate. The commentator clarifies that 'actual' means real external attribution, not hypothetical mental attribution; thus in 'Every white thing is a body,' a black person is not included because he is not actually white. The usage of the Arabs, such as the Quranic verses on theft and adultery, supports Ibn Sina's view: the command applies only to those who actually committed the act, not to everyone who could possibly commit it. An objector argues that al-Farabi's 'possibility' is the general possibility that includes actuality, so his view is also true. The commentator responds by implying that the preference for actuality is justified by direct linguistic usage, which does not require considering general possibility. Finally, the commentator refutes the claim that al-Farabi's view cannot account for external propositions (al-qadiyya al-kharijiyya), stating that the disagreement concerns the truth of the title itself, not the mode of existence of the individuals; external existence does not entail actual attribution of the title.

Propositions
advanced
8 min
Dispute on the Mode of Subject Term Truth in Universal Propositions
Author presents the three positions and evaluates them with Quranic evidence and logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on the truth condition of the subject term in universal propositions and evaluate their support from Quranic usage and logical principles.

Core Idea
The truth of a universal's subject: actual possession, possibility, or contextual determination?

In Islamic logic, the truth of a universal proposition like 'Every writer is human' depends on how we understand the subject term 'writer'. Does it mean actually existing writers, or anyone who could possibly write? This debate among Ibn Sina, al-Farabi, and Ibn Rushd has direct implications for interpreting Quranic commands. This lesson unpacks their views and the evidence brought to support each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Find the three views: Ibn Sina requires actual possession of the attribute (past, present, or future); al-Farabi allows any individual who can possibly possess it; Ibn Rushd's grandson holds that the mode of subject truth mirrors the predicate's mode. Note the names and key phrases.
The Arabic terms: القول بالفعل (actualism), القول بالإمكان (possibilism), and the contextual view.
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The commentator uses Q.5:38 (thief) and Q.24:2 (fornicator) to support actualism. The command applies only to those who have actually committed the act, not all possible thieves. This shows Arabic usage (isti'mal) favors actuality.
The argument: if possibility were sufficient, the command would apply to everyone, which is absurd.
3
Examine the objection and refutation
An objector argues that al-Farabi's possibility view also satisfies the verses because possibility includes actuality (the narrower implies the broader). The refutation: the intended possibility is general possibility (imkan 'amm), not mere logical possibility; the verses specify actuality, so there is no need for the broader.
The commentator concludes that usage is decisive for actualism.
4
Understand the external proposition issue
Some claim that external propositions (al-qadiyya al-kharijiyya) require actual existence, contradicting al-Farabi's view. The refutation: the dispute is about the truth of the title on individuals, not about their existence. Even if individuals exist externally, the title may not actually apply.
This prevents a false dichotomy between existence and predication.

شرح البناني على السلم المرونق
إِذِ الأَوَّلُ هُوَ الَّذِي يَشْهَدُ لَهُ الاِسْتِعْمَالُ فِي لُغَةِ العَرَبِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَتَارَةٌ يُقْصَدُ بِالمَوْضُوعِ الأَفْرَادُ قصارة. -
فعلى قول ابن سينا معناه كل شخص متّصف بالكتابة بالفعل ماضياً، أو حالياً، أو آتياً فهو ضاحك. -
وعلى قول الفارابي يكون معناه : كلُّ فرد يمكن انصافه بالكتابة ماضياً، أو حالياً، أو آتياً . - وعلى القول الثالث وهو لحفيد ابن رشد تكون جهة صدق الكاتب على الأفراد كجهة صدق الضاحك، فإن أردت أنَّه ضاحك بالفعل، فالمراد بالموضوع الكاتب بالفعل، وإن أردت أنه ضاحك بالإمكان فالمراد بالكاتب من يمكن اتصافه بالكتابة.
هذا عند الإطلاق، فإن قيد نحو : كلُّ كاتب بالفعل فهو كذا»، فلا نزاع.
قوله : (يَشْهَدُ لَهُ الِاسْتِعْمَالُ) أي : كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا [المائدة: ٣٨] ، و الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا ﴾ [النور: ۲] ؛ أي: كلُّ مَن ثبت له السَّرقة بالفعل والزنا بالفعل، وإلّا أمر بقطع يد كلّ إنسان؛ لإمكان سرقته، وجلد كل لإمكان زناه.
واعترض بعض المحققين ترجيحهم القول بالفعل بهذا : بأنَّ الإمكان المراد به ما هو أعمُّ مِنَ الفعل ؛ إذ المراد : الإمكان العام المعدود في مواد القضايا، وهو الإمكان فى نفس الأمر ؛ أي: كون فيصدقُ بالفعل، ومهما صدق الأخصُّ صدق الأعم، فكلام الفارابي الشَّيء غير ممتنع، صادق بالآيتين، لكنَّ الشَّارح أشار إلى ترجيح مذهب الشَّيخ الذي يفرض الاتصاف بالفعل؛ بأنَّ الاستعمال إنَّما جاء على ذلك، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار الإمكان العام.
قوله : (وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَتَارَةٌ... إلخ) أشار بهذا إلى ردّ ما قيل: إنَّ القضيَّة الخارجيَّة لا تأتي على مذهب الفارابي القائل بالإمكان في صدق العنوان؛ لأنَّهم اعتبروا في الخارجيَّة الوجود خارجاً، وذلك يستلزم الصدق بالفعل .
ووجه الرَّدُ : أَنَّ الخلاف بينهما في صدق العنوان على الأفراد ؛ سواء اعتبرنا وجودها في الخارج أو في التقدير، ولا يلزم من اعتبار وجود الأفراد خارجاً أن يصدق عليها العنوان بالفعل، كما زعمه القائل .
The commentary of al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq: Since the first is that which usage in the Arabic language testifies to, and occasionally by 'subject' is meant individuals. So according to Ibn Sina, its meaning is: every person described with writing actually (past, present, or future) is laughing. According to al-Farabi, its meaning is: every individual that can be described with writing (past, present, or future). According to the third opinion, which is that of the grandson of Ibn Rushd, the mode of truth of 'writer' for individuals is like the mode of truth of 'laughing': if you mean that he is laughing actually, then by the subject is meant the actual writer; if you mean that he is laughing potentially, then by 'writer' is meant the one who can be described with writing. This is when unqualified. If it is qualified, like 'every actual writer is such-and-such,' there is no dispute. His saying: (usage testifies to it) i.e., like His statement, the Exalted: 'And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands' and 'the female fornicator and the male fornicator, flog them'; meaning: everyone for whom theft actually occurred and fornication actually occurred; otherwise, he would have commanded cutting off the hand of every human being due to the possibility of his theft, and flogging everyone due to the possibility of his fornication. And some scholars objected to their preferring the actual mode with this: that the possibility intended is broader than actuality; for what is intended is the general possibility counted among the matters of propositions, which is possibility in the nature of the case, i.e., it may be actually true. Whenever the narrower is true, the broader is true. So the statement of al-Farabi is something not impossible, true with the two verses. But the commentator pointed to the preference of the opinion of the Master (Ibn Sina) which assumes the actual description, because usage only comes in that way, so there is no need to consider general possibility. His saying: (And occasionally, etc.) — he indicated by this a refutation of what was said: that the external proposition does not occur according to al-Farabi's opinion that possibility is in the truth of the title, because they considered in the external proposition existence outside, and that necessitates actual truth. And the face of the refutation is that the disagreement between them is about the truth of the title upon individuals, whether we consider their existence outside or in estimation, and it does not follow from considering the existence of individuals outside that the title is true of them actually, as the claimant claimed.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the differing views on the condition under which the subject term of a universal proposition is taken to apply to individuals. The author is commenting on a matn that states 'the first is that which usage testifies to' regarding the question of whether the subject term (like 'writer' or 'thief') is taken as actually existent or merely possible. He presents three positions: Ibn Sina holds that the subject must be actually described by the attribute (actual writing), al-Farabi holds that the subject can be any individual that could possibly be so described, and the grandson of Ibn Rushd holds that it depends on the intended mode of the predicate. The commentator then explains that the Quranic verses about thieves and fornicators support the actual mode, because the command to cut off hands and flog is addressed only to those who have actually committed the act, not to all possible thieves. An objection is raised by some scholars: since possibility is broader than actuality, and whenever the narrower is true the broader is true, al-Farabi's view would also be true for those verses. The commentator refutes this by noting that the intended possibility is not mere logical possibility but general possibility (imkan 'amm) which is a modality of the matter itself, and the usage in the verses is specifically for actual cases. Then, another issue is addressed: the external proposition (al-qadiyya al-kharijiyya) might be thought incompatible with al-Farabi's view because it requires actual existence. The refutation clarifies that the dispute is about the truth of the title on individuals, not about the actual existence of the individuals themselves; even if individuals are considered externally existent, the title may not actually apply to them. For example, a person who exists but never writes is not actually a writer.

Key Terms
القول بالفعل
القول بالإمكان
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the third opinion (Ibn Rushd's grandson) hold regarding the mode of truth of the subject term?
A) The subject must actually possess the attribute.
B) The subject can be any individual that possibly possesses the attribute.
C) The mode of truth of the subject is like that of the predicate.
D) The subject is always understood in terms of general possibility.

Key Takeaway
The actualist position (Ibn Sina) is preferred because Arabic linguistic usage, as evidenced by Quranic commands, treats subject terms as referring to individuals who actually possess the attribute, not merely those who could possess it.

External and Real Propositions
Author defines external and real propositions with emphasis on attributive truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions and explain the role of attributive truth (al-sidq al-taqyidi).

Core Idea
External propositions restrict to actual existents; real propositions include mentally estimated ones.

This passage introduces two crucial types of categorical propositions in Islamic logic. Understanding their difference—especially the concept of attributive truth—is essential for correctly interpreting universal statements in theology and jurisprudence. The author clarifies common misconceptions about subject existence in negative propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author defines the external proposition (الموجودة في أحد الأزمنة) and the real proposition (ما يشملها ويشمل المقدرة في الذهن).
The external proposition restricts its subject to individuals that actually exist in the past, present, or future. The real proposition includes both actual and hypothetical individuals.
2
Understand attributive truth (الصدق التقييدي)
Note that the truth of an external proposition is attributive, not judgmental. This means the subject is restricted to existing individuals, but the proposition does not assert their existence. Consider the example: 'Every whiteness is a color' does not require an actual white object.
This distinction prevents the incorrect implication that negative propositions require the existence of the subject.
3
Compare the scope
External propositions cover individuals that exist in one of the three times. Real propositions also cover individuals that could exist in the mind.
The scope difference is critical when evaluating theological claims about believers or prophets.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Every believer is in paradise'. If understood externally, it applies only to believers who exist or will exist. If understood as real, it applies even to hypothetical believers in God's knowledge.
This affects discussions on predestination and the status of non-existent believers.

قوله : (سِتَ عَشْرَةَ نِسْبَةٌ) حاصلها : أنَّ المحصورات أربع كما ذكر الشارح، وكلٌّ من هذه الأربع تنظرُ مع غيرها، فتلك ستّ عشرة من ضرب أربع في أربع، فإنِ اتَّفق كمها وكيفها بأن كانتا معاً كلِّيَّتين موجبتين أو سالبتين، أو جزئيتين موجبتين أو سالبتين؛ ففيه أربع صورٍ، وإن اختلفا في الكم والكيف، أو في أحدهما، كانت اثنتي عشرة صورة، وقد وضع اليوسي رحمه الله تعالى جدولاً يضبط هذه النسب؛ هذه صورته :
(۱) في (أ) : «بِهَا» بدلاً من «به» .
Chapter on Propositions and Their Judgments. (The subject) existing in one of the times, and the proposition is then called 'external'. And sometimes is meant by it what includes them and includes that which is mentally estimated, and the proposition is then called 'real'. Each of them has four divisions: affirmative, negative, universal, particular. And thereby the relations between them are sixteen relations; see them in al-Mukhtasar. His saying: (existing in one of the times) i.e., like our saying: every believer is eternally in paradise; i.e., everyone who has existed among believers or will exist is in paradise. His saying: (and the proposition is then called 'external') i.e., because the individuals judged about in it are external. That is when you say 'every writer is a human', then the meaning is: every individual of whom 'writer' is true actually in external reality, whether he existed now, before, or will exist. And what we mean is the attributive (taqyidi) truth, not the judgmental (hukmi) truth; i.e., restricting the subject to being existent, not judging that it exists. So if we say 'every whiteness is a color', its meaning is: every individual of whom the name 'whiteness' is true in external reality, i.e., being described in external reality by whiteness, it is judged to have color-ness. And that does not require that there is in external reality something for which whiteness is actually proven; otherwise, the negative would necessitate the existence of the subject, and so what is established that it does not necessitate the existence of the subject would not be valid. His saying: (what includes them and includes the mentally estimated) — for example, when you intend by your saying 'every believer is in paradise': everyone who, if his existence were assumed and he were a believer, it occurs in the knowledge of Allah and His will that he exists or not, he is eternally in paradise. His saying: (sixteen relations) — their summary: that the categorical propositions are four, as the commentator mentioned, and each of these four is considered with the others, so that is sixteen from multiplying four by four. If they agree in quantity and quality, i.e., both are universal affirmative or both negative, or both particular affirmative or both negative, then there are four images. And if they differ in quantity and quality, or in one of them, then there are twelve images. And al-Yusi, may Allah have mercy on him, has placed a table that regulates these relations; this is its form. (1) In manuscript (A): 'بِهَا' instead of 'به'.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage defines two important types of categorical propositions: external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya). An external proposition is one whose subject is taken to refer to individuals that actually exist in external reality in one of the three times (past, present, or future). For example, 'Every believer is in paradise' means: every believer who existed or will exist is in paradise. The author clarifies that the truth condition here is 'attributive truth' (sidq taqyidi) – the subject is restricted to actually existing individuals – not 'judgmental truth' (sidq hukmi) which would involve a judgment that the subject exists. This distinction is important to avoid the incorrect conclusion that negative propositions imply the existence of the subject. For instance, 'Every whiteness is a color' does not require that there be an actual white object; it simply means that any actual white thing is a color. A real proposition, on the other hand, includes both actual individuals and mentally estimated (muqaddara) individuals. The example given is 'Every believer is in paradise' when taken to mean: anyone who, if they were to exist and be a believer, would be in paradise according to God's knowledge and will. This is a broader scope. Both external and real propositions have four divisions: affirmative, negative, universal, particular. Therefore, when comparing any two propositions, the possible relations are sixteen (4 × 4). The commentary explains that if both propositions agree in quantity and quality, there are four cases; if they differ in one or both, there are twelve. A reference is made to a table by al-Yusi.

القضية الحقيقية
الصدق التقييدي

The Sixteen Logical Relations
Author explains the combinatorial basis for sixteen relations between categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the sixteen relations between categorical propositions based on their quantity and quality and identify the four matching and twelve differing pairs.

Core Idea
Four proposition types yield sixteen relation pairs: four when matching, twelve when differing.

After defining the two types of propositions, the author explains that each type has four divisions (affirmative, negative, universal, particular). This creates a combinatorial framework where any two propositions can be compared, resulting in sixteen possible relations. These relations are the foundation for understanding immediate inference and the square of opposition.

Guided Path
1
List the four proposition types
The four categorical proposition types are: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). These are the 'four' mentioned in the passage (المحصورات أربع).
These four are standard in Aristotelian logic and are used in Islamic logic as well.
2
Set up all pairs
Each proposition can be paired with itself and with each other, yielding 4 × 4 = 16 pairs. The passage says: 'Each of these four is considered with the others, so that is sixteen from multiplying four by four.'
These include pairs like (A,A), (A,E), (A,I), (A,O), etc.
3
Identify matching pairs (اتفق كمها وكيفها)
If two propositions agree in both quantity and quality (both A, both E, both I, both O), there are 4 such pairs. The passage says 'fihi arba' suwar' (there are four images).
These pairs are either identical or equivalent in truth conditions.
4
Identify differing pairs (اختلفا في الكم والكيف أو في أحدهما)
If they differ in quantity, quality, or both, there are 12 pairs. The author mentions al-Yusi's table that organizes these relations, likely showing opposition relations like contrariety, subcontrariety, subalternation, etc.
Understanding these differing pairs is essential for inferring one proposition from another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Propositions
beginner

Kharijiyya and Haqiqiyya: Two Types of Categorical Propositions
Author defines external and real propositions and their four subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external and real propositions and classify any categorical proposition as one of the four subtypes for each type.

Core Idea
External propositions judge actual existents; real propositions include hypotheticals.

In Islamic logic, propositions are divided not only by quantity and quality but also by the scope of their subject. An external proposition (kharijiyya) judges only individuals that actually exist in time, while a real proposition (haqiqiyya) judges both actual and mentally estimated hypothetical individuals. This distinction is crucial for understanding how universal and particular claims apply in different contexts, such as in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of an external proposition
Look at the phrase 'the one that exists in one of the times'. Note that the subject is restricted to individuals that have actual existence in the past, present, or future. For example, 'Every believer is in Paradise' means every believer who actually exists or will exist.
2
Identify the defining feature of a real proposition
The real proposition includes both existing individuals and those that are mentally estimated, even if they never exist. For example, 'Every believer is in Paradise' can also mean any person who would be a believer if they existed, as in God's knowledge.
This is indicated by 'what includes them and includes the mentally estimated'.
3
Classify the four subtypes for each type
Each type is divided into four: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Write them out as a 2x2 grid for both external and real.
These are the 'four divisions' mentioned in the text.
4
Understand the restrictive truth condition
In an external proposition, the subject is restricted to actual existence, but this does not imply a separate judgment of existence. The truth condition is restrictive (taqyidi) not judgmental (hukmi). For example, 'Every whiteness is a color' does not require that whiteness actually exist; it only restricts the subject to those individuals that happen to be white in reality.
This nuance is explained in the commentary to avoid the fallacy that negative propositions would require subject existence.
5
Test your understanding with examples
Given a proposition like 'Every human is animal', state whether it is external or real (it can be both: external if considering actual humans, real if including possible humans). Then determine its quantity and quality.
The text uses this example later for the relations table.

Source Text

باب في القضايا وأحكامها
المَوْجُودَةُ فِي أَحَدِ الأَزْمِنَةِ، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : «خَارِجِيَّةٌ»، وَتَارَةً يُقْصَدُ بِهِ مَا يَشْمَلُهَا وَيَشْمَلُ المُقَدَّرَةَ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : (حَقِيقِيَّةٌ»، وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَام : مُوجَبَةٍ، وَسَالِبَةٍ ، كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَجُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَبِذَلِكَ تَكُونُ النِّسَبُ بَيْنَهَا سِتَ عَشْرَةَ نِسْبَةً، انْظُرْهَا فِي المُخْتَصَرِ».
قصارة .
قوله : (المَوْجُودَةُ فِي أَحَدِ الأَزْمِنَةِ) أي : كقولنا : كلُّ مؤمن فهو مخلد في الجنَّة ؛ أي: كلُّ مَن وجد مِنَ المؤمنين أو يوجد فهو في الجنَّة.
قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : خَارِجِيَّةٌ) أي : لأنَّ الأفراد المحكوم عليها فيها خارجيَّة، وذلك أنَّك إذا قلت كل كاتب إنسان»، فالمعنى : كلُّ فردٍ صدق عليه الكاتب بالفعل في الخارج؛ بأن وجد الآن أو قبل أو يوجد.
:
ومرادنا : الصدق التَّقييديُّ، لا الحكميُّ ؛ أي: تقييد المحكوم عليه بأنَّه موجود، لا الحكم بأن موجود، فإذا قلنا : كلُّ بياض لون» يكون معناه : كلُّ فردٍ يصدقُ عليه اسم «البياض» في الخارج: بأن يكون موصوفاً في الخارج بالبياضية، فهو محكوم عليه باللونيّة، ولا يقتضي ذلك : أن يكون ثمَّ في الخارج أثبتت له البياضيَّة بالفعل، وإلا كانتِ السَّالبة مقتضيَّةٌ لوجود الموضوع، فلا يصح ما تقرّر من أنَّها لا تقتضي وجود الموضوع. قوله : (مَا يَشْمَلُهَا وَيَشْمَلُ المُقَدَّرَةَ فِي الذِّهْنِ مثلاً كأن تريد بقولك: كلُّ مؤمن في الجنة» : كلُّ مَن لو قدر ،وجوده، فكان مؤمناً جرى في علم الله تعالى وإرادته أن يوجد أو لا فهو مخلد في الجنة.
مخلد
يوجد
قوله : (سِتَ عَشْرَةَ نِسْبَةٌ) حاصلها : أنَّ المحصورات أربع كما ذكر الشارح، وكلٌّ من هذه الأربع تنظرُ مع غيرها، فتلك ستّ عشرة من ضرب أربع في أربع، فإنِ اتَّفق كمها وكيفها بأن كانتا معاً كلِّيَّتين موجبتين أو سالبتين، أو جزئيتين موجبتين أو سالبتين؛ ففيه أربع صورٍ، وإن اختلفا في الكم والكيف، أو في أحدهما، كانت اثنتي عشرة صورة، وقد وضع اليوسي رحمه الله تعالى جدولاً يضبط هذه النسب؛ هذه صورته :
