الحقيقية أعم
ووجه العمل به أن تأخذ قضيَّة مَّا مِن القضايا المكتوبة في طول الجدول وهي الخارجيَّات لأربع، مع قضيَّةٍ مَّا مِنَ القضايا المكتوبة في عرضه وهي الحقيقيَّاتُ الأربع، وتنظر بيت التقاطع بينهما، فتجد فيه المناسبة.
وهي
مثلاً : إذا أخذت أولى الطُّوليَّات وهي الكلية الموجبة الخارجية، مع أولى العرضيات الكلية الموجبة الحقيقيَّة، ونظرت بيت التَّقاطع وجدت فيه: «العموم من وجه»، وهو النسبة بينهما ، . وهكذا في سائرها، وأمثلة ذلك وتوجيهه ينظر في حاشية اليوسي»، لكنّي أذكر لك مثالاً واحداً لعله ينشرح صدرك لنظر ما بقي : فإذا كانتا معاً موجبتين فبينهما عموم من وجه: - يصدقان معاً في نحو : كلُّ إنسان حيوان». 
· : - وتنفرد الحقيقية في نحو كل عنقاء طائر من كلِّ ممكن معدوم وقع فيه الحكم باعتبار فرض الوجود، لا باعتبار الوجود.
وتنفرد الخارجية حيث يكون المحمول ثابتاً لجميع الأفراد الخارجية دون المقدرة؛ كما لو فرضت أنَّه لم يوجد ولا يوجد في الخارج مِن أنواع اللون إلَّا السَّواد، فيصدقُ بالاعتبار الخارجي : كلُّ لون سواد» لا بالاعتبار الحقيقي، والحق الجزئية .
Section on propositions and their rulings. The one that exists in one of the times, and the proposition is then called 'external'; and sometimes by it is meant what includes it and includes what is mentally estimated, and the proposition is then called 'real'. Each of them has four divisions: affirmative, negative, universal, particular. By this, the relations between them become sixteen relations; see them in the Mukhtasar. Commentary: His statement: 'the one that exists in one of the times' means like our saying: 'Every believer is eternally in Paradise'; i.e., everyone who among the believers has existed or will exist is in Paradise. His statement: 'and the proposition is then called external' means because the individuals judged in it are external. That is because when you say 'Every writer is a human', the meaning is: every individual of whom 'writer' is actually true externally, whether now, before, or in the future. And our intent is the restrictive truth, not the judgmental truth; i.e., restricting the subject that it exists, not judging that it exists. So if we say 'Every whiteness is a color', its meaning is: every individual of whom the name 'whiteness' is true externally, i.e., being described externally with whiteness, is judged to have color, and that does not require that there exists externally an actual positing of whiteness for it; otherwise the negative would require the existence of the subject, and it would not be valid what has been established that it does not require the existence of the subject. His statement: 'what includes them and includes the mentally estimated', for example you mean by your saying 'Every believer is in Paradise': everyone who, if his existence were supposed and he were a believer, would occur in God's knowledge and will whether he exists or not, he is eternally in Paradise. His statement: 'sixteen relations': their summary is that the four categoricals as the commentator mentioned, and each of these four is considered with the others, so there are sixteen by multiplying four by four. If they agree in quantity and quality, i.e., both are universal affirmative or negative, or both are particular affirmative or negative, then there are four forms. If they differ in quantity and quality, or in one of them, there are twelve forms. And al-Yusi (may God have mercy on him) made a table to regulate these relations; its form is: [table]. And the way to use it is that you take some proposition from the propositions written in the length of the table — which are the four external ones — with some proposition from the propositions written in its width — which are the four real ones — and you look at the intersection box, and you find the relation there. For example: if you take the first of the vertical ones, which is the universal affirmative external, with the first of the horizontal ones, the universal affirmative real, and you look at the intersection box, you find there: 'general in one way', which is the relation between them. And so on for the rest. The examples of that and their explanation are seen in the Hashiya of al-Yusi, but I will mention one example for you, perhaps your heart will be opened to consider what remains: if both are affirmative, then between them is generality in one way — they are both true in something like 'Every human is an animal'. And the real proposition is unique in something like 'Every phoenix is a bird' for every possible non-existent thing where the judgment occurs by considering hypothetical existence, not considering existence. And the external proposition is unique where the predicate is affirmed for all external individuals without the hypothetical ones; as if you suppose that there has never existed nor will exist in the external world of types of color except black, then by the external consideration it is true: 'Every color is black', not by the real consideration — and the truth is particularity.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions: external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya). It then classifies each into four kinds (affirmative/negative, universal/particular), leading to sixteen possible relations between them. The author explains that an external proposition judges actually existing individuals in time, while a real proposition includes both existing and mentally hypothetical individuals. The commentator clarifies that in the external proposition, the truth is restrictive, not judgmental — i.e., it restricts the subject to actual existence, not that we judge it to exist. For example, 'Every whiteness is a color' means every individual actually described as whiteness in the external world, without requiring an actual existence of whiteness. Otherwise, negations would falsely imply existence of the subject. The real proposition, on the other hand, includes hypothetical cases like 'Every believer is in Paradise' meaning even those who would exist if they were believers in God's knowledge. The sixteen relations arise from pairing each of the four kinds (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) from the external set with each from the real set. If they agree in quantity and quality, there are four forms of agreement; if they differ, there are twelve forms. The commentator presents a table by al-Yusi showing the relations. For example, a universal affirmative external and a universal affirmative real have 'generality in one way' (mutually overlapping). An example: Both are true in 'Every human is an animal'. But the real proposition alone applies to hypotheticals like 'Every phoenix is a bird' (if a phoenix existed), while the external proposition alone applies if the predicate is true only for actual individuals, e.g., if only black exists in reality, then 'Every color is black' is true externally but not really. The passage concludes that the correct view is particularity in such cases.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes an external proposition?
A) A proposition that judges only mentally estimated individuals.
B) A proposition that judges only individuals that actually exist in time.
C) A proposition that judges both actual and hypothetical individuals.
D) A proposition that does not require the existence of the subject.

Key Takeaway
External and real propositions differ in whether the subject is restricted to actual existents or includes hypotheticals, and this distinction affects how we interpret universal and particular claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following propositions as external or real, and then as universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative: (a) 'Every believer is in Paradise' (interpreted as actual believers). (b) 'Some writer is human' (interpreted as actual writers). (c) 'Every possible intelligent being is rational'. (d) 'No non-existent thing is colored' (considering hypothetical non-existents).
?
Explain why the proposition 'Every whiteness is a color' can be external without implying whiteness actually exists, using the restrictive truth condition.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Sixteen Relations Between External and Real Propositions
Author enumerates the sixteen relations and presents al-Yusi's table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use al-Yusi's table to find the logical relation between any external proposition and its corresponding real proposition when both have the same quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Sixteen relations emerge from pairing four external with four real proposition types.

After learning about external and real propositions, we now explore the sixteen possible logical relations between them. These relations are catalogued in a table by al-Yusi, which we will learn to navigate. Understanding these relations is essential for comparing propositions in theological and legal arguments, where one may need to know whether two propositions are equivalent, contrary, or overlapping.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight proposition types
There are four external types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and four real types (same). Write them as a 2x2 matrix for each.
These are the 'four divisions' from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the table layout
Al-Yusi's table has external propositions as rows (vertical) and real propositions as columns (horizontal). The entries are the relations. For example, the top-left cell (universal affirmative external vs universal affirmative real) gives 'عموم من وجه' (generality in one way).
The table is presented in the commentary.
3
Identify the relation by matching quantity and quality
Take an external proposition and a real proposition with specific quantity and quality. Find the row for the external, the column for the real, and read the intersection.
If they agree in both quantity and quality (both universal affirmative, etc.), there are only four possible relations: عموم من وجه (overlap), مباينة (disjoint), etc. If they differ, there are twelve other relations.
4
Analyze an example: both universal affirmative
The commentary gives the example 'Every human is animal' where both propositions are universal affirmative. The relation is 'عموم من وجه' because they both apply to actual humans (external) and all possible humans (real) — they overlap. But they are not identical because external can be true while real false when the predicate is not true for hypotheticals.
Another example: if the external universal affirmative is true but the real is false, the relation would be that external is more general? Actually, the table says for both universal affirmative: عموم من وجه, meaning each can be true where the other is false. So they overlap but not one is subset.
5
Practice with the table
Take the external universal negative and the real particular affirmative. Find the intersection in the table. The commentary does not list all, but the table is given. Locate the corresponding cell and interpret the relation.
The table is printed in the commentary. Refer to it.

Section on propositions and their rulings. The one that exists in one of the times, and the proposition is then called 'external'; and sometimes by it is meant what includes it and includes what is mentally estimated, and the proposition is then called 'real'. Each of them has four divisions: affirmative, negative, universal, particular. By this, the relations between them become sixteen relations; see them in the Mukhtasar. Commentary: His statement: 'the one that exists in one of the times' means like our saying: 'Every believer is eternally in Paradise'; i.e., everyone who among the believers has existed or will exist is in Paradise. His statement: 'and the proposition is then called external' means because the individuals judged in it are external. That is because when you say 'Every writer is a human', the meaning is: every individual of whom 'writer' is actually true externally, whether now, before, or in the future. And our intent is the restrictive truth, not the judgmental truth; i.e., restricting the subject that it exists, not judging that it exists. So if we say 'Every whiteness is a color', its meaning is: every individual of whom the name 'whiteness' is true externally, i.e., being described externally with whiteness, is judged to have color, and that does not require that there exists externally an actual positing of whiteness for it; otherwise the negative would require the existence of the subject, and it would not be valid what has been established that it does not require the existence of the subject. His statement: 'what includes them and includes the mentally estimated', for example you mean by your saying 'Every believer is in Paradise': everyone who, if his existence were supposed and he were a believer, would occur in God's knowledge and will whether he exists or not, he is eternally in Paradise. His statement: 'sixteen relations': their summary is that the four categoricals as the commentator mentioned, and each of these four is considered with the others, so there are sixteen by multiplying four by four. If they agree in quantity and quality, i.e., both are universal affirmative or negative, or both are particular affirmative or negative, then there are four forms. If they differ in quantity and quality, or in one of them, there are twelve forms. And al-Yusi (may God have mercy on him) made a table to regulate these relations; its form is: [table]. And the way to use it is that you take some proposition from the propositions written in the length of the table — which are the four external ones — with some proposition from the propositions written in its width — which are the four real ones — and you look at the intersection box, and you find the relation there. For example: if you take the first of the vertical ones, which is the universal affirmative external, with the first of the horizontal ones, the universal affirmative real, and you look at the intersection box, you find there: 'general in one way', which is the relation between them. And so on for the rest. The examples of that and their explanation are seen in the Hashiya of al-Yusi, but I will mention one example for you, perhaps your heart will be opened to consider what remains: if both are affirmative, then between them is generality in one way — they are both true in something like 'Every human is an animal'. And the real proposition is unique in something like 'Every phoenix is a bird' for every possible non-existent thing where the judgment occurs by considering hypothetical existence, not considering existence. And the external proposition is unique where the predicate is affirmed for all external individuals without the hypothetical ones; as if you suppose that there has never existed nor will exist in the external world of types of color except black, then by the external consideration it is true: 'Every color is black', not by the real consideration — and the truth is particularity.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions: external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya). It then classifies each into four kinds (affirmative/negative, universal/particular), leading to sixteen possible relations between them. The author explains that an external proposition judges actually existing individuals in time, while a real proposition includes both existing and mentally hypothetical individuals. The commentator clarifies that in the external proposition, the truth is restrictive, not judgmental — i.e., it restricts the subject to actual existence, not that we judge it to exist. For example, 'Every whiteness is a color' means every individual actually described as whiteness in the external world, without requiring an actual existence of whiteness. Otherwise, negations would falsely imply existence of the subject. The real proposition, on the other hand, includes hypothetical cases like 'Every believer is in Paradise' meaning even those who would exist if they were believers in God's knowledge. The sixteen relations arise from pairing each of the four kinds (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) from the external set with each from the real set. If they agree in quantity and quality, there are four forms of agreement; if they differ, there are twelve forms. The commentator presents a table by al-Yusi showing the relations. For example, a universal affirmative external and a universal affirmative real have 'generality in one way' (mutually overlapping). An example: Both are true in 'Every human is an animal'. But the real proposition alone applies to hypotheticals like 'Every phoenix is a bird' (if a phoenix existed), while the external proposition alone applies if the predicate is true only for actual individuals, e.g., if only black exists in reality, then 'Every color is black' is true externally but not really. The passage concludes that the correct view is particularity in such cases.

Understanding External and Real Propositions and Their Relations
Table and explanation of four types of external and real propositions with their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is external or real and determine the logical relation between an external and a real proposition using the provided table.

Core Idea
External propositions consider only actual existence; real propositions consider all possible individuals.

In this passage, the commentator draws on al-Sanusi to explain why the copula and modality were omitted in the Isaghuji and then presents a table that maps the logical relations between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions. Understanding this table is crucial for evaluating statements in Islamic logic, because it tells you under what conditions two propositions can be true together, one more general, or mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify external vs. real propositions
An external proposition (kharijiyya) judges only actually existing individuals. A real proposition (haqiqiyya) judges all possible individuals, including hypothetical non-existent ones. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true both externally (since all existing humans are animals) and really (even if hypothetically more humans existed, they would be animals).
2
Read the table structure
The table has four rows (vertical axis) for external propositions (universal affirmative, particular negative, universal negative, particular affirmative) and four columns (horizontal axis) for real propositions (same four forms). Each cell shows the relation between the corresponding external and real proposition.
The cell at the intersection gives the relation: 'umum min wajh' (common from one aspect), 'mubayana' (mutual exclusion), 'al-haqiqiyya a'am' (real is more general), or 'al-haqiqiyya akhass' (real is less general).
3
Apply the table to a concrete pair
Take the first row (universal affirmative external) and first column (universal affirmative real). The cell says 'umum min wajh'. This means they sometimes agree and sometimes disagree. Example: 'Every human is an animal' is true both externally and really, so they agree. But 'Every phoenix is a bird' is true really (assuming existence) but false externally (since no phoenix exists), so they disagree.
The commentator gives a second example: if only black exists externally, 'Every color is black' is true externally but false really, because real propositions consider hypothetical colors.
4
Generalize to all four forms
Repeat for each combination. For instance, universal affirmative external with particular negative real yields 'al-haqiqiyya a'am' (real is more general). The pattern follows the table. The key is to understand the distinction: external depends on actual existence, real depends on nature regardless of existence.

Second: Shaykh al-Sanusi says in his commentary on Isaghuji: Perhaps the author only omitted explaining the copula because it is not necessary to mention it in all languages; only the Persians are obliged to mention it. For this reason also he omitted the explanation of modality (jiha), which is the word indicating the quality of the relation of the proposition. The table: [External/Real, universal affirmative/particular negative, etc.] The way to work with it: you take a certain proposition from those written in the length of the table, which are the four external ones, with a certain proposition from those written in its width, which are the four real ones, and look at the intersection cell, and you find the relation there. For example: if you take the first of the vertical ones, which is the universal affirmative external, with the first of the horizontal ones, the universal affirmative real, and look at the intersection, you find 'common from one aspect' (al-umum min wajh), which is the relation between them. And so on for the rest. Examples and their explanation are seen in the marginalia of al-Yusi, but I mention one example for you: if both are affirmative, there is common from one aspect: they are both true in something like 'Every human is an animal'. The real proposition is uniquely true in something like 'Every phoenix is a bird' – from every possible non-existent about which the judgment is made by assuming existence, not by actual existence. The external proposition is uniquely true where the predicate is established for all external individuals without the hypothetical, as if you suppose that there is no color in the external world except black, so it is true by external consideration 'Every color is black', not by real consideration, and the particular is true.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions into external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) types, and it provides a table to show the logical relations between them. The commentator begins by citing al-Sanusi, who suggests that the author of Isaghuji omitted the copula and modality from his work because not all languages require them; for instance, Persian necessitates the copula. This leads to the table, which compares the four categorical forms (universal affirmative, particular negative, etc.) across external and real propositions. The table shows that depending on the combination of quantity and quality, the relation between an external and a real proposition is one of: common from one aspect (عموم من وجه), mutual exclusion (مباينة), or the real being more general/less general. The commentator then explains how to use the table: take one proposition from the vertical axis (external) and one from the horizontal axis (real), and the intersection cell gives the relation. He provides an example: a universal affirmative external proposition combined with a universal affirmative real proposition yields 'common from one aspect' because they are both true in cases like 'Every human is an animal', but the external can be false in hypothetical cases while the real can be true for non-existent subjects. He also gives an example where the external is uniquely true: if only black exists externally, then 'Every color is black' is true externally but not really (since real considers potential colors). In this context, 'common from one aspect' means they agree in some cases and disagree in others. The classification is important for evaluating propositions in reasoning, especially in demonstrations and dialectic.

The Four Modal Qualities of Propositions
Defines the four modal qualities of propositions and explains their enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition according to its modal quality: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, or absoluteness.

Core Idea
Every proposition has one of four modal qualities; understanding them is key to evaluating arguments in kalam.

In this lesson, we explore the four modal qualities that every proposition possesses in reality, independent of verbal expression. The passage explains why these four—necessity (darura), possibility (imkan), perpetuity (dawam), and absoluteness (itlaq)—are exhaustive. This classification is foundational for modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand necessity (darura)
Necessity means the opposite is inconceivable by reason. For example, 'Every human is mortal' is necessary in reality.
It is the strongest modal quality.
2
Understand possibility (imkan)
Possibility means the opposite is conceivable by reason. For example, 'Every body is moving' is possible, not necessary.
Here we consider absolute possibility (imkan 'amm), the opposite of necessity.
3
Understand perpetuity (dawam) and absoluteness (itlaq)
Perpetuity means the proposition holds at all times, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'. Absoluteness means it does not hold at all times, e.g., 'Some humans are writing'.
These two are opposites.
4
Apply the classification
Given a proposition, determine its modal quality. For instance, 'The world is temporally originated' is possible (since its opposite is conceivable).
Practice with Islamic examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب في القضايا وأحكامها
الَّتِي هِيَ الضَّرُورَةُ، وَمُقَابِلُهَا الإِمْكَانُ أَوِ الدَّوَامُ، وَمُقَابِلُهُ الإِطْلَاقُ، فَإِنَّ هَذِهِ الكَيْفِيَّةَ لَا بُدَّ مِنْهَا لِكُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ ، وَتُسَمَّى بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى مَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ : مَادَّةٌ»، فَإِنْ صَرَّحَ بِاللَّفْظِ الدَّالّ عَلَيْهَا ؛ كَأَنْ يُقَالَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ سُمِّيَ ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُنَا : (بِالضَّرُورَةِ»: «جِهَةٌ»، وَسُمِّيَتْ القَضِيّةُ إِذْ ذَاكَ : «مُوَجَّهَةٌ» . اهـ .
قصارة.
قوله : (الَّتِي هِيَ الضَّرُورَةُ، وَمُقَابِلُهَا ... إلخ) إِنَّما انحصرت أنواع الكيفية في الأمور الأربعة؛ لأنَّ الكيفيَّة : إمَّا ضرورة، أو لا ضرورة.
ولا ضرورة هي الإمكان العام؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة هي: ما لا يجوز العقل خلافه، ومقابله : ما يجوز العقل خلافه وهو الإمكان، فتقابلا تقابل المساوي للنقيض، فصح الانحصار بعدم وجود ثالث، وأما في الأخيرين فلأنَّ الدَّوام يقابله: «لا دوام»، وهو إطلاق.
قوله : (لِكُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ مرادُهُ : النّسبة الحكميَّة التي هي موردُ الإيجاب والسَّلب، وهي ثبوتُ الشَّي للشَّيء أو انتفاؤه عنه ، لا الماديَّة، ولا الحكم لِمَا تقدَّم أنَّ النِّسبة تُطلقُ بالاشتراك على ما ذكر . ومعنى كلامه : أنَّ هذه النِّسبة لا بدَّ لها من كيفية تتكيَّف بها في نفس الأمر؛ أي: صفة تتّصفُ بها بحسب الواقع، وقد أشار لهذا المعنى شيخنا سيدي حمدون، فقال:
لَا بُدَّ لِلنِّسْبَةِ مِنْ ضَرُورَهُ نَظَرِ تَكُونُ أَوْ ضَرُورَة أَوْ ضِدُّهَا الإِمْكَانُ، ثُمَّ الإطلاق أَوْ ضِدُّهُ الدَّوَامُ ذَاتُ إطلاق تُدْعَى لَدَى اصْطِلاحِهِمْ: مُوَجَّهَه إِنْ كَلِمَةٌ كَانَتْ لَهَا مُوَجَّهَه أي: مدلولاً بها عليها.
قوله : «مَادَّةٌ») أي: مادَّة القضيَّة، كما تسمى أيضاً: عنصرها». قوله : (وَسُمِّيَتْ القَضِيّةُ إِذْ ذَاكَ : «مُوَجَّهَةٌ» كما أنَّها تسمَّى عند تركه منها : «معراة»، وإن كانت ( في نفس الأمر لا تخرج عن معنى الجهة وهي حينئذٍ محتملة لجميع الجهات؛ إلَّا أنَّ الجهة التي جعلها الحاكم للنّسبة دالة على المادة تارةً تكون موافقة للمادة في نفس الأمر، فتكون القضيَّة صادقة؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة»، و : كلُّ جرم متحرك بالإمكان الخاص، وتارة تكون مخالفة لها، فتكون القضيَّة كاذبة؛ كما لو جعلت الضَّرورة جهة للمثال الثاني والإمكان الخاص جهة للأول.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. Which are necessity (darura), and its opposite is possibility (imkan) or perpetuity (dawam), and its opposite is absoluteness (itlaq). For this quality (kayfiyya) is necessary for every relation in reality (fi nafs al-amr), and it is called, with respect to what is in reality, 'matter' (madda). If one uses a word indicating it, such as saying 'Every human is an animal by necessity', then that word indicating it is called 'direction' (jiha), and the proposition is then called 'modal' (muwajjaha). [End of quote.] Commentary: His saying 'which are necessity...' The types of quality are limited to four because quality is either necessity or not necessity. Not necessity is absolute possibility, because necessity is what reason cannot conceive otherwise, and its opposite is what reason can conceive otherwise, which is possibility. So they are opposites like contradictories, and the enumeration is complete because there is no third. As for the latter two, because perpetuity is opposed by 'non-perpetuity', which is absoluteness. His saying 'for every relation' means the relational judgment which is the subject of affirmation and negation, i.e., the existence of a thing for a thing or its non-existence, not the material relation nor the judgment, as previously stated that 'relation' is used homonymously for what was mentioned. The meaning of his statement: this relation necessarily has a quality by which it is qualified in reality, i.e., a property it possesses according to reality. Our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun pointed to this meaning, saying: The relation must have necessity of consideration, either necessity or its opposite possibility, then absoluteness or its opposite perpetuity; it is called in their terminology 'modal' if there is a word indicating it. His saying 'matter' means the matter of the proposition, also called its 'element'. His saying 'and the proposition is then called modal' just as it is called 'bare' (mu'arra) when the word is omitted, but in reality it does not depart from the meaning of direction; it is then potential for all directions. However, the direction that the judge assigns to the relation as indicative of the matter sometimes agrees with the matter in reality, so the proposition is true, like 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'Every body is possibly moving with special possibility', and sometimes it disagrees with it, so the proposition is false, as if one made necessity the direction for the second example and special possibility the direction for the first.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the four modal qualities of propositions: necessity (darura), possibility (imkan), perpetuity (dawam), and absoluteness (itlaq). The commentator begins by stating that every judgment (the relational aspect of a proposition) must have a quality in reality (fi nafs al-amr). This quality, when considered in itself, is called the 'matter' (madda) of the proposition. When it is explicitly stated by a word like 'by necessity', that word is called the 'direction' (jiha), and the proposition becomes 'modal' (muwajjaha). If no modal term is stated, it is called 'bare' (mu'arra). The commentator then explains why these four are exhaustive: any modal quality is either necessity or not necessity; not necessity is absolute possibility (imkan 'amm). This leads to two pairs: necessity vs. possibility, and perpetuity vs. absoluteness. He clarifies that the relation under discussion is the predicative relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) between subject and predicate, not the material relation or the judgment itself. The necessity of a modal quality for every proposition is emphasized: every relation in reality has a certain modal character. The commentator quotes a poem from his teacher Hamdun summarizing the types. Finally, he explains that the stated direction may or may not match the actual matter, giving examples: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is true because the matter is necessary, but if one says 'Every body is necessarily moving' (where the matter is only possible), the proposition is false. This shows the importance of matching the jiha to the madda for truth.

Matter (Madda) and Direction (Jiha) in Modal Propositions
Explains the concepts of madda and jiha and truth conditions for modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the actual modal quality (madda) from the verbal marker (jiha) in a proposition and determine truth based on their agreement.

Core Idea
Madda is the real modal character; jiha is just a word that may or may not match reality.

This lesson focuses on the crucial distinction between the intrinsic modal quality of a proposition (madda) and the explicit word indicating it (jiha). The passage explains that a proposition is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) when a jiha is present, and 'bare' (mu'arra) when absent. Truth depends on whether the stated jiha corresponds to the actual madda.

Guided Path
1
Understand madda (matter)
The madda is the actual modal quality of the relation in reality, independent of words. It is either necessity, possibility, perpetuity, or absoluteness.
It is the real modal status, also called 'element' of the proposition.
2
Understand jiha (direction)
The jiha is the word like 'by necessity' that expresses the modal quality. If present, the proposition is muwajjaha; if absent, mu'arra.
The jiha can be absent even though the madda exists.
3
Check agreement for truth
A proposition is true if the jiha agrees with the madda. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is true because the madda is necessity. But 'Every body is necessarily moving' is false because the madda is possibility.
The examples in the passage illustrate this clearly.

Source Text

ب في القضايا وأحكامها
الَّتِي هِيَ الضَّرُورَةُ، وَمُقَابِلُهَا الإِمْكَانُ أَوِ الدَّوَامُ، وَمُقَابِلُهُ الإِطْلَاقُ، فَإِنَّ هَذِهِ الكَيْفِيَّةَ لَا بُدَّ مِنْهَا لِكُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ ، وَتُسَمَّى بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى مَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ : مَادَّةٌ»، فَإِنْ صَرَّحَ بِاللَّفْظِ الدَّالّ عَلَيْهَا ؛ كَأَنْ يُقَالَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ سُمِّيَ ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُنَا : (بِالضَّرُورَةِ»: «جِهَةٌ»، وَسُمِّيَتْ القَضِيّةُ إِذْ ذَاكَ : «مُوَجَّهَةٌ» . اهـ .
قصارة.
قوله : (الَّتِي هِيَ الضَّرُورَةُ، وَمُقَابِلُهَا ... إلخ) إِنَّما انحصرت أنواع الكيفية في الأمور الأربعة؛ لأنَّ الكيفيَّة : إمَّا ضرورة، أو لا ضرورة.
ولا ضرورة هي الإمكان العام؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة هي: ما لا يجوز العقل خلافه، ومقابله : ما يجوز العقل خلافه وهو الإمكان، فتقابلا تقابل المساوي للنقيض، فصح الانحصار بعدم وجود ثالث، وأما في الأخيرين فلأنَّ الدَّوام يقابله: «لا دوام»، وهو إطلاق.
قوله : (لِكُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ مرادُهُ : النّسبة الحكميَّة التي هي موردُ الإيجاب والسَّلب، وهي ثبوتُ الشَّي للشَّيء أو انتفاؤه عنه ، لا الماديَّة، ولا الحكم لِمَا تقدَّم أنَّ النِّسبة تُطلقُ بالاشتراك على ما ذكر . ومعنى كلامه : أنَّ هذه النِّسبة لا بدَّ لها من كيفية تتكيَّف بها في نفس الأمر؛ أي: صفة تتّصفُ بها بحسب الواقع، وقد أشار لهذا المعنى شيخنا سيدي حمدون، فقال:
لَا بُدَّ لِلنِّسْبَةِ مِنْ ضَرُورَهُ نَظَرِ تَكُونُ أَوْ ضَرُورَة أَوْ ضِدُّهَا الإِمْكَانُ، ثُمَّ الإطلاق أَوْ ضِدُّهُ الدَّوَامُ ذَاتُ إطلاق تُدْعَى لَدَى اصْطِلاحِهِمْ: مُوَجَّهَه إِنْ كَلِمَةٌ كَانَتْ لَهَا مُوَجَّهَه أي: مدلولاً بها عليها.
قوله : «مَادَّةٌ») أي: مادَّة القضيَّة، كما تسمى أيضاً: عنصرها». قوله : (وَسُمِّيَتْ القَضِيّةُ إِذْ ذَاكَ : «مُوَجَّهَةٌ» كما أنَّها تسمَّى عند تركه منها : «معراة»، وإن كانت ( في نفس الأمر لا تخرج عن معنى الجهة وهي حينئذٍ محتملة لجميع الجهات؛ إلَّا أنَّ الجهة التي جعلها الحاكم للنّسبة دالة على المادة تارةً تكون موافقة للمادة في نفس الأمر، فتكون القضيَّة صادقة؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة»، و : كلُّ جرم متحرك بالإمكان الخاص، وتارة تكون مخالفة لها، فتكون القضيَّة كاذبة؛ كما لو جعلت الضَّرورة جهة للمثال الثاني والإمكان الخاص جهة للأول.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. Which are necessity (darura), and its opposite is possibility (imkan) or perpetuity (dawam), and its opposite is absoluteness (itlaq). For this quality (kayfiyya) is necessary for every relation in reality (fi nafs al-amr), and it is called, with respect to what is in reality, 'matter' (madda). If one uses a word indicating it, such as saying 'Every human is an animal by necessity', then that word indicating it is called 'direction' (jiha), and the proposition is then called 'modal' (muwajjaha). [End of quote.] Commentary: His saying 'which are necessity...' The types of quality are limited to four because quality is either necessity or not necessity. Not necessity is absolute possibility, because necessity is what reason cannot conceive otherwise, and its opposite is what reason can conceive otherwise, which is possibility. So they are opposites like contradictories, and the enumeration is complete because there is no third. As for the latter two, because perpetuity is opposed by 'non-perpetuity', which is absoluteness. His saying 'for every relation' means the relational judgment which is the subject of affirmation and negation, i.e., the existence of a thing for a thing or its non-existence, not the material relation nor the judgment, as previously stated that 'relation' is used homonymously for what was mentioned. The meaning of his statement: this relation necessarily has a quality by which it is qualified in reality, i.e., a property it possesses according to reality. Our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun pointed to this meaning, saying: The relation must have necessity of consideration, either necessity or its opposite possibility, then absoluteness or its opposite perpetuity; it is called in their terminology 'modal' if there is a word indicating it. His saying 'matter' means the matter of the proposition, also called its 'element'. His saying 'and the proposition is then called modal' just as it is called 'bare' (mu'arra) when the word is omitted, but in reality it does not depart from the meaning of direction; it is then potential for all directions. However, the direction that the judge assigns to the relation as indicative of the matter sometimes agrees with the matter in reality, so the proposition is true, like 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'Every body is possibly moving with special possibility', and sometimes it disagrees with it, so the proposition is false, as if one made necessity the direction for the second example and special possibility the direction for the first.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the four modal qualities of propositions: necessity (darura), possibility (imkan), perpetuity (dawam), and absoluteness (itlaq). The commentator begins by stating that every judgment (the relational aspect of a proposition) must have a quality in reality (fi nafs al-amr). This quality, when considered in itself, is called the 'matter' (madda) of the proposition. When it is explicitly stated by a word like 'by necessity', that word is called the 'direction' (jiha), and the proposition becomes 'modal' (muwajjaha). If no modal term is stated, it is called 'bare' (mu'arra). The commentator then explains why these four are exhaustive: any modal quality is either necessity or not necessity; not necessity is absolute possibility (imkan 'amm). This leads to two pairs: necessity vs. possibility, and perpetuity vs. absoluteness. He clarifies that the relation under discussion is the predicative relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) between subject and predicate, not the material relation or the judgment itself. The necessity of a modal quality for every proposition is emphasized: every relation in reality has a certain modal character. The commentator quotes a poem from his teacher Hamdun summarizing the types. Finally, he explains that the stated direction may or may not match the actual matter, giving examples: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is true because the matter is necessary, but if one says 'Every body is necessarily moving' (where the matter is only possible), the proposition is false. This shows the importance of matching the jiha to the madda for truth.

جهة
موجهة
معراة

Key Takeaway
The madda is the real modal quality, while the jiha is a verbal marker; truth requires that the jiha matches the madda.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the following, decide whether the proposition is true or false based on the agreement of jiha and madda: 'The universe is necessarily temporally originated' (true if madda is necessity, but actually it is possible, so false), 'Every human is possibly speaking' (possible madda, jiha is 'possibly', so true).
?
Construct a proposition where the jiha disagrees with the madda and explain why it is false.

Definitions of the Four Modal Terms
Defines the four modal terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define darura, imkan, dawam, and itlaq and provide a logical example from Islamic theology for each.

Core Idea
Modal terms specify the quality of a relation in a proposition.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary defines the four modal terms essential for understanding qiyas and immediate inference. We will break down each term and see how they apply to Islamic theological propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of darura
Find the phrase 'وُجُوبُ النِّسْبَةِ بِالعَقْلِ'. Note that necessity is of the relation, and it is by the intellect, whether immediate or theoretical.
Example: 'The world is originated by necessity' is theoretical necessity.
2
Define imkan
Find 'كَوْنُ النِّسْبَةِ غَيْرَ مُمْتَنِعَةٍ'. Possibility means the relation is not impossible.
Example: 'Every human is a writer by possibility' shows possibility.
3
Define dawam
Find 'استِمْرَارُ النِّسْبَةِ'. Perpetuity means the relation continues for the duration of the subject's existence.
Example: 'Every disbeliever is punished in the afterlife perpetually'.
4
Define itlaq
Find 'ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ بِالفِعْلِ'. Absoluteness means actual affirmation or negation, without modal restriction.
Example: 'Every human is mortal by general absoluteness'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الضَّرُورَةِ» : وُجُوبُ النِّسْبَةِ بِالعَقْلِ وَلَوْ نَظَرِيًّا . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الإِمْكَانِ : كَوْنُ النِّسْبَةِ غَيْرَ مُمْتَنِعَةٍ، وَ: «الدَّوَامِ» : اسْتِمْرَارُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَ الإِطْلَاقِ : ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ بِالفِعْلِ، أَوْ نَفْيُهُ عَنْهُ كَذَلِكَ.
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «مُقَابَلَةِ الإِمْكَانِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ : أَنَّهُ يُقَابِلُهَا فِي نَقِيضِ قَضِيَّتِهَا ، نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَنَقِيضُهُ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ بِالإِمْكَانِ»، وَكَذَا الإِطْلَاقُ يُقَابِلُ الدَّوَامَ فِي نَقِيض قَضِيَّتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَالإِمْكَانُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الضَّرُورَةِ، وَالإِطْلَاقُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الدَّوَامِ، وَالأَعَمُّ يُجَامِعُ الأَخَصَّ وَلَا يُقَابِلُهُ .
وقوله : (وبهذا يرد ما يقال : إذا كانت الجهة دالة على المادَّة، وهي الكيفية التي في نفس الأمر، فنحو: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالإمكان الخاص لا تكون موجهةً ؛ لأنَّها ليست دالة على ما في نفس الأمر، بل على خلافه .
قوله : (وُجُوبُ النِّسْبَةِ بِالعَقْلِ) أي : سواء كانت إيجابية أو سلبية ضروريا؛ نحو: «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو نظرياً؛ نحو: «العالم حادث بالضرورة» و : «ليس العالم بقديم بالضّرورة». قوله : (كَوْنُ النِّسْبَةِ غَيْرَ مُمْتَنِعَةٍ) يعني : نسبة المحمول للموضوع؛ إيجابيَّةً كانت، أو سلبيَّة؛ نحو : كلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان». قوله : (وَ: «الدَّوَام»: اسْتِمْرَارُ النِّسْبَةِ) أي: استمرار نسبة المحمول للموضوع بدوام ذات الموضوع إيجاباً أو سلباً ؛ نحو: كلُّ كافر معذَّب في الآخرة دائماً». قوله: (وَ: «الإطلاق»: ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ بِالفِعْلِ) مثالُهُ: «كل إنسان فهو ميت بالإطلاق العام». فإن قيل : الإطلاق في الأصل عدم التقييد بجهةٍ مِنَ الجهات فلا ينبغي عده في الموجهات . فالجواب : أنَّهم اصطلحوا على تخصيص اسم المطلقة بما كانت نسبتها فعليَّة؛ لتبادر الفعل إلى الذهن عند الإطلاق دون الإمكان فللفعليّة كيفيَّةٌ زائدة على نفس النّسبة؛ لأنَّ النِّسبة أعم من أن تكون بالفعل أو بالإمكان فليس المراد بـ «الإطلاق»: عدم التَّقييد، حتَّى يَرِدَ ما ذكر .
And what is meant by 'necessity' is the necessity of the relation by the intellect, even if theoretical. And what is meant by 'possibility' is that the relation is not impossible. And 'perpetuity' is the continuation of the relation. And 'absoluteness' is the actual affirmation of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it, likewise.
And what is meant by 'the contrast of possibility to necessity' is that it contrasts with it in the contradictory of its proposition, like: 'Every human is an animal by necessity', and its contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal by possibility.' And likewise absoluteness contrasts with perpetuity in the contradictory of its proposition. Otherwise, possibility is more general than necessity, and absoluteness is more general than perpetuity, and the more general co-occurs with the more specific and does not contrast with it.
He said: (and by this is refuted what is said: If the direction indicates the material, which is the quality in reality, then an example like 'Every human is an animal by specific possibility' would not be directed because it does not indicate what is in reality, but rather its opposite.)
He said: (the necessity of the relation by the intellect) i.e., whether it is affirmative or negative, either necessary like 'Every human is an animal by necessity', or theoretical like 'The world is originated by necessity' and 'The world is not eternal by necessity.' He said: (that the relation is not impossible) means the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether affirmative or negative; like 'Every human is a writer by possibility.' He said: (and 'perpetuity' is the continuation of the relation) i.e., the continuation of the relation of the predicate to the subject for the duration of the subject's existence, affirmative or negative; like 'Every disbeliever is punished in the afterlife perpetually.' He said: (and 'absoluteness' is the actual affirmation of the predicate for the subject) its example: 'Every human is mortal by general absoluteness.' And if it is said: Absoluteness originally means absence of restriction to any direction, so it should not be counted among directed propositions. The answer: They terminologically restricted the name 'absolute' to that whose relation is actual, because actuality springs to mind when absoluteness is mentioned, unlike possibility. So actuality has an additional quality beyond the relation itself, because the relation is more general than being actual or possible. So what is meant by 'absoluteness' is not the absence of restriction, such that the objection would arise.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage provides definitions for the four modal terms and explains how they contrast. First, darura (necessity) is defined as the necessity of the relation by the intellect, whether the necessity is immediate (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). Imkan (possibility) means the relation is not impossible, i.e., it is possible for the predicate to be affirmed or denied of the subject. Dawam (perpetuity) means the relation holds continuously for as long as the subject exists. Itlaq (absoluteness) means the predicate is actually affirmed or denied of the subject.

The text then clarifies how these terms contrast: possibility contrasts with necessity not directly, but in the contradictory of a necessary proposition. For example, 'Every human is an animal by necessity' has as its contradictory 'Some human is not an animal by possibility.' Similarly, absoluteness contrasts with perpetuity in the contradictory. However, possibility is more general than necessity (it includes both necessity and non-necessity), and absoluteness is more general than perpetuity. The more general does not directly oppose the more specific; they can co-occur.

A refutation is given against the objection that if direction indicates the material (the reality), then a proposition like 'Every human is an animal by specific possibility' would not be directed because the reality is necessity, not possibility. The answer is that direction only indicates the modality in reality if it is correct; otherwise it indicates a claim about reality, which may be false.

Finally, the passage expands each definition with examples and answers an objection about absoluteness: although it originally means absence of restriction, in logic it is restricted to actuality because that is the default meaning. Therefore, 'every human is mortal by general absoluteness' is a directed proposition because its actuality is explicit.

How Modal Terms Contrast in Contradictory Propositions
Explains the contrast of modal terms in contradictory propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a modal proposition by correctly applying the contrast rule between possibility and necessity, and between absoluteness and perpetuity.

Core Idea
Possibility contrasts with necessity only in the contradictory, not directly.

The passage explains an important rule: modal terms of different scope (general vs. specific) do not directly oppose each other. Instead, the general term (e.g., possibility) contrasts with the specific term (necessity) in the contradictory of the specific proposition. We will see how this works and apply it to Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule of contrast
Find the sentence: 'والمُرَادُ بِـ «مُقَابَلَةِ الإِمْكَانِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ : أَنَّهُ يُقَابِلُهَا فِي نَقِيضِ قَضِيَّتِهَا'. Understand that possibility does not directly oppose necessity but does so in the contradictory.
2
Work through the example
Take the necessary proposition: 'Every human is an animal by necessity'. Its contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal by possibility'. Notice that the modal term changed from necessity to possibility.
This is because necessity entails actuality, but possibility is the complement in the contradictory.
3
Apply the same to absoluteness and perpetuity
Similarly, absoluteness contrasts with perpetuity in the contradictory. For example, 'Every disbeliever is punished in the afterlife perpetually' has contradictory 'Some disbeliever is not punished in the afterlife by absoluteness'.
4
Understand why direct contrast fails
The passage explains that possibility is more general than necessity (it includes both necessity and non-necessity). The more general does not directly oppose the more specific; they can coexist.
That is why the contrast is via the contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الضَّرُورَةِ» : وُجُوبُ النِّسْبَةِ بِالعَقْلِ وَلَوْ نَظَرِيًّا . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «الإِمْكَانِ : كَوْنُ النِّسْبَةِ غَيْرَ مُمْتَنِعَةٍ، وَ: «الدَّوَامِ» : اسْتِمْرَارُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَ الإِطْلَاقِ : ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ بِالفِعْلِ، أَوْ نَفْيُهُ عَنْهُ كَذَلِكَ.
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «مُقَابَلَةِ الإِمْكَانِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ : أَنَّهُ يُقَابِلُهَا فِي نَقِيضِ قَضِيَّتِهَا ، نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَنَقِيضُهُ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ بِالإِمْكَانِ»، وَكَذَا الإِطْلَاقُ يُقَابِلُ الدَّوَامَ فِي نَقِيض قَضِيَّتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَالإِمْكَانُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الضَّرُورَةِ، وَالإِطْلَاقُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الدَّوَامِ، وَالأَعَمُّ يُجَامِعُ الأَخَصَّ وَلَا يُقَابِلُهُ .
وقوله : (وبهذا يرد ما يقال : إذا كانت الجهة دالة على المادَّة، وهي الكيفية التي في نفس الأمر، فنحو: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالإمكان الخاص لا تكون موجهةً ؛ لأنَّها ليست دالة على ما في نفس الأمر، بل على خلافه .
قوله : (وُجُوبُ النِّسْبَةِ بِالعَقْلِ) أي : سواء كانت إيجابية أو سلبية ضروريا؛ نحو: «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو نظرياً؛ نحو: «العالم حادث بالضرورة» و : «ليس العالم بقديم بالضّرورة». قوله : (كَوْنُ النِّسْبَةِ غَيْرَ مُمْتَنِعَةٍ) يعني : نسبة المحمول للموضوع؛ إيجابيَّةً كانت، أو سلبيَّة؛ نحو : كلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان». قوله : (وَ: «الدَّوَام»: اسْتِمْرَارُ النِّسْبَةِ) أي: استمرار نسبة المحمول للموضوع بدوام ذات الموضوع إيجاباً أو سلباً ؛ نحو: كلُّ كافر معذَّب في الآخرة دائماً». قوله: (وَ: «الإطلاق»: ثُبُوتُ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوعِ بِالفِعْلِ) مثالُهُ: «كل إنسان فهو ميت بالإطلاق العام». فإن قيل : الإطلاق في الأصل عدم التقييد بجهةٍ مِنَ الجهات فلا ينبغي عده في الموجهات . فالجواب : أنَّهم اصطلحوا على تخصيص اسم المطلقة بما كانت نسبتها فعليَّة؛ لتبادر الفعل إلى الذهن عند الإطلاق دون الإمكان فللفعليّة كيفيَّةٌ زائدة على نفس النّسبة؛ لأنَّ النِّسبة أعم من أن تكون بالفعل أو بالإمكان فليس المراد بـ «الإطلاق»: عدم التَّقييد، حتَّى يَرِدَ ما ذكر .
And what is meant by 'necessity' is the necessity of the relation by the intellect, even if theoretical. And what is meant by 'possibility' is that the relation is not impossible. And 'perpetuity' is the continuation of the relation. And 'absoluteness' is the actual affirmation of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it, likewise.
And what is meant by 'the contrast of possibility to necessity' is that it contrasts with it in the contradictory of its proposition, like: 'Every human is an animal by necessity', and its contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal by possibility.' And likewise absoluteness contrasts with perpetuity in the contradictory of its proposition. Otherwise, possibility is more general than necessity, and absoluteness is more general than perpetuity, and the more general co-occurs with the more specific and does not contrast with it.
He said: (and by this is refuted what is said: If the direction indicates the material, which is the quality in reality, then an example like 'Every human is an animal by specific possibility' would not be directed because it does not indicate what is in reality, but rather its opposite.)
He said: (the necessity of the relation by the intellect) i.e., whether it is affirmative or negative, either necessary like 'Every human is an animal by necessity', or theoretical like 'The world is originated by necessity' and 'The world is not eternal by necessity.' He said: (that the relation is not impossible) means the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether affirmative or negative; like 'Every human is a writer by possibility.' He said: (and 'perpetuity' is the continuation of the relation) i.e., the continuation of the relation of the predicate to the subject for the duration of the subject's existence, affirmative or negative; like 'Every disbeliever is punished in the afterlife perpetually.' He said: (and 'absoluteness' is the actual affirmation of the predicate for the subject) its example: 'Every human is mortal by general absoluteness.' And if it is said: Absoluteness originally means absence of restriction to any direction, so it should not be counted among directed propositions. The answer: They terminologically restricted the name 'absolute' to that whose relation is actual, because actuality springs to mind when absoluteness is mentioned, unlike possibility. So actuality has an additional quality beyond the relation itself, because the relation is more general than being actual or possible. So what is meant by 'absoluteness' is not the absence of restriction, such that the objection would arise.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage provides definitions for the four modal terms and explains how they contrast. First, darura (necessity) is defined as the necessity of the relation by the intellect, whether the necessity is immediate (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). Imkan (possibility) means the relation is not impossible, i.e., it is possible for the predicate to be affirmed or denied of the subject. Dawam (perpetuity) means the relation holds continuously for as long as the subject exists. Itlaq (absoluteness) means the predicate is actually affirmed or denied of the subject.

The text then clarifies how these terms contrast: possibility contrasts with necessity not directly, but in the contradictory of a necessary proposition. For example, 'Every human is an animal by necessity' has as its contradictory 'Some human is not an animal by possibility.' Similarly, absoluteness contrasts with perpetuity in the contradictory. However, possibility is more general than necessity (it includes both necessity and non-necessity), and absoluteness is more general than perpetuity. The more general does not directly oppose the more specific; they can co-occur.

A refutation is given against the objection that if direction indicates the material (the reality), then a proposition like 'Every human is an animal by specific possibility' would not be directed because the reality is necessity, not possibility. The answer is that direction only indicates the modality in reality if it is correct; otherwise it indicates a claim about reality, which may be false.

Finally, the passage expands each definition with examples and answers an objection about absoluteness: although it originally means absence of restriction, in logic it is restricted to actuality because that is the default meaning. Therefore, 'every human is mortal by general absoluteness' is a directed proposition because its actuality is explicit.

C) Because they are the same

Key Takeaway
General modal terms contrast with specific ones only in the contradictory of the specific proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every believer is rewarded in paradise by perpetuity'. Write its contradictory using the correct modal term. Answer: 'Some believer is not rewarded in paradise by absoluteness'.

Refuting an Objection: The Legitimacy of Specific Possibility Propositions
Refutes an objection about specific possibility propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that a proposition with 'specific possibility' cannot be a directed proposition by explaining the distinction between the direction and the reality.

Core Idea
A proposition's direction indicates the claim made, not necessarily the actual state of affairs.

An objection is raised against the use of 'specific possibility' in directed propositions. The objector argues that since the reality is necessity, claiming possibility is contrary to reality and thus not a proper direction. The commentator refutes this by clarifying that direction concerns the quality of the claim, not correspondence with reality. Let's analyze this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the phrase 'وَبهذا يرد ما يقال: إذا كانت الجهة دالة على المادة... فنحو: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالإمكان الخاص لا تكون موجهة'. The objection is that a proposition with specific possibility (imkan khas) cannot be directed because the reality is necessity, so the direction would not indicate the reality.
2
Understand the response
The commentator refutes by saying that the direction indicates the modality that the proposition asserts, not the actual reality. Even if the reality is necessity, one can still make a claim of possibility, and that claim is a directed proposition.
3
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between the truth condition of the proposition and its direction. Direction is about the claim of the modality, not the absolute reality.
4
Evaluate the refutation
The refutation is sound because it maintains that logical direction is about the form of the proposition, not its correspondence with every reality. This allows modal logic to handle false but directed propositions.

Source Text

Distinguishing Positive (Muhassala) from Privative (Ma'dula) Propositions
Author defines and exemplifies positive (muhassala) and privative (ma'dula) propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is muhassala or ma'dula and determine which type of ma'dula it is (subject, predicate, or both).

Core Idea
The key difference is whether negation is part of the term or external to the assertion.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial classification of categorical propositions based on the role of the negation particle. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing the logical form of statements in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with quantified propositions. We will break down the definitions and examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
Look for 'la', 'laysa', or other negation words in the proposition. If there is no negation at all, the proposition is muhassala (simple affirmative).
2
Determine if negation is attached to a term
If negation is present, check whether it is directly attached to the subject (e.g., 'la hayy' as a single concept), to the predicate (e.g., 'la 'alim'), or stands separate (e.g., 'laysa Zaid bi-'alim').
A separate negation ('laysa') that negates the copula does not make the proposition ma'dula.
3
Classify by attachment
If negation is part of the subject only → ma'dulat al-mawdu'. If part of the predicate only → ma'dulat al-mahmul. If part of both → ma'dulat al-tarafayn. Otherwise, it is muhassala.
Example: 'Every non-living is inanimate' is ma'dulat al-mawdu' because 'non-living' contains 'la'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّنْبِيهُ الثَّالِثُ : جَمِيعُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةَ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مُحَصِّلَةٍ، وَمَعْدُولَةٍ . فَالمُحَصِّلَةُ هِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَكُونُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ جُزْءاً لِشَيْءٍ مِنْ طَرَفِيهَا»؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَكُونَ فِيهَا سَلْبٌ أَصْلاً ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ فِيهَا وَلَمْ يُجْعَلْ جُزْءاً مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِعَالِمٍ». وَالمَعْدُولَةُ هِيَ : الَّتِي يُجْعَلُ السَّلْبُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَوْضُوعِهَا»؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلٌّ لَا حَيٌّ جَمَادٌ»، أَوْ مِنْ مَحْمُولِهَا ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ ، أَوْ مِنْهُمَا ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ لَا حَيٌّ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ»، فَالأُوْلَى مَعْدُولَةُ المَوْضُوعِ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ مَعْدُولَةُ المَحْمُولِ، وَالثَّالِثَةُ مَعْدُولَةُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ .
The Third Notice: All that has preceded from the categorical propositions divides into two divisions: positive (muhassala) and privative (ma'dula). The positive is that in which the particle of negation is not a part of either of its two extremes; meaning that there is no negation in it at all, like 'Zaid is a scholar', or there is negation in it but it is not made part of either of them, like 'Zaid is not a scholar'. The privative is that in which negation is made a part of its subject, like 'Every non-living is inanimate', or of its predicate, like 'Zaid is not-a-scholar', or of both, like 'Every non-living is not-a-scholar'. The first is privative of subject, the second privative of predicate, and the third privative of both extremes.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of categorical propositions from a sharh (commentary) on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author explains a third note (تنبيه) in which he divides all previously mentioned categorical propositions into two types: muhassala (positive, or 'achieved') and ma'dula (privative, or 'negated'). A proposition is muhassala if the negation particle (like 'laysa' or 'la') is not an integral part of either the subject or the predicate term. This includes two subcases: either the proposition has no negation at all (e.g., 'Zaid is a scholar'), or it contains a negation that negates the whole copula but does not enter into the composition of the subject or predicate term itself (e.g., 'Zaid is not a scholar' – here the negation is on the copula, not part of 'Zaid' or 'scholar'). The second main type is ma'dula, where the negation particle is made part of either the subject term, the predicate term, or both. This yields three varieties: (1) ma'dulat al-mawdu' (privative of subject), e.g., 'Every non-living is inanimate' – here 'non-living' includes 'la' as part of the subject concept; (2) ma'dulat al-mahmul (privative of predicate), e.g., 'Zaid is not-a-scholar' – here the predicate is 'not-a-scholar' as a single term; (3) ma'dulat al-tarafayn (privative of both extremes), e.g., 'Every non-living is not-a-scholar' – negation enters both subject and predicate. The distinction is crucial for understanding logical form: in a muhassala proposition, the negation is external to the terms, while in a ma'dula it is internal, affecting the intension of the terms. This classification is foundational for later discussions of quantification and modality.

Key Terms
مُحَصِّلَة
مَعْدُولَة
حَرْفُ السَّلْب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a ma'dulat al-mawdu' (privative of subject) proposition?
A) 'Zaid is not a scholar'
B) 'Every non-living is inanimate'
C) 'Zaid is not-a-scholar'
D) 'Some human is not a writer'

Key Takeaway
The classification of categorical propositions into muhassala and ma'dula hinges on whether the negation particle is internal or external to the terms, which affects logical form and subsequent inference rules.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following propositions as muhassala or ma'dula; if ma'dula, specify the type (subject, predicate, or both): (a) 'Zaid is not a scholar' (b) 'Every non-believer is in hell' (c) 'The borrower is not-trustworthy' (d) 'Some animal is not a horse'.
?
Consider the proposition 'Every non-living (la hayy) is not-sentient (la hiss)'. Explain why it is a ma'dula of both extremes and rewrite it as a muhassala proposition.

Affirmative Categorical Propositions (al-Muḥaṣṣila)
Author defines affirmative and negative propositions and the three types of negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is affirmative (muḥaṣṣila) and distinguish it from negative-term propositions.

Core Idea
An affirmative proposition keeps negation outside the terms.

In this lesson, we learn the definition of muḥaṣṣila, which includes both positive propositions like 'Zayd is knowledgeable' and negative copula propositions like 'Zayd is not knowledgeable', as long as the negation is not attached to the subject or predicate. This distinction is subtle but crucial for later operations.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
Search for 'laysa' or 'lā' in the proposition. If absent, the proposition is straightforward affirmative.
2
Check if negation is attached to a term
See if the negation immediately precedes the subject or predicate, forming a compound term like 'lā ʿālimun' (non-knowledgeable).
3
Classify as muḥaṣṣila
If the negation is not part of any term, the proposition is muḥaṣṣila, regardless of whether the overall statement is affirmative or negative.
Example: 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' has negation on the copula, not on 'Zayd' or 'knowledgeable'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّنْبِيهُ الثَّالِثُ : جَمِيعُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةَ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مُحَصِّلَةٍ، وَمَعْدُولَةٍ . فَالمُحَصِّلَةُ هِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَكُونُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ جُزْءاً لِشَيْءٍ مِنْ طَرَفِيهَا»؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَكُونَ فِيهَا سَلْبٌ أَصْلاً ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ ، أَوْ يَكُونَ فِيهَا وَلَمْ يُجْعَلْ جُزْءاً مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِعَالِمٍ». وَالمَعْدُولَةُ هِيَ : الَّتِي يُجْعَلُ السَّلْبُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَوْضُوعِهَا»؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلٌّ لَا حَيٌّ جَمَادٌ»، أَوْ مِنْ مَحْمُولِهَا ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ ، أَوْ مِنْهُمَا ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ لَا حَيٌّ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ»، فَالأُوْلَى مَعْدُولَةُ المَوْضُوعِ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ مَعْدُولَةُ المَحْمُولِ، وَالثَّالِثَةُ مَعْدُولَةُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ .
The third notice: All the preceding categorical propositions are divided into two divisions: affirmative (muhaṣṣila) and negative (maʿdūla). The affirmative is that in which the particle of negation is not a part of either of its two extremes; either there is no negation at all, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable'; or there is negation but it is not made part of either extreme, like 'Zayd is not knowledgeable'. The negative (maʿdūla) is that in which negation is made part of its subject, like 'Every non-living is inanimate'; or part of its predicate, like 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable'; or both, like 'Every non-living is non-knowledgeable'. The first is negative in subject, the second is negative in predicate, the third is negative in both extremes.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification. The author divides all categorical propositions into two main types: affirmative (muhaṣṣila) and negative (maʿdūla). Affirmative propositions either have no negation, e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable', or have negation that does not enter into the term itself, e.g., 'Zayd is not knowledgeable'. The negative propositions have the negation integrated as part of the subject or predicate, producing three subtypes: negative subject, negative predicate, and both. This distinction is crucial because the logical properties of these propositions differ, especially in conversion and contradiction.

Key Terms
محصلة
طرف

D) Every non-living is non-knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
A muḥaṣṣila proposition can be either positive or negative in meaning, as long as negation is not a component of any term.

Negative Categorical Propositions (al-Maʿdūla) and Their Types
Author defines affirmative and negative propositions and the three types of negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a negative proposition (maʿdūla) and classify it as maʿdūlat al-mawḍūʿ, maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl, or maʿdūlat al-ṭarafayn.

Core Idea
Negative propositions embed negation into the term itself, creating three distinct types.

This lesson explains the second main division of categorical propositions: maʿdūla, where the negation particle is part of the subject or predicate. The author gives three examples, each showing a different placement of the negation. Understanding these types is essential for correct logical manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Find 'lā' or similar particle that may be attached to a term.
2
Determine which term contains the negation
Check if the negation is attached to the subject (e.g., 'lā ḥayyun'), predicate (e.g., 'lā ʿālimun'), or both.
If the negation is not attached to any term, it is not maʿdūla.
3
Classify the type
If negation is only in subject: maʿdūlat al-mawḍūʿ; only in predicate: maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl; in both: maʿdūlat al-ṭarafayn.
Example: 'Every non-living is inanimate' has 'non-living' as a negative subject, so it is maʿdūlat al-mawḍūʿ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The affirmative is that in which the particle of negation is not a part of either of its two extremes; either there is no negation at all, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable'; or there is negation but it is not made part of either extreme, like 'Zayd is not knowledgeable'. The negative (maʿdūla) is that in which negation is made part of its subject, like 'Every non-living is inanimate'; or part of its predicate, like 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable'; or both, like 'Every non-living is non-knowledgeable'. The first is negative in subject, the second is negative in predicate, the third is negative in both extremes.

The 19 Modal Propositions: Classification Overview
Author breaks down the 19 propositions into types and gives a mnemonic poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to break down the 19 modal propositions into unqualified and qualified categories, and compute the total count for each modal type.

Core Idea
19 propositions = 4 unqualified + 15 qualified; modal types interlock.

The matn stated that the previous categorical propositions amount to 19. In this passage, the commentator details that these 19 are composed of 4 unqualified (one from each modal type: necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible) and 15 qualified. He then gives the exact counts for each modal category and confirms the total. This classification is the backbone for analyzing modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base claim from the matn
The matn asserts there are 19 categorical propositions. The commentator expands this by distinguishing unqualified (muṭlaqa min al-qayd) and qualified (muqayyada).
Unqualified propositions have no additional temporal or modal condition; qualified ones do.
2
Distinguish unqualified and qualified propositions
Note that there are 4 unqualified (one per modal type) and 15 qualified. Together they sum to 19.
The commentator then breaks down the qualified into specific modal categories.
3
Count the necessary propositions
The necessary (ḍarūriyya) propositions: 1 unqualified + 6 qualified = 7 total. The poem later enumerates these as: absolute necessary, conditional (general and specific), temporal, spreading (general and specific).
These correspond to the first lines of the poem.
4
Count the perpetual, absolute, and possible propositions
Perpetual (dāʾima): 1 unqualified + 2 qualified = 3 total. Absolute (muṭlaqa in the specific sense): 1 unqualified + 3 qualified = 4 total. Possible (mumkina): 1 unqualified + 4 qualified = 5 total.
The commentator explicitly states these totals.
5
Verify the total of 19
Add the totals: necessary (7) + perpetual (3) + absolute (4) + possible (5) = 19. This confirms the matn's claim.
This verification shows the logical consistency of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تِسْعَ عَشَرَةَ قَضِيَّةٌ) أربع مطلقة من القيد من كلِّ نوع واحدة، وخمس عشرة مقيدة؛ ستُ ضروريَّات، وثنتان مِنَ الدَّوائم، وثلاث مِنَ المطلقات بالمعنى السابق، وأربع من الممكنات؛ فالضَّروريّة مطلقها ومقيدها سبع، والدوائم كذلك ثلاث، والمطلقات من حيث هي أربع، والممكنات جميعها خمس وقد نظم هذا شيخ بعض شيوخنا مولانا أبو حفص الفاسي، فقال: وَلِلضَّرُورَةِ انم مُطلَقاً يُرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ وَقْتِيَّةٌ مُنْتَشِرًا بِقَيْدِ لَا دَوَامَ أَوْ فِي مَا سَوَى مُطْلَقَةٍ، فَالنَّظْمُ سَبْعاً قَدْ حَوَى وَلِلدَّوَامِ مُطلقاً عُرْفِيَّة وَبِالعُمُومِ وَالخُصُوص تُنْعَت فَهِيَ ثَلَاتٌ وَلِلإطلاقِ بَدًا مُطْلَقَةٌ عَمَّتْ وَزِدْ مَا وُجِدًا رُورَةٌ وَلَا دَوام حِيْنِيَّةٌ تَرْبعُ فِي النَّظَّام ستا نَمَتْ لِلإِمْكَانِ مَا عَمَّ وَمَا خُصَّ وَمَا لِلوَقْتِ وَالحِيْنِ انْتَمَى ثُمَّ المُرَكَّبَاتُ مِنْهَا تُنْظَم وَلِلدَّوَامِ فَهِيَ خَمْسٌ تَعْلَم مُمْكِنَةٌ خُمَّتْ وَذَاتاً لا كَذَا سَبْعٌ وَبِالشَّظَرِ بِهَا فَلْتَاخُذَا
His saying: '19 propositions': four unqualified from the restriction, one of each type; and fifteen qualified: six necessary, two of the perpetuals, three of the absolute in the previous sense, and four of the possible. So the necessary: its absolute and its qualified are seven; the perpetuals are three; the absolutes (as such) are four; the possibles are five in total. And one of our teachers, Mawlana Abu Hafs al-Fasi, composed: (poem)...
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharḥ) on the matn's statement that the previous categorical propositions amount to 19. The author details that 4 are unqualified (one of each type: necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible) and 15 are qualified. He then counts 6 necessary, 2 perpetual, 3 absolute, 4 possible as qualified; but then says necessary absolute and qualified are 7 (including the unqualified), perpetuals 3, absolutes 4, possibles 5 (including unqualified? Actually the possible unqualified is one, so qualified possible are 4, total 5). This totals 19 when combined. He then presents a mnemonic poem by Abu Hafs al-Fasi that lists the types. The poem enumerates the necessary types: absolute, conditional (general and specific), temporal, spreading (general and specific), etc. Then perpetual: absolute, conventional (general and specific). Then absolute (in the sense): general absolute, existential non-necessary, etc. Then possible and compounds. This classification is the standard framework for modal propositions in post-classical logic.

دائمة
مطلقة
ممكنة

Key Takeaway
The 19 propositions are systematically divided into four modal categories, each with a specific number of unqualified and qualified subtypes, totaling 19.
