The Mnemonic Poem for the 19 Propositions
Author breaks down the 19 propositions into types and gives a mnemonic poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decode the mnemonic poem by Abu Hafs al-Fasi and list the specific proposition types it enumerates.

Core Idea
The poem condenses 19 proposition types into a memorable verse.

After giving the numerical breakdown, the commentator presents a poem by his teacher's teacher, Abu Hafs al-Fasi, that names each of the 19 propositions. This poem serves as a memory aid, listing the necessary types first (7), then perpetual (3), absolute (4), and possible (5), with compound propositions also mentioned. Understanding the poem helps internalize the classification.

Guided Path
1
Locate the poem in the passage
The poem begins after 'فقال:' and is written in rajaz meter. It is a single line in the text but broken into segments for clarity.
The poem is a mnemonic, not a full explanation.
2
Parse the first part on necessary propositions
The first line says: 'وَلِلضَّرُورَةِ انم مُطلَقاً يُرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ وَقْتِيَّةٌ مُنْتَشِرًا بِقَيْدِ لَا دَوَامَ أَوْ فِي مَا سَوَى مُطْلَقَةٍ'. This enumerates: absolute necessary, conditional (general and specific), temporal, spreading (general and specific), and 'in what is other than absolute' – that gives 7.
Each phrase corresponds to a subtype; the total is 7 as stated earlier.
3
Continue with perpetual, absolute, and possible propositions
Next: 'وَلِلدَّوَامِ مُطلقاً عُرْفِيَّة وَبِالعُمُومِ وَالخُصُوص تُنْعَت فَهِيَ ثَلَاتٌ' – perpetual: absolute, conventional (general and specific) = 3. Then: 'وَلِلإطلاقِ بَدًا مُطْلَقَةٌ عَمَّتْ وَزِدْ مَا وُجِدًا رُورَةٌ وَلَا دَوام حِيْنِيَّةٌ تَرْبعُ' – absolute: general absolute, non-necessary existence, temporal without perpetuity = 4. Then: 'لِلإِمْكَانِ مَا عَمَّ وَمَا خُصَّ وَمَا لِلوَقْتِ وَالحِيْنِ انتَمَى ثُمَّ المُرَكَّبَاتُ مِنْهَا تُنْظَم وَلِلدَّوَامِ فَهِيَ خَمْسٌ تَعْلَم مُمْكِنَةٌ خُمَّتْ وَذَاتاً لا كَذَا سَبْعٌ' – possible: general, specific, temporal, six (?) and then compound from them, and then 'five you know' – this is confusing; the commentator earlier said possibles are 5, but the poem seems to count more? Actually the poem says 'five you know' for the possible, and then mentions compounds, perhaps making 7? But the text says 'possible: five in total'. The poem might have a different count for possible including compounds? The commentator says 'and the compounds from them are organized' and then 'for the perpetual they are five' – possibly misread. Better to stick with the earlier explicit count: possibles total 5, and the poem lists 5 specific types plus compounds? The poem requires careful parsing but the commentator's summary is authoritative.
The poem's exact interpretation may vary; but the commentator's arithmetic is clear: possibles = 5.
4
Use the poem as a memory aid
Memorize the poem segment by segment. The first line gives the 7 necessary, the second gives 3 perpetuals, third gives 4 absolutes, fourth gives 5 possibles and compounds. Practice recalling the types.
The poem is metered and rhythmic, making it easier to remember.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تِسْعَ عَشَرَةَ قَضِيَّةٌ) أربع مطلقة من القيد من كلِّ نوع واحدة، وخمس عشرة مقيدة؛ ستُ ضروريَّات، وثنتان مِنَ الدَّوائم، وثلاث مِنَ المطلقات بالمعنى السابق، وأربع من الممكنات؛ فالضَّروريّة مطلقها ومقيدها سبع، والدوائم كذلك ثلاث، والمطلقات من حيث هي أربع، والممكنات جميعها خمس وقد نظم هذا شيخ بعض شيوخنا مولانا أبو حفص الفاسي، فقال: وَلِلضَّرُورَةِ انم مُطلَقاً يُرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ وَقْتِيَّةٌ مُنْتَشِرًا بِقَيْدِ لَا دَوَامَ أَوْ فِي مَا سَوَى مُطْلَقَةٍ، فَالنَّظْمُ سَبْعاً قَدْ حَوَى وَلِلدَّوَامِ مُطلقاً عُرْفِيَّة وَبِالعُمُومِ وَالخُصُوص تُنْعَت فَهِيَ ثَلَاتٌ وَلِلإطلاقِ بَدًا مُطْلَقَةٌ عَمَّتْ وَزِدْ مَا وُجِدًا رُورَةٌ وَلَا دَوام حِيْنِيَّةٌ تَرْبعُ فِي النَّظَّام ستا نَمَتْ لِلإِمْكَانِ مَا عَمَّ وَمَا خُصَّ وَمَا لِلوَقْتِ وَالحِيْنِ انْتَمَى ثُمَّ المُرَكَّبَاتُ مِنْهَا تُنْظَم وَلِلدَّوَامِ فَهِيَ خَمْسٌ تَعْلَم مُمْكِنَةٌ خُمَّتْ وَذَاتاً لا كَذَا سَبْعٌ وَبِالشَّظَرِ بِهَا فَلْتَاخُذَا
His saying: '19 propositions': four unqualified from the restriction, one of each type; and fifteen qualified: six necessary, two of the perpetuals, three of the absolute in the previous sense, and four of the possible. So the necessary: its absolute and its qualified are seven; the perpetuals are three; the absolutes (as such) are four; the possibles are five in total. And one of our teachers, Mawlana Abu Hafs al-Fasi, composed: (poem)...
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharḥ) on the matn's statement that the previous categorical propositions amount to 19. The author details that 4 are unqualified (one of each type: necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible) and 15 are qualified. He then counts 6 necessary, 2 perpetual, 3 absolute, 4 possible as qualified; but then says necessary absolute and qualified are 7 (including the unqualified), perpetuals 3, absolutes 4, possibles 5 (including unqualified? Actually the possible unqualified is one, so qualified possible are 4, total 5). This totals 19 when combined. He then presents a mnemonic poem by Abu Hafs al-Fasi that lists the types. The poem enumerates the necessary types: absolute, conditional (general and specific), temporal, spreading (general and specific), etc. Then perpetual: absolute, conventional (general and specific). Then absolute (in the sense): general absolute, existential non-necessary, etc. Then possible and compounds. This classification is the standard framework for modal propositions in post-classical logic.

Seven Necessary Propositions in Modal Logic
Author enumerates the seven necessary propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish each of the seven types of necessary propositions based on their qualifications and examples.

Core Idea
Necessity is qualified: absolute, conditional on a description, specific time, or indefinite time.

In modal logic, propositions can assert necessity with varying degrees of qualification. This lesson unpacks the seven classifications of necessary propositions, each illustrated with an Islamic science example. Mastering these distinctions will sharpen your ability to analyze modal claims in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
Notice that the seven types are grouped by what qualifies the necessity: no qualification, a description of the subject, a specific time, or an indefinite time.
2
Distinguish absolute from conditional
The absolute necessary (e.g., 'Every body is temporally originated') has no condition. The conditional ties necessity to a description (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is writing').
The absolute is unconditionally true.
3
Differentiate general vs specific conditional
The general conditional does not address what happens when the description ceases; the specific conditional explicitly states that the necessity does not persist when the description is left.
Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is writing, not perpetually' is specific.
4
Recognize temporal vs spreading
Temporal propositions specify a definite time (e.g., 'at the time of writing'); spreading propositions use an indefinite time (e.g., 'at some time').
5
Practice with examples
Given a statement like 'Every possible being is non-existent at some time', classify it as spreading absolute. Apply the criteria to each example in the passage.
Check if time is definite or indefinite, and if a condition is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١ - فالمطلقةُ الضَّروريَّة؛ نحو: «كل جرم حادث بالضرورة». ٢ - والمشروطة العامة : ما قيدت ضرورتها بوصف الموضوع من غير تعرض لنفي الدوام عند مفارقة ذلك الوصف؛ نحو: كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع بالضَّرورة ما دام كاتباً»، سميت : «مشروطة» ؛ لأنَّا شرطنا ضروريَّتها بدوام العنوان للذات، وعامَّةٌ ؛ لأنَّها أعمُّ مِن أختها التي بعدها . ٣ - والمشروطة الخاصة مثلها، لكن مع التَّعرُّض لنفي الدوام عند مفارقة الوصف؛ نحو: كلُّ كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً». ٤ - والوقتيَّة ؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان متحرّك الأصابع بالضَّرورة وقت الكتابة»، سميت : «وقتيَّةٌ لتقييدها بالوقت، ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييدها بما يأتي. ٥ - والوقتية غير الموصوفة بإطلاق مثلها، لكن مع التعرض لنفي الدوام. ٦ - والمنتشرة المطلقة كالوقتيَّة إلَّا أنَّ الوقت فيها غير معيَّن؛ نحو: كلُّ ممكن معدوم بالضرورة وقتاً ما» . ٧ - والمنتشرة غير الموصوفة بإطلاق مثلها، لكن مع التقييد، فهذه الضروريات السبع؛ الأولى مطلقة والمشروطتان مقيَّدتان بدوام الوصف والوقتيَّتان بوقت معيَّن، والمنتشرتان بوقت مبهم .
1. The absolute necessary: e.g., 'Every body is temporally originated necessarily.' 2. The general conditional: its necessity is restricted by the description of the subject without addressing negation of perpetuity when that description is left; e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing.' It is called 'conditional' because we stipulated its necessity with the continuation of the title for the essence, and 'general' because it is more general than its sister that follows. 3. The specific conditional: like it, but with addressing the negation of perpetuity when the description is left; e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is writing, not perpetually.' 4. The temporal: e.g., 'Every human moves his fingers necessarily at the time of writing.' It is called 'temporal' for being restricted by time, and 'absolute' for not being restricted by what follows. 5. The temporal non-described with absoluteness: similar, but with addressing negation of perpetuity. 6. The general spreading absolute: like the temporal except that the time is unspecified; e.g., 'Every possible being is non-existent necessarily at some time.' 7. The spreading non-described with absoluteness: similar, but with restriction. So these are the seven necessary: the first is absolute; the two conditionals are restricted by the continuation of the description; the two temporals by a specific time; the two spreading by an indefinite time.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the types of necessary propositions. The author lists seven examples, explaining the different qualifications: unconditional necessity (absolute necessary), necessity conditional on an attribute (general and specific conditional), necessity at a specific time (temporal and its variant), and necessity at an indefinite time (spreading and its variant). For example, 'Every body is temporally originated necessarily' is an absolute necessary proposition because it holds without any condition. The conditional propositions require a condition such as 'as long as he is writing', with the specific one also noting that it is not perpetual. The temporal ones specify a time like 'at the time of writing', while the spreading ones use an indefinite time like 'at some time'. This detailed classification allows for precise analysis of modal statements in argumentation.

مشروطة عامة
مشروطة خاصة
وقتية
منتشرة

The seven necessary propositions allow precise modal qualification in logical statements, distinguishing absolute, conditional, temporal, and spreading necessity.

Examples of the Three Perpetual Propositions
Author provides examples for perpetual propositions: absolute, general conventional, and specific conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of perpetual propositions (absolute, general conventional, specific conventional) based on their definitions and examples.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions can be absolute or conventional; conventional ones rely on custom, not rational necessity.

This lesson introduces the three types of perpetual propositions through concrete examples. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating modal claims in Islamic logic, especially in Kalam arguments about perpetuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute perpetual
The absolute perpetual (دائمة مطلقة) asserts uninterrupted continuity without any condition. Example: 'Every sphere is moving perpetually' — a claim about the celestial model of the time.
No condition or custom is invoked.
2
Understand the conventional perpetuals
Conventional perpetuals (عرفية) rely on custom or habit, not logical necessity. They are true as long as a certain description holds. Example: 'Every eater moves his lower jaw as long as he is eating' — true by habit, not necessity.
Even if reason does not necessitate perpetuity, custom does.
3
Distinguish general from specific conventional
The general conventional (عرفية عامة) is broader: it does not address negation of perpetuity when the description ends. The specific conventional (عرفية خاصة) adds a restriction, similar to the necessary distinction.
The specific conventional is more restrictive in its scope.
4
Notice the parallel with necessary propositions
The author points out that removing 'necessarily' from the three necessary examples yields the three perpetual examples. This shows a structural link between modal categories.
Helps memorize the types by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدوائم : ١ - فالدائمة المطلقة ؛ نحو: «كل فلك متحرك دائماً». ٢ - والعرفية العامة : «ما قيد دوامها بوصف الموضوع من غير تعرض لنفي الدوام عند مفارقة ذلك الوصف»؛ نحو: كلُّ آكل متحرك الفكِّ الأسفل ما دام أكلاً»، سميت: «عرفيَّة؛ للاكتفاء في دوام نسبتها بالعرف والعادة، وإن لم يقتض العقل دوامها، كما في المثال المذكور، وعامَّةٌ ; لأَنَّها أَعمُّ مِنَ الَّتي بعدها . ٣ - والعرفيَّةُ الخاصة مثلها مع التّقييد ; فأمثلةُ الضَّروريّات الثَّلاث الأُوَل إن حذف منها لفظ الضرورة ، صارت أمثلة للدوائم الثلاث على الترتيب. فهذه الدَّوائم الثَّلاث : الأولى مطلقة، والعرفيَّتان مقيَّدتان بدوام الوصف.
As for the perpetuals: 1. The absolute perpetual: e.g., 'Every sphere is moving perpetually.' 2. The general conventional: its perpetuity is restricted by the description of the subject without addressing negation of perpetuity when that description is left; e.g., 'Every eater moves his lower jaw as long as he is eating.' It is called 'conventional' because sufficiency in the perpetuity of its relation is by custom and habit, even if reason does not necessitate its perpetuity, as in the example; and 'general' because it is more general than the one after. 3. The specific conventional: like it but with restriction. So the examples of the first three necessary, if the word 'necessity' is removed from them, become examples of the three perpetuals in order. So these are the three perpetuals: the first is absolute, and the two conventionals are restricted by the perpetuity of the description.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage explains perpetual propositions. The absolute perpetual is exemplified by 'Every sphere is moving perpetually' — a claim of continuous motion based on the then-accepted cosmological model. The conventional perpetuals are based on habit or custom, such as 'Every eater moves his lower jaw as long as he is eating', which holds as long as the person eats but is not logically necessary. The distinction between general and specific is similar to that in necessary propositions: the specific conventional also addresses negation of perpetuity when the description ends. Notably, the author points out that if you remove the word 'necessarily' from the first three necessary examples, they become the three perpetual examples, showing a structural parallel between the two modal categories.

عرفية عامة
عرفية خاصة

The Two Types of Absolute Propositions in the Specific Sense
Author provides examples of absolute propositions: general absolute and existential non-necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general absolute and the existential non-necessary proposition and identify their characteristics from examples.

Core Idea
General absolute asserts actuality without modality; existential non-necessity explicitly denies necessity.

Within the 19-proposition modal logic, the 'absolute' propositions are a specific class. This lesson clarifies two types: the general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) and the existential non-necessary (wujūdiyya lā ḍarūriyya). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for classifying propositions in Islamic logical arguments, especially in theological contexts where modal status affects validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Look at the Arabic: 'المطلقة العامة' and 'المطلقة الوجودية اللا ضرورية'. Note the key terms for general absolute and existential non-necessary.
These are subtypes of 'absolute in the specific sense'.
2
Understand the definitions
The general absolute asserts the relation without any modal or temporal restriction. The existential non-necessary asserts actuality while explicitly negating necessity.
The negation of necessity is what distinguishes it from the general absolute.
3
Analyze the examples
Compare 'Every human is mortal with general absoluteness' and 'Every human is mortal, not necessarily'. The first does not comment on necessity; the second explicitly denies it.
In Islamic theology, mortality is necessary, but logically it is treated as existential non-necessary to highlight the modal distinction.
4
Apply to new examples
Practice by classifying other statements: e.g., 'Every fire is hot' (general absolute) vs 'Every scholar is knowledgeable, not necessarily' (existential non-necessary).
Context matters: in logic, necessity is strict logical necessity, not physical or theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المطلقات : ١ - فالمطلقة العامة؛ نحو: «كل إنسان ميت بالإطلاق العام». ٢ - والمطلقة الوجوديَّة اللا ضروريَّة وهي : المحكوم فيها بفعليَّة النسبة مع التقييد بنفي الضرورة ؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان فهو ميت لا بالضَّرورة»، سمّيت : «وجوديَّة؛ لوجود نسبتها بالفعل، و: «لا ضروريَّة؛ لتقييدها بنفي الضرورة.
As for the absolutes: 1. The general absolute: e.g., 'Every human is mortal with the general absoluteness.' 2. The existential non-necessary: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is asserted with restriction by negation of necessity; e.g., 'Every human is mortal, not necessarily.' It is called 'existential' for the existence of its relation actually, and 'non-necessary' for its restriction by negation of necessity.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage covers the absolute propositions in the specific sense (muṭlaqa bi al-maʿnā al-akhṣ) within the 19-proposition framework. The first is the general absolute, which asserts the relation without any temporal or modal restriction, exemplified by 'Every human is mortal' — this is true for all humans generally but not necessarily in the logical sense. The second is the existential non-necessary, which asserts that the relation actually holds while denying that it is necessary. For instance, 'Every human is mortal, not necessarily' means that mortality is actual but not a necessary truth in the modal sense (since it is contingent? Actually in Islamic theology, mortality is necessary for humans, but here the logical classification distinguishes between necessity and actuality). This type of proposition is important in modal logic for statements that are actual but not necessary.

Key Terms
مطلقة عامة (muṭlaqa ʿāmma): A general absolute proposition that asserts the relation without any modal or temporal qualification, simply as actual. [Note: In earlier logic, 'absolute' often meant unconditional; here it is a specific modal type within the 19.]
وجودية لا ضرورية (wujūdiyya lā ḍarūriyya): An existential non-necessary proposition that asserts the actuality of the relation but explicitly denies its necessity.

Key Takeaway

Classification of the Five Possible Propositions
Author defines the five types of possible propositions and distinguishes their restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of possible propositions based on their restrictions, and classify any given modal proposition according to this scheme.

Core Idea
The possible proposition is divided by its restriction: absolute vs. restricted in specific possibility, time, perpetuity, or description.

In this passage, the author defines five distinct types of possible propositions, moving from the most general to progressively more restricted forms. Understanding these restrictions is essential for correctly interpreting modal statements in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where the modality of a claim often determines its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general possible (mumkina 'āmma)
This is the broadest category: a proposition whose relation is not impossible. It includes both necessary and contingent statements. Example: 'Every human is an animal with general possibility.' Note that it is absolute, i.e., not restricted.
2
Distinguish the specific possible (mumkina khāṣṣa)
This adds the condition that the relation is neither necessary nor impossible; it must be equally possible on both sides. Example: 'Every body is moving with specific possibility.' This excludes necessary truths, unlike the general.
The specific possible is also called the contingent.
3
Recognize the temporal possible (mumkina waqtiyya)
Possibility is restricted to a specific time. Example: 'Every human is dead with general possibility at the time of death.' The possibility is only valid at that time.
4
Understand the temporal possible (hiniyya) restricted to the subject's description
Possibility is tied to the time when the subject possesses a certain description. Example: 'Every eater is hungry with possibility when he is eating.'
The condition is the subject's description, not an absolute time.
5
Identify the perpetual possible (mumkina dā'ima)
Possibility is judged to be perpetual, i.e., always possible. Example: 'Every possible non-existent is non-existent with perpetual possibility.'
This means the non-existence of a possible being is always possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الممكنات : ۱ - فالممكنة العامة؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسانٍ حَيَوَان بالإمكان العام ؛ لأنَّ المراد بها ما نسبتها غير ممتنعة . ٢ - والممكنة الخاصة : ما حكم فيها بأنَّ نسبتها غير ممتنعة ولا ضرورية، بل جائزة مستوية الطَّرفين»؛ نحو: كلُّ جرم متحرك بالإمكان الخاص»، سمّيت : «ممكنة»؛ لأنَّ جهتها إمكان، و «خاصَّةٌ» ؛ لاختصاصها بمستوي الطرفين، فلا تصدق بالضَّرورة، بخلاف العامة. ٣ - والممكنة الوقتيّة هي: التي قيَّد إمكانها بوقت معيَّن»؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ميت بالإمكان العام وقت مفارقة الرُّوح له سمّيت ممكنةً» لما سبق، ووقتيَّةٌ»؛ لتقييد جهة إمكانها بوقت معيَّن . ٤ - والممكنة الحينيَّة وهي : التي قيَّد إمكانها بحين وصف الموضوع»؛ نحو: «كل آكل جائع بالإمكان حين هو آكل». ه - والممكنة الدائمة: «الَّتي حكم فيها بدوام الإمكان»؛ نحو: كلُّ ممكن معدوم بالإمكان دائماً . فهذه الممكنات الخمس ؛ الأولى منهنَّ مطلقة، والأربع بعدها مقيَّدة؛ إما بخصوص الإمكان، أو بوقت معيَّن أو بالدوام، أو بحين وصف الموضوع.
As for the possible propositions: 1. The general possible; example: 'Every human is an animal with general possibility' because by it is meant what is not impossible. 2. The specific possible: that in which it is judged that its relation is neither impossible nor necessary, but permissible equally on both sides; example: 'Every body is moving with specific possibility'; it is called 'possible' because its modality is possibility, and 'specific' because it is specific to equal possibility on both sides, so it is not true with necessity, unlike the general. 3. The temporal possible: that whose possibility is restricted to a specific time; example: 'Every human is dead with general possibility at the time of the soul's departure from him'; called 'possible' for the reason mentioned, and 'temporal' because its possibility modality is restricted to a specific time. 4. The temporal possible (hiniyya): that whose possibility is restricted to the time of the subject's description; example: 'Every eater is hungry with possibility when he is eating'. 5. The perpetual possible: that in which possibility is judged to be perpetual; example: 'Every possible non-existent is non-existent with perpetual possibility'. So these are the five possible propositions; the first of them is absolute, and the four after are restricted: either by specific possibility, or by a specific time, or by perpetuity, or by the time of subject's description.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible (mumkina) propositions into five types. It is a definitional passage typical of a commentary. The first type is the general possible (mumkina 'āmma), which simply means the relation is not impossible, as in 'Every human is an animal with general possibility'—that is, it is not impossible for a human to be an animal. The second type is the specific possible (mumkina khāṣṣa), which adds that the relation is neither necessary nor impossible, but equally possible on both sides; thus it excludes necessary truths. For example, 'Every body is moving with specific possibility' means it is equally possible for a body to be moving or not. The third type is the temporal possible (mumkina waqtiyya), where the possibility is restricted to a specific time, such as 'Every human is dead with general possibility at the time of death'. The fourth type is the temporal possible that is restricted to the time of the subject's description (mumkina ḥīniyya), like 'Every eater is hungry with possibility when he is eating'. The fifth is the perpetual possible (mumkina dā'ima), where possibility is judged to be perpetual, e.g., 'Every possible non-existent is non-existent with perpetual possibility', meaning it is always possible for a non-existent to remain non-existent. The author then summarizes: the first is absolute (unrestricted), and the four after are restricted—either by specific possibility, by a specific time, by perpetuity, or by the time of the subject's description.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة (al-mumkina al-‘āmma): A possible proposition where the relation is not impossible; it includes both necessary and contingent relations. [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
الممكنة الخاصة (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa): A possible proposition where the relation is neither necessary nor impossible, i.e., equally possible on both sides. [Note: This is the standard definition of 'contingent' in Aristotelian logic.]
الممكنة الوقتيّة (al-mumkina al-waqtiyya): A possible proposition restricted to a specific time; the possibility is only valid at that time. [Note: Some later logicians may include this under 'temporal modalities'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the specific possible (mumkina khāṣṣa)?
A) Every human is an animal necessarily.
B) Every body is either moving or at rest.
C) Every body is moving with specific possibility.
D) Every human is dead with general possibility at death.

Key Takeaway
The possible proposition is classified into five types based on the restriction on its possibility: general (absolute), specific (contingent), temporal (time-bound), temporal by description (hiniyya), and perpetual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Given the proposition: 'Every scholar is learning when he is a student', determine which type of temporal possible it is and justify.

Simple and Compound Modal Propositions
Author defines simple/compound and explains modified rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given modal proposition as simple or compound and identify the modified predicate when specified.

Core Idea
Compound modal propositions combine a positive and negative judgment; 'not permanently' is the key signal.

Modal propositions carry necessity, possibility, or other modalities. But a single proposition can sometimes contain two judgments, one positive and one negative. This lesson shows you how to spot such compound propositions and why the rule of 'modified' focuses on the predicate, not the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal operator
Look for terms like 'necessarily', 'absolutely', 'not permanently', 'not necessarily', 'specific possibility'. Note the adverb or phrase that specifies the modality.
2
Check for compound indicators
If the proposition includes 'not permanently' or 'not necessarily' or 'specific possibility', it is compound. Otherwise, it is simple.
The rule is from Imam Ibn Marzuq's verse.
3
Separate the two judgments
In a compound, the positive judgment is the part before the restricting phrase; the negative judgment is the meaning of the restriction. For example, 'Every human dies absolutely, not permanently' contains 'Every human dies' (positive) and 'No human dies permanently' (negative).
The negative is often equivalent to a universal negation.
4
Handle modified (ma'dula) propositions
If a proposition is called 'modified' without further specification, it means the predicate is modified (e.g., 'man is non-seeing'). Modification of the subject does not affect meaning because subjects refer to individuals; predicates refer to concepts.
This explains why 'modified' usually refers to predicate modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها وَالمَعْدُولَةُ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ تُصْرَفُ لِمَعْدُولَةِ المَحْمُولِ . قصارة. ثمَّ إنَّ هذه القضايا الموجهة تنقسم إلى : ۱ - بسيطة وهي: «المحكوم فيها بحكم واحدٍ إيجاباً أو سلباً»؛ نحو: «كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة ، و لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة. ٢ - والمركبة هي : المحكوم فيها بحكمين إيجابي وسلبي»؛ سميت: «مركبة»، لأن معناها مركَبُ مِن معنى قضيَّتين موجبةٌ وسالبة؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ميت بالإطلاق لا دائماً، فهذه اشتملت على قضيَّتين :إحداهما موجبة وهي ما قبل قولنا : لا دائماً، والأخرى سالبة وهي المفهومة من قولنا : لا دائماً» ؛ لأنَّ معناه أنَّ الموت لا يدوم للإنسان، وإنما ينتفي دوامه إذا انتفى الموت عنه بالفعل يوماً ما، فصار قولنا : لا دائماً» في قوَّة قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بميت بالإطلاق العام، فتكون هذه المركبة وهي الوجوديَّة اللا دائمة مركبة من مطلقتين عامتين موجبة وسالبة . : والضَّابط لمعرفة المركَّب والبسيط أنَّ ما اشتمل مِنَ الموجّهات على التقييد بلا دائماً أو لا بالضَّرورة أو على الإمكان الخاص فهو مركب وما سوى ذلك بسيط، كما أفاده الإمام ابن مرزوق في نظم «الجمل»، بقوله : " وَمَا حَوَى مِنَ القَضَايَا لَا كَذَا أَوْ خَاصِ إِمْكَانٍ مُرَكَبٍ خُذَا وَمَا عَرَى عَنْ ذَيْنِ بِالبَـسِـيـط فَادْعُ لِمَنْ قَرَّبَ يَا نَشِيط و خاص في كلامه بتخفيف الصَّاد؛ للوزن مضاف إلى إمكان) إضافة وصف لموصوفه، و: «عرى من باب «فَرَح»، فالمركَّباتُ حينئذٍ مِنَ الموجهات التسع عشرة سبع . قوله في التنبيه الثالث : (وَالمَعْدُولَةُ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ ... إلخ) وجه ذلك كما قال السعد : أنَّ العدول في الموضوع ممَّا لا أثر له في المعنى؛ لأنَّ المراد مِنَ الموضوع ما صدق عليه؛ أي: الأفراد سواء عبّر عنه بما فيه عدول، أو تحصيل بخلاف المحمول، فإنَّ المراد منه المفهوم، فيختلف بالتحصيل والعدول ؛ لأنَّ الحكم على الشَّيء بالأمور الوجودية يخالف الحكم عليه بالأمور العدمية.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. The modified (ma'dula) when unrestricted is interpreted as modified predicate. Say: then these modal propositions are divided into: 1. Simple: that in which a single judgment is made, positive or negative; example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', and 'No human is a stone necessarily'. 2. Compound: that in which two judgments are made, positive and negative; it is called 'compound' because its meaning is composed of the meaning of two propositions, one positive and one negative; example: 'Every human dies absolutely, not permanently'. This includes two propositions: one positive which is before our saying 'not permanently', and the other negative which is understood from our saying 'not permanently' because its meaning is that death does not persist for humans, but its persistence is negated when death actually ceases from him one day, so our saying 'not permanently' becomes in force like our saying 'No human is dead with absolute generality'. Thus this compound, which is the existential non-permanent, is composed of two general absolute propositions, one positive and one negative. And the rule for identifying compound and simple is: whatever of the modal propositions includes restriction by 'not permanently' or 'not necessarily' or specific possibility is compound, and whatever is free from that is simple. As stated by Imam Ibn Marzuq in the versification of al-Jumal, saying: 'And whatever proposition contains "not so" or "specific possibility", take it as compound; and whatever is free from these two, call it simple, O active one.' And 'خاص' in his speech is with a lightened ص for the meter, added to 'إمكان' as an adjective to its described. And 'عرى' is from the paradigm of 'فرح'. So the compounds among the nineteen modal propositions are seven. His statement in the third notice: ('The modified when unrestricted...') The reason for that is as al-Sa'd said: that modification in the subject has no effect on the meaning because what is intended by the subject is that to which it applies, i.e., individuals, whether expressed with modification or without, unlike the predicate, because what is intended is the concept, so it differs whether it is actualized or modified; because judging something by positive attributes is different from judging it by negative attributes.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is from a chapter on propositions and their rulings. It is a definitional and rule-giving passage. The author first states a principle: when the term 'modified' (ma'dula) is used without specification, it refers to modification of the predicate, not the subject. Then he divides modal propositions into simple and compound. Simple propositions involve a single judgment, either positive or negative, such as 'Every human is an animal necessarily' or 'No human is a stone necessarily'. Compound propositions contain both a positive and a negative judgment combined, such as 'Every human dies absolutely, not permanently'. This example contains the positive judgment 'Every human dies' and the negative judgment 'No human dies permanently', which is understood from 'not permanently'. The author explains that 'not permanently' is equivalent to 'No human is dead with absolute generality' (i.e., not at all times). Thus this compound proposition is composed of two general absolute propositions: one positive and one negative. The author then provides a rule (ḍābiṭ) to identify compound propositions: any modal that includes the restriction 'not permanently', 'not necessarily', or 'specific possibility' is compound; otherwise it is simple. He quotes a versification by Imam Ibn Marzūq as a mnemonic. The verse says: 'Whatever proposition contains "not so" (lā ka-dhā) or "specific possibility" (khāṣṣ imkān), take it as compound; and whatever is free from these two, call it simple.' The author clarifies the meter and morphology. He notes that among the nineteen modal propositions, seven are compound. Finally, he returns to the initial statement about 'modified' (the 'third notice'), explaining that modification of the subject does not affect meaning because the subject refers to individuals (the external referents), whereas modification of the predicate does matter because the predicate is a concept—judging by positive attributes differs from judging by negative attributes.

Definition and Classification of Ma'dula and Muhassala
Ibn 'Arafa defines al-ma'dula, then the commentator distinguishes between negative muhassala and affirmative ma'dula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is ma'dula or muhassala based on the position of the negation particle.

Core Idea
The negation particle defines the type: inside the term gives ma'dula, outside gives muhassala.

In this lesson, we unpack Ibn 'Arafa's concise definition of al-ma'dula and al-muhassala. The key is to locate the negation particle: if it is part of the subject or predicate, the proposition is ma'dula; otherwise it is muhassala. Once you master this distinction, you can correctly classify the four types of categorical propositions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
In the proposition, find the word 'لا' or 'غير' etc. Determine whether it is attached to the subject or predicate (e.g., 'لا عالم' in the predicate) or functions as a separate particle negating the copula.
2
Check if the negation is part of a term
If the negation is inseparable from the term (e.g., 'Zayd is non-knowing'), then the proposition is ma'dula. If the negation stands alone and negates the attribution (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowing'), it is muhassala.
3
Apply to the fourfold division
Recognize that ma'dula has three subtypes: ma'dula subject, ma'dula predicate, and ma'dula both. Muhassala is the fourth type. The commentator notes these are distinct from another set of four divisions coming later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ : وَالمَعْدُولَةُ مَا السَّلْبُ جُزْءٌ طَرَفِهَا، وَغَيْرُهَا مُحَصَّلَةٌ، فَالأَقْسَامُ أَرْبَعَةٌ، وَالمُعْتَبَرُ حَالُ المَحْمُولِ . اهـ .
سُمِّيَتْ : مَعْدُولَةٌ» ؛ لِأَنَّ حَرْفَ السَّلْبِ فِيهَا قَدْ عُدِلَ بِهِ عَمَّا هُوَ الْأَصْلُ فِيهِ، مِنْ كَوْنِهِ دَالَّا عَلَى سَلْبٍ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ عَنِ الْآخَرِ .
وَكُلٌّ مِنَ المَعْدُولَةِ وَالمُحَصِّلَةِ : إِمَّا مُوجَبَةٌ ، أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ فَالأَقْسَامُ أَرْبَعَةٌ، وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَهَا ظَاهِرُ، وَإِنَّمَا يَقَعُ الالْتِبَاسُ بَيْنَ المُحَصِّلَةِ السَّالِبَةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِعَالِم، وَبَيْنَ المَعْدُولَةِ المَوْجَبَةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا فِيهَا سَلْبٌ وَاحِدٌ . »؛
وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَهَا بِأَنْ قَالُوا : إِنَّ الصَّالِبَةَ المُحَصِّلَةَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ المُوْجَبَةِ المَعْدُولَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ كُلَّمَا ثَبَتَ أَنَّ زَيْداً مَوْصُوفٌ بِغَيْرِ العِلْمِ، وَهُوَ مَعْنَى المَعْدُولَةِ، ثَبَتَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مَوْصُوفاً بِالعِلْم،
وَهُوَ مَعْنَى السَّالِبَةِ.
قوله : (فَالأَقْسَامُ أَرْبَعَةٌ) مرادُهُ بـ «الأقسام هنا : معدولة الموضوع، ومعدولة المحمول، ومعدولة الطرفين والمحصلة فهي غير الأقسام الأربعة الآتية بعد. قوله : (لِأَنَّ حَرْفَ السَّلْبِ فِيهَا قَدْ عُدِلَ ...) إلخ بيانُهُ : أنَّ حرف السَّلب إنَّما وضع لرفع النسبة ؛ أي سلب الحكم ،ورفعه، ولا دخل له في حمل ولا وضع، فإذا جعل مع غيره بمنزلة شيء واحد محمولاً ؛ نحو: زيد لا عالم»، أو موضوعاً؛ نحو: «لا حيوان جماد»، فقد عدل به عن موضوعه الأصلي.
قوله : (وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَهَا بِأَنْ قَالُوا : إِنَّ السَّالِيَةَ ...) إلخ هذا الذي أشار له هذا الشارح هو من جهة المصدوق، واقتصر عليه ؛ لأنَّ به يظهر الأثر خارجاً، وإلا فقد فرَّقُوا بينهما من جهة المفهوم، والمصدوق، والمادة، واللفظ :
- أما الفرقُ من جهة المفهوم فهو : أنَّ الحكم في الموجبة المعدولة بالإيقاع، وفي السالبة المحصلة بالانتزاع؛ فـ زيد لا عالم حكم بإيقاع نسبة لا «عالم» على «زید»، و: «زيد ليس هو بعالم نزعنا نسبة «لا عالم عنه.
- وأما من جهة الماصدق، فهو ما أشار له الشارح.
- وأما من جهة المادة، فإنَّ حرف السَّلب جزءٌ مِنَ المحمول في المعدولة، وخارج عنه رافع له في السالبة.
- وأما من جهة اللفظ، فهو : تأخر الرّابطة لفظاً أو نيَّةٌ عنِ السَّلب في السالبة، وتقدمها كذلك عليه في الموجبة ؛ لأنَّ شأن الرابطة أن تربط ما بعدها بما قبلها ؛ إيجاباً كان أو سلباً، فإن تأخر عنها حرف السلب صار مربوطاً بها ومحكوماً به مع ما انضاف إليه على ما قبلها، وخرج عن أن يكون سالباً لشيء، فكانت القضيَّة موجبة، وإن تأخرت عن حرف السلب سلبها، فكانت القضيَّة سالبة؛ لأنَّ الرَّبط فيها مسلوبٌ .
Ibn 'Arafa said: 'Al-ma'dula is that in which negation is part of one of its terms; other than it is muhassala. So the divisions are four, and the consideration is the state of the predicate.' It was called 'ma'dula' because the particle of negation in it has been diverted from what is its original function, which is indicating the negation of one of the two terms from the other. And each of al-ma'dula and al-muhassala is either affirmative or negative, so the divisions are four, and the difference between them is apparent. Only confusion occurs between the negative muhassala, such as 'Zayd is not knowing', and the affirmative ma'dula, such as 'Zayd is non-knowing', because each of them contains a single negation. And they distinguished between them by saying: Indeed, the negative muhassala is more general than the affirmative ma'dula, because whenever it is established that Zayd is described by non-knowledge, which is the meaning of al-ma'dula, it is established that he is not described by knowledge, which is the meaning of the negative. His saying: 'So the divisions are four' - he means by 'divisions' here: ma'dula subject, ma'dula predicate, ma'dula both, and muhassala; these are other than the four divisions coming later. His saying: 'Because the particle of negation in it has been diverted...' its explanation: The particle of negation was only placed to remove the relation, i.e., to negate the judgment and raise it, and it has no role in attribution or positing. So when it is made together with another as a single thing, as a predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is not-knowing', or as a subject, e.g., 'non-animal is inanimate', then it has been diverted from its original function. His saying: 'And they distinguished between them by saying: Indeed, the negative...' this which the commentator pointed to is from the aspect of extension, and he limited himself to it because through it the effect appears externally. Otherwise, they distinguished between them from the aspect of concept, extension, matter, and wording: - As for the distinction from the aspect of concept: the judgment in the affirmative ma'dula is by way of positing, and in the negative muhassala by way of withdrawal. For 'Zayd is non-knowing' is a judgment by positing the relation of 'non-knowing' to Zayd, and 'Zayd is not knowing' we withdraw the relation of 'non-knowing' from him. - And as for the aspect of extension: it is what the commentator indicated. - And as for the aspect of matter: the particle of negation is part of the predicate in the ma'dula, and outside it, being removed from it, in the negative. - And as for the aspect of wording: the copula, in wording or intention, is postponed from the negation in the negative, and precedes it similarly in the affirmative. Because the function of the copula is to connect what is after it to what is before it, whether affirmative or negative. If the particle of negation is postponed from it, it becomes connected to it and judged by it together with what is joined to it upon what is before it, and it ceases to be a negator of something, so the proposition becomes affirmative. And if it is postponed from the particle of negation, it negates it, so the proposition becomes negative, because the connection in it is negated.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson on the types of categorical propositions, specifically distinguishing between 'ma'dula' (qualified) and 'muhassala' (unqualified) propositions. It opens with Ibn 'Arafa's technical definition: al-ma'dula is a proposition where the negation particle is part of one of its terms (subject or predicate), while anything else is muhassala. The commentator then explains that the divisions are four: ma'dula subject, ma'dula predicate, ma'dula both, and muhassala. The name 'ma'dula' is derived from 'adala' (to divert), because the negation particle is diverted from its original function of negating the relation to become part of the term. The key conceptual difficulty is the confusion between the negative muhassala (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowing') and the affirmative ma'dula (e.g., 'Zayd is non-knowing'), because both contain a single negation. The commentator then presents a detailed distinction between them from four aspects: concept, extension, matter, and wording. From the aspect of concept, the affirmative ma'dula posits the relation of 'non-knowing' to Zayd, while the negative muhassala withdraws that relation. From extension, as indicated earlier, the negative muhassala is more general (it is true when Zayd does not exist, whereas the affirmative ma'dula requires existence). From matter, the negation is part of the predicate in ma'dula, but external and removing it in the negative. From wording, the copula precedes the negation in the affirmative ma'dula, but follows it in the negative muhassala, affecting the logical form. This fourfold distinction is crucial for avoiding logical errors in propositional analysis.

المحصلة
السالبة المحصلة
الموجبة المعدولة

D) The subject is negated.

Key Takeaway
The defining feature of a ma'dula proposition is that the negation particle is integrated into the term itself, not operating on the relation.

Fourfold Distinction: Negative Muhassala vs Affirmative Ma'dula
Ibn 'Arafa defines al-ma'dula, then the commentator distinguishes between negative muhassala and affirmative ma'dula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a negative muhassala from an affirmative ma'dula using the four aspects: concept, extension, matter, and wording.

Core Idea
Same negation, different logical structure: inside the predicate makes it affirmative, outside makes it negative.

The most confusing pair in propositional logic is the negative muhassala ('Zayd is not knowing') and the affirmative ma'dula ('Zayd is non-knowing'). Both contain one negation, but their logical behavior differs. The commentator provides a fourfold distinction that clarifies the difference from concept, extension, matter, and wording. Mastering this distinction prevents errors in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish by concept
In the affirmative ma'dula, the judgment posits (iqa') the relation of 'non-knowing' to Zayd. In the negative muhassala, the judgment withdraws (intiza') that relation. So one affirms a privative attribute, the other denies a positive attribute.
2
Distinguish by extension
The negative muhassala is more general: it is true when Zayd does not exist, whereas the affirmative ma'dula requires Zayd's existence to be meaningful. For example, 'Zayd is not knowing' is true if Zayd does not exist, but 'Zayd is non-knowing' presupposes Zayd exists.
3
Distinguish by matter
In the affirmative ma'dula, the negation particle is part of the predicate (e.g., 'lā 'ālim'). In the negative muhassala, the negation is external and removes the predicate.
4
Distinguish by wording
The copula (rābiṭa) precedes the negation in the affirmative ma'dula ('Zayd huwa lā 'ālim'), but follows it in the negative muhassala ('laysa Zayd bi-'ālim'). This word order affects logical form.

قوله : (فَالأَقْسَامُ أَرْبَعَةٌ) مرادُهُ بـ «الأقسام هنا : معدولة الموضوع، ومعدولة المحمول، ومعدولة الطرفين والمحصلة فهي غير الأقسام الأربعة الآتية بعد. قوله : (لِأَنَّ حَرْفَ السَّلْبِ فِيهَا قَدْ عُدِلَ ...) إلخ بيانُهُ : أنَّ حرف السَّلب إنَّما وضع لرفع النسبة ؛ أي سلب الحكم ،ورفعه، ولا دخل له في حمل ولا وضع، فإذا جعل مع غيره بمنزلة شيء واحد محمولاً ؛ نحو: زيد لا عالم»، أو موضوعاً؛ نحو: «لا حيوان جماد»، فقد عدل به عن موضوعه الأصلي.
قوله : (وَفَرَّقُوا بَيْنَهَا بِأَنْ قَالُوا : إِنَّ السَّالِيَةَ ...) إلخ هذا الذي أشار له هذا الشارح هو من جهة المصدوق، واقتصر عليه ؛ لأنَّ به يظهر الأثر خارجاً، وإلا فقد فرَّقُوا بينهما من جهة المفهوم، والمصدوق، والمادة، واللفظ :
- أما الفرقُ من جهة المفهوم فهو : أنَّ الحكم في الموجبة المعدولة بالإيقاع، وفي السالبة المحصلة بالانتزاع؛ فـ زيد لا عالم حكم بإيقاع نسبة لا «عالم» على «زید»، و: «زيد ليس هو بعالم نزعنا نسبة «لا عالم عنه.
- وأما من جهة الماصدق، فهو ما أشار له الشارح.
- وأما من جهة المادة، فإنَّ حرف السَّلب جزءٌ مِنَ المحمول في المعدولة، وخارج عنه رافع له في السالبة.
- وأما من جهة اللفظ، فهو : تأخر الرّابطة لفظاً أو نيَّةٌ عنِ السَّلب في السالبة، وتقدمها كذلك عليه في الموجبة ؛ لأنَّ شأن الرابطة أن تربط ما بعدها بما قبلها ؛ إيجاباً كان أو سلباً، فإن تأخر عنها حرف السلب صار مربوطاً بها ومحكوماً به مع ما انضاف إليه على ما قبلها، وخرج عن أن يكون سالباً لشيء، فكانت القضيَّة موجبة، وإن تأخرت عن حرف السلب سلبها، فكانت القضيَّة سالبة؛ لأنَّ الرَّبط فيها مسلوبٌ .
Ibn 'Arafa said: 'Al-ma'dula is that in which negation is part of one of its terms; other than it is muhassala. So the divisions are four, and the consideration is the state of the predicate.' It was called 'ma'dula' because the particle of negation in it has been diverted from what is its original function, which is indicating the negation of one of the two terms from the other. And each of al-ma'dula and al-muhassala is either affirmative or negative, so the divisions are four, and the difference between them is apparent. Only confusion occurs between the negative muhassala, such as 'Zayd is not knowing', and the affirmative ma'dula, such as 'Zayd is non-knowing', because each of them contains a single negation. And they distinguished between them by saying: Indeed, the negative muhassala is more general than the affirmative ma'dula, because whenever it is established that Zayd is described by non-knowledge, which is the meaning of al-ma'dula, it is established that he is not described by knowledge, which is the meaning of the negative. His saying: 'So the divisions are four' - he means by 'divisions' here: ma'dula subject, ma'dula predicate, ma'dula both, and muhassala; these are other than the four divisions coming later. His saying: 'Because the particle of negation in it has been diverted...' its explanation: The particle of negation was only placed to remove the relation, i.e., to negate the judgment and raise it, and it has no role in attribution or positing. So when it is made together with another as a single thing, as a predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is not-knowing', or as a subject, e.g., 'non-animal is inanimate', then it has been diverted from its original function. His saying: 'And they distinguished between them by saying: Indeed, the negative...' this which the commentator pointed to is from the aspect of extension, and he limited himself to it because through it the effect appears externally. Otherwise, they distinguished between them from the aspect of concept, extension, matter, and wording: - As for the distinction from the aspect of concept: the judgment in the affirmative ma'dula is by way of positing, and in the negative muhassala by way of withdrawal. For 'Zayd is non-knowing' is a judgment by positing the relation of 'non-knowing' to Zayd, and 'Zayd is not knowing' we withdraw the relation of 'non-knowing' from him. - And as for the aspect of extension: it is what the commentator indicated. - And as for the aspect of matter: the particle of negation is part of the predicate in the ma'dula, and outside it, being removed from it, in the negative. - And as for the aspect of wording: the copula, in wording or intention, is postponed from the negation in the negative, and precedes it similarly in the affirmative. Because the function of the copula is to connect what is after it to what is before it, whether affirmative or negative. If the particle of negation is postponed from it, it becomes connected to it and judged by it together with what is joined to it upon what is before it, and it ceases to be a negator of something, so the proposition becomes affirmative. And if it is postponed from the particle of negation, it negates it, so the proposition becomes negative, because the connection in it is negated.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson on the types of categorical propositions, specifically distinguishing between 'ma'dula' (qualified) and 'muhassala' (unqualified) propositions. It opens with Ibn 'Arafa's technical definition: al-ma'dula is a proposition where the negation particle is part of one of its terms (subject or predicate), while anything else is muhassala. The commentator then explains that the divisions are four: ma'dula subject, ma'dula predicate, ma'dula both, and muhassala. The name 'ma'dula' is derived from 'adala' (to divert), because the negation particle is diverted from its original function of negating the relation to become part of the term. The key conceptual difficulty is the confusion between the negative muhassala (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowing') and the affirmative ma'dula (e.g., 'Zayd is non-knowing'), because both contain a single negation. The commentator then presents a detailed distinction between them from four aspects: concept, extension, matter, and wording. From the aspect of concept, the affirmative ma'dula posits the relation of 'non-knowing' to Zayd, while the negative muhassala withdraws that relation. From extension, as indicated earlier, the negative muhassala is more general (it is true when Zayd does not exist, whereas the affirmative ma'dula requires existence). From matter, the negation is part of the predicate in ma'dula, but external and removing it in the negative. From wording, the copula precedes the negation in the affirmative ma'dula, but follows it in the negative muhassala, affecting the logical form. This fourfold distinction is crucial for avoiding logical errors in propositional analysis.

السالبة المحصلة
الموجبة المعدولة
الإيقاع
الانتزاع
الرابطة

A) The affirmative ma'dula negates the predicate; the negative muhassala affirms the predicate.
B) The affirmative ma'dula posits the relation of 'non-knowing'; the negative muhassala withdraws that relation.
C) Both posit the same relation but with different word orders.
D) The affirmative ma'dula uses implicit negation; the negative muhassala uses explicit negation.

Key Takeaway
The fourfold distinction provides a robust framework to tell apart two seemingly identical propositions by examining concept, extension, matter, and wording.

Truth Conditions: Refining the Common View on Negatives
Author refines the common view on truth conditions for negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth conditions of the four proposition types (muhassala and ma'dula, affirmative and negative) and identify when they are equal based on predicate type.

Core Idea
Truth conditions for negatives are not one-size-fits-all: predicate type determines whether non-existence yields truth.

You have likely heard that negative propositions are true when the subject does not exist. But this rule has hidden limits. In this lesson, we will explore a crucial refinement from the tradition: the predicate's nature—existential or privative—changes the picture. You will learn how the affirmative ma'dula and the negative muhassala become equal, and why this matters for logical precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
Recognize the four combinations: affirmative muhassala (Zayd is knowing), affirmative ma'dula (Zayd is non-knowing), negative muhassala (Zayd is not knowing), and negative ma'dula (Zayd is not non-knowing). Note that the predicate in ma'dula is privative (non-knowing) while in muhassala it is existential (knowing).
2
Recall the common rule
The well-known view: negative propositions are true when the subject does not exist, while affirmatives require existence. Apply this to negative muhassala: 'The anqa is not seeing' is true because the anqa does not exist.
The common rule is stated first; the verification will limit it.
3
Apply the refinement for predicate type
According to the verification (tahqiq), the common rule holds only when the predicate is an existential attribute (wasf wujudy). For privative predicates, the affirmative ma'dula is also true in non-existence, making it equal to the negative muhassala—both are true regardless of the subject's existence.
Equality means they share truth values in all situations.
4
Extend to the second pair
Now consider the affirmative muhassala and negative ma'dula. The negative ma'dula (Zayd is not non-knowing) is equivalent to the affirmative muhassala (Zayd is knowing) via double negation. Therefore, they are also equal and both require the subject's existence to be true.
This contrasts with the first pair, which do not require existence.
5
Consider the objection and the broader significance
Al-Tayyib objects that any affirmation of an attribute requires the subject's existence, challenging the idea that affirmative ma'dula could be true without existence. The passage ends with a distinction between mental existence (required for the judgment) and external existence (required for the truth of the attribute). This shows the depth of the discussion.
Understanding these truth conditions is crucial for evaluating logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

وَتَزِيدُ الصَّالِبَةُ بِصِدْقِهَا عِنْدَ عَدَمِ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ ; لِأَنَّ المُوْجَبَةَ تَقْتَضِي وُجُودَ المَوْضُوعِ بِخِلَافِ السَّالِبَةِ ; هَذَا الَّذِي اشْتُهِرَ بَيْنَهُمْ.
وَالتَّحْقِيقُ : أَنَّ ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، بَلْ هُوَ كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ» مُخْتَصُّ بِمَا إِذَا كَانَ المَحْمُولُ وَصْفاً وُجُودِيًا , نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ أَوْ أَبْيَضُ مَثَلاً», وَالمَحْمُولُ في المَعْدُولَةِ عَدَمِيٌّ , وَحِينَئِذٍ فَهِيَ مُسَاوِيَةٌ لِلسَّالِبَةِ المُحَصِّلَةِ لَا عُمُومَ بَيْنَهُمَا , وَكُلُّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا تَصْدُقُ عِنْدَ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوع وَعِنْدَ عَدَمِهِ، كَمَا أَنَّ الحَقِّ فِي المُوْجَبَةِ المُحَصِّلَةِ ; نَحْو: «زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ», وَالصَّالِبَةِ المَعْدُولَةِ [ب/ ٣٤]; نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِلَا عَالِم أَنَّهُمَا مُتَسَاوِيتَانِ أَيْضاً; لِأَنَّ مَدْلُولَ الصَّالِبَةِ نَفْيْ كَوْنِ زَيْدٍ غَيْرَ عَالِم, فَيَجِبُ أَنْ يَثْبُتَ لَهُ نَقِيضُهُ، وَهُوَ كَوْنُهُ عَالِماً، وَإِلَّا ارْتَفَعَ النَّقِيضَانِ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا إِلَّا بِوُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ
لِحُكْمِهِمَا عَلَيْهِ بِوَصْفٍ وُجُودِيٌّ , خِلَافاً لِمَن قَالَ بِعُمُومِ الثَّانِيَةِ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.
قوله : (وَتَزِيدُ الصَّالِبَةُ بِصِدْقِهَا عِنْدَ عَدَمِ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ أي : كما إذا قلت: «العنقاء ليس ببصير، ولا يصحُ: «العنقاء هو ليس ببصير».
قوله : (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ ... إلخ) هو تابع فيه للسنوسي.
واعترضه شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب : بأنَّ التَّحقيق أنَّ ثبوت صفة ما لموضوع ما يقتضي ثبوت ذلك الموضوع; إذ لو لم يكن ثابتاً ما ثبتت له، فالإيجاب يقتضي وجود الموضوع في الذهن مِن حيث إِنَّه حكم، فلا بد من تصور المحكوم عليه، وصدق ثبوت المحمول للموضوع يقتضي وجوده أيضاً; لأنَّ ثبوت المحمول له فرع ثبوته في نفسه. والفَرْقُ بين هذين الوجودين : أنَّ الأوّل الذي يقتضيه الحكم إنَّما يعتبر حال الحكم ;
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. And the negative is more extensive in its truth when the subject does not exist, because the affirmative requires the existence of the subject, unlike the negative. This is what is well-known among them. But the verification is that this is not unrestricted; rather, it is as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar, restricted to when the predicate is an existential attribute, like 'Zayd is knowing' or 'white' for example. And the predicate in al-ma'dula is privative; then it is equal to the negative muhassala, not more general. Each of them is true when the subject exists and when it does not. Likewise, the truth concerning the affirmative muhassala, such as 'Zayd is knowing', and the negative ma'dula, such as 'Zayd is not non-knowing', is that they are also equal, because the meaning of the negative is the negation of Zayd being non-knowing, so its opposite must be established for him, which is his being knowing; otherwise the two opposites would be removed, so it follows that each of them is true only with the existence of the subject, because they both predicate an existential attribute of him, contrary to those who said the second is more general. And Allah knows best. His saying: 'And the negative is more extensive in its truth when the subject does not exist' means as when you say: 'The anqa is not seeing,' and it is not valid: 'The anqa is non-seeing.' His saying: 'But the verification...' he follows [al-Sanusi] in it. My teacher Sayyid al-Tayyib objected to it: That the verification is that the establishment of an attribute for a subject requires the existence of that subject; because if it were not established, it would not be established for it. So affirmation requires the existence of the subject in the mind insofar as it is a judgment, so the subject must be conceived. And the truth of the predicate's existence for the subject also requires its existence, because the predicate's existence for it is a branch of its existence in itself. The difference between these two existences is that the first, which the judgment requires, is considered only at the time of judgment.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage addresses a common claim in logic: that a negative proposition can be true even when the subject does not exist (e.g., 'The anqa is not seeing' is true because the anqa is fictitious), while an affirmative requires the subject's existence. The author first states this well-known view, then presents a verification (tahqiq) that refines it. The verification, following al-Sanusi, restricts the generality: the negative is more extensive only when the predicate is an existential attribute (wasf wujudy). For the affirmative ma'dula (where the predicate is privative, like 'non-knowing'), it is actually equal to the negative muhassala in truth conditions; both are true whether the subject exists or not. Then the author extends this to the pair of affirmative muhassala (e.g., 'Zayd is knowing') and negative ma'dula (e.g., 'Zayd is not non-knowing'), arguing they are also equal, because the negative ma'dula is logically equivalent to the affirmative muhassala (double negation). The reasoning: if 'Zayd is not non-knowing' were true without Zayd's existence, then 'Zayd is knowing' would also have to be true (since they are equivalents), but that would require existence, so consistency demands existence. Finally, an objection by al-Tayyib argues that any affirmation of an attribute requires the subject's existence both mentally and externally, challenging the verification's claim that the affirmative ma'dula can be true without existence. The passage ends mid-objection, noting a distinction between two types of existence: the one required by the judgment (mental) and the one required by the truth of the attribute (external). This is a nuanced discussion of truth conditions for different types of propositions.

Key Terms
الصالبة (al-saliba): negative proposition
الموجبة (al-mujaba): affirmative proposition
وصف وجودي (wasf wujudy): existential attribute
وصف عدمي (wasf 'adami): privative attribute
المساواة (al-musawat): equality in truth conditions

Key Takeaway
The truth conditions of categorical propositions depend on whether the predicate is existential or privative, and the common rule that negatives are true in non-existence only applies to those with existential predicates.

Restricted Advantage of Negative Propositions and Equivalence with Privatives
Author explains the restricted truth advantage of negative propositions and the equivalence of privative and simple negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which a negative proposition has a truth advantage over an affirmative, and explain the equivalence between a privative negative and a simple affirmative in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A negative's truth advantage only applies when the predicate is a positive attribute; with privative predicates, negatives and affirmatives are equivalent.

In classical Islamic logic, it is often said that a negative proposition (al-saliba) is true when the subject does not exist, whereas an affirmative (al-mujaba) requires subject existence. However, this rule is not absolute. The verification (tahqiq) by later logicians restricts this advantage to cases where the predicate is a positive attribute. Moreover, when the predicate is privative (ma'dula), the negative proposition behaves exactly like a simple affirmative—both require subject existence. In this lesson, we examine these refined truth conditions and their implications for logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the famous claim about negatives
Find the sentence beginning with 'وَتَزِيدُ الصَّالِبَةُ'. Note that the claim is that negatives have an advantage in truth when the subject does not exist, because affirmatives require subject existence.
This is the widely held view among logicians.
2
Locate the restriction in the verification
Look for 'وَالتَّحْقِيقُ' and note that the author states this advantage is not absolute; it is restricted to cases where the predicate is a positive attribute (وصف وجودي) like 'knowledgeable' or 'white'.
The restriction is crucial for correct application.
3
Analyze the privative proposition case
The author says that when the predicate is privative (adami), the privative negative (al-ma'dula) is equivalent to the simple negative (al-saliba al-muhassila) and both are true whether the subject exists or not. Contrast this with the restricted advantage earlier.
Both are negative propositions, hence they do not require subject existence.
4
Examine the equivalence between simple affirmative and privative negative
The author argues that 'Zayd is knowledgeable' (simple affirmative) and 'Zayd is not not-knowledgeable' (privative negative) are equivalent. Understand the double negation reasoning: negating the privative entails affirming the positive attribute, so both require subject existence.
This shows that privative negatives can behave like affirmatives when considered under double negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وَتَزِيدُ الصَّالِبَةُ بِصِدْقِهَا عِنْدَ عَدَمِ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ ; لِأَنَّ المُوْجَبَةَ تَقْتَضِي وُجُودَ المَوْضُوعِ بِخِلَافِ السَّالِبَةِ ; هَذَا الَّذِي اشْتُهِرَ بَيْنَهُمْ.
وَالتَّحْقِيقُ : أَنَّ ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى إِطْلَاقِهِ، بَلْ هُوَ كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ» مُخْتَصُّ بِمَا إِذَا كَانَ المَحْمُولُ وَصْفاً وُجُودِيًا، نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ أَوْ أَبْيَضُ مَثَلاً»، وَالمَحْمُولُ في المَعْدُولَةِ عَدَمِيٌّ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَهِيَ مُسَاوِيَةٌ لِلسَّالِبَةِ المُحَصِّلَةِ لَا عُمُومَ بَيْنَهُمَا، وَكُلُّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا تَصْدُقُ عِنْدَ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوع وَعِنْدَ عَدَمِهِ، كَمَا أَنَّ الحَقَّ فِي المُوْجَبَةِ المُحَصِّلَةِ؛ نَحْو: «زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ»، وَالصَّالِبَةِ المَعْدُولَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِلَا عَالِمٍ» أَنَّهُمَا مُتَسَاوِيتَانِ أَيْضاً؛ لِأَنَّ مَدْلُولَ الصَّالِبَةِ نَفْيُ كَوْنِ زَيْدٍ غَيْرَ عَالِمٍ، فَيَجِبُ أَنْ يَثْبُتَ لَهُ نَقِيضُهُ، وَهُوَ كَوْنُهُ عَالِماً، وَإِلَّا ارْتَفَعَ النَّقِيضَانِ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا إِلَّا بِوُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ لِحُكْمِهِمَا عَلَيْهِ بِوَصْفٍ وُجُودِيٍّ، خِلَافاً لِمَنْ قَالَ بِعُمُومِ الثَّانِيَةِ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.
Chapter on propositions and their rulings. And the negative proposition has an advantage in its truth when the subject does not exist, because the affirmative requires the existence of the subject, contrary to the negative. This is what is famous among them. And the verification is that this is not absolute, but rather it is as in the commentary on al-Mukhtasar: it is specific to when the predicate is a positive attribute, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable' or 'white' for example, whereas the predicate in the privative is non-existent. Then it is equivalent to the simple negative; there is no generality between them, and each of them is true when the subject exists and when it does not. Just as the truth regarding the simple affirmative, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable', and the privative negative, like 'Zayd is not not-knowledgeable', is that they are also equivalent, because the meaning of the negative is the negation of Zayd being not-knowledgeable, so its opposite must be established for him, which is his being knowledgeable, otherwise the two contradictories would be removed. Thus it follows that each of them is true only with the existence of the subject, because they judge upon him with a positive attribute, contrary to those who said that the second is general. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage defines and verifies a key logical point about negative propositions. It begins by stating the well-known view that a negative proposition (saliba) has an advantage over an affirmative (mujaba) in being true when the subject does not exist, since affirmative requires subject existence. However, the author then presents a verification (tahqiq) that this is not absolute; it is restricted to cases where the predicate is a positive attribute (wasf wujudi). When the predicate is non-existent (adami), as in a privative proposition (ma'dula), the privative negative is equivalent to the simple negative (saliba muhassila) and both are true whether the subject exists or not. The author further explains that a simple affirmative (muhassila) and a privative negative (saliba ma'dula) are also equivalent: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' means the same as 'Zayd is not not-knowledgeable', because negating the negation of knowledge entails affirming knowledge. This equivalence holds because both judge the subject with a positive attribute, requiring the subject's existence. Thus the claim that the negative has a general truth advantage is refuted; the truth conditions depend on the nature of the predicate.

المعدولة
المحصلة

B) Only when the predicate is a positive attribute (wujudi).
C) Only when the predicate is privative (adami).
D) Only when the predicate is negative.

Key Takeaway
The truth advantage of negative propositions is restricted to positive attribute predicates, and privative negatives are equivalent to simple negatives in truth conditions and to simple affirmatives in meaning.

Distinguishing Privative and Negative Propositions by Matter and Wording
Author differentiates privative from negative propositions by matter and wording, and explains recognition rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is privative (ma'dula) or simple negative (saliba muhassila) by analyzing the position of the negation relative to the copula and its role in the predicate.

Core Idea
Privative and negative differ in logical matter: in privative, negation is part of the predicate; in negative, it removes the predicate.

Understanding the distinction between privative and negative propositions is crucial for precise logical analysis. In Islamic logic, the privative (al-ma'dula) treats negation as part of the predicate, while the simple negative (al-saliba al-muhassila) treats it as external. This passage from Bajuri's commentary on Bannani explains the difference through matter (madda) and wording (lafz), providing a practical rule for recognition based on the position of the copula (rabita).

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural components
Locate the subject, copula (if present), negation particle (e.g., لا, ليس), and predicate in the proposition.
The copula may be explicit like هو or implicit through tarkib.
2
Determine the position of the copula relative to negation
If the copula precedes the negation particle, the negation is part of the predicate, making it privative. If the copula follows the negation, the negation is external, making it negative.
Example: 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable' (privative) vs 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' (negative).
3
Handle cases without an explicit copula
When the copula is absent, decide by intention: if the speaker intends the negation to be part of the predicate (i.e., the negation is annexed to the predicate), it is privative; otherwise it is simple negative.
Al-Sa'd also mentions conventional usage of certain words to indicate the type.
4
Test with an Islamic logical example
Take a proposition from a kalam text, e.g., 'The world is not eternal' (العالم ليس بقديم). Here 'ليس' is both copula and negation? Actually in Arabic, 'ليس' functions as a negative copula. But according to the rule, if the copula (embedded in ليس) precedes the predicate, it's negative? This requires careful parsing. A clearer example: 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable' (زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ) where 'هُوَ' (copula) precedes 'لا' (negation), indicating privative.
Apply the rule consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- وأمَّا من جهة المادة، فإنَّ حرف السَّلب جزءٌ مِنَ المحمول في المعدولة، وخارج عنه رافع له في السالبة.
- وأما من جهة اللفظ، فهو : تأخر الرّابطة لفظاً أو نيَّةٌ عنِ السَّلب في السالبة، وتقدمها كذلك عليه في الموجبة ; لأنَّ شأن الرابطة أن تربط ما بعدها بما قبلها ; إيجاباً كان أو سلباً، فإن تأخر عنها حرف السلب صار مربوطاً بها ومحكوماً به مع ما انضاف إليه على ما قبلها، وخرج عن أن يكون سالباً لشيء، فكانت القضيَّة موجبة، وإن تأخرت عن حرف السلب سلبها، فكانت القضيَّة سالبة; لأنَّ الرَّبط فيها مسلوبٌ .
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ العُدُولَ يُعْرَفُ بِتَقَدُّمِ الرَّابِطَةِ عَلَى حَرْفِ السَّلْبِ؛ نَحْوُ : زَيْدٌ هُوَ لَا عَالِمٌ ، فَإِنْ لَمْ تُذْكَرْ رَابِطَةٌ، وَقَعَ الفَرْقُ بِالنِّيَّةِ ; أَيْ : إِذَا نَوَى كَوْنَهُ جُزْءاً مِنَ المَحْمُولِ بِأَنْ يَنْوِي رَبَّطَ السَّلْبِ فَعُدُولٌ، وَإِلَّا فَتَحْصِيلٌ . قَالَ السَّعْدُ : أَوْ يُعْرَفُ بِأَنْ يُصْطَلَحَ عَلَى تَخْصِيصِ بَعْضِ الأَلْفَاظِ بِالسَّلْبِ وَبَعْضِهَا
- As for the aspect of matter: the negation particle is part of the predicate in the privative, while in the negative it is external to it and removes it.
- As for the aspect of wording: the copula follows the negation in wording or intention in the negative, and precedes it likewise in the affirmative; because the function of the copula is to connect what comes after it with what comes before it, whether positive or negative. If the negation follows after it, it becomes connected to and judged by it along with what is annexed to it on what precedes, and it ceases to be negative for something, so the proposition becomes affirmative. If it precedes the negation, it negates it, so the proposition becomes negative, because the connection in it is negated.
And know that the privative is recognized by the precedence of the copula over the negation particle, like 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable'. If no copula is mentioned, the distinction is by intention: if one intends it to be part of the predicate by intending the negation to be connected, then it is privative; otherwise it is simple (muhassala). Al-Sa'd said: or it is recognized by convention to designate some expressions for negation and others for...
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage provides a clear distinction between privative (ma'dula) and negative (saliba) propositions from two angles. First, from the aspect of matter (al-madda): in the privative, the negation particle is part of the predicate, making it a single term meaning the absence of a quality, whereas in a simple negative, the negation is external and removes the predicate. Second, from the aspect of wording (al-lafz): the position of the copula (rabita) relative to the negation determines the type. In a negative proposition, the copula follows the negation (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' — the copula 'is' is implicit, but when explicit, it comes after 'not'). In a privative, the copula precedes the negation (e.g., 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable'). If no copula is expressed, the intention of the speaker determines whether the negation is part of the predicate or separate. Al-Sa'd adds that conventional usage of certain words can also indicate the distinction. This passage is a parsing exercise: students must learn to identify the type of proposition by analyzing the structure and intention.

Key Terms
العدول
الرابطة
السالبة المحصلة

A) The negation particle is before the copula.
B) The copula precedes the negation particle.
C) There is no copula.
D) The predicate is positive.

Key Takeaway
The privative uses negation as part of the predicate, while the negative uses it externally; the copula's position reveals the type.
