Two Existences in Propositions: Mental vs. Actual
Objection that affirmative requires subject existence, and answer distinguishing mental from external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of existence (mental and actual) required by affirmative and negative propositions, and apply this distinction to evaluate objections about subject existence.

Core Idea
Affirmatives require two existences; negatives require only mental presence.

A common confusion in logic is whether a proposition requires the subject to actually exist. This lesson clarifies a key distinction: every judgment requires the subject to be present in the mind, but only affirmative propositions require the subject to have some form of actual existence (mental or external) corresponding to the predicate's duration. This distinction resolves objections raised by earlier scholars like al-Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two existences
The passage distinguishes between the mental existence that is needed for any judgment (al-wujud al-dhihni al-muqtada bi al-hukm) and the actual existence required by the predicate's establishment (al-wujud al-muqtada bi thubut al-mahmul). Note the difference: the first is a prerequisite for conceiving the subject; the second is a consequence of the predicate being true of it.
The first existence is 'at the moment of judgment', the second is 'according to the predicate's duration'.
2
Apply to affirmative and negative propositions
For an affirmative like 'Zayd is knowledgeable', the subject must exist both mentally (to conceive it) and actually (because knowledge is predicated). For a negative like 'Zayd is not knowledgeable', the subject must exist mentally but need not actually exist—the negation does not require the subject to have any actual attribute. This is why 'The phoenix is not blind' is true as long as the phoenix is conceived, even though it has no external existence.
The negative shares only the first existence with the affirmative.
3
Analyze the privative affirmative
The passage notes that in affirmative privatives (mujaba ma'dula) like 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable' (i.e., ignorant), the predicate is positive (ignorance), so the proposition actually affirms a real attribute. Therefore, it requires actual existence of the subject, just like a regular affirmative. This contrasts with the simple negative (saliba muhassila) where no positive attribute is affirmed.
Al-Sa'd's view in Sharh al-Maqasid supports this: 'not-knowledgeable' equals 'ignorant'.
4
Evaluate al-Sanusi's objection
Al-Sanusi had objected that logicians claim affirmatives require subject existence, which is false for negatives and some affirmatives. The commentator argues al-Sanusi mistakenly thought 'existence' meant external existence only. Once the distinction is made, his objection dissolves because affirmatives require actual existence (mental or external) while negatives require only mental presence.
Al-Sanusi was preceded in this mistake by al-Razi and al-'Uqbani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَتَزِيدُ الصَّالِبَةُ ... إلخ) هو تابع فيه للسنوسي.
واعترضه شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب : بأنَّ التَّحقيق أنَّ ثبوت صفة ما لموضوع ما يقتضي ثبوت ذلك الموضوع؛ إذ لو لم يكن ثابتاً ما ثبتت له، فالإيجاب يقتضي وجود الموضوع في الذهن مِن حيث إِنَّه حكم، فلا بد من تصور المحكوم عليه، وصدق ثبوت المحمول للموضوع يقتضي وجوده أيضاً؛ لأنَّ ثبوت المحمول له فرع ثبوته في نفسه. والفرقُ بين هذين الوجودين : أنَّ الأوّل الذي يقتضيه الحكم إنَّما يعتبر حال الحكم؛ أي : بمقدار ما يحكم الحاكم بالمحمول على الموضوع كلحظة مثلاً، وأما الوجود الذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع فهو بحسب ثبوت ذلك المحمول إن دائماً فدائم، وإن ساعة فساعة، وإن لحظة فلحظة، وإن خارجاً فخارجٌ، وإن ذهناً فذهن. فالسَّالبة تشارك الموجبة في اقتضاء الوجود الأول دون الثَّاني، وليس مراد القوم أنَّ الموجبة تقتضي وجود الموضوع في الخارج حتّى يعترض عليهم : بأنَّ الحقَّ التفصيل في القضايا . والحاصل: أنَّ الحضور في الذهن لا بدَّ منه في كلِّ منهما ; إذ لا يلزم من عدم وجود الشَّيء عدم تعقله للحكم عليه، ولذا أطلقوا في أنَّ الحكم على الشَّيء فرع تصوره، فالمشترك بينهما هو الوجود في الذهن حال الحكم؛ أي وقوعه، وأما الوجود حال اعتبار الحكم فلا تقتضيه إلَّا الموجبة دون السَّالبة، فانتفاء المحمول عن الموضوع لا يقتضي وجوده، وثبوته للموضوع يقتضي وجوده . وكأنَّ الشَّيخ السَّنوسي حيث اعترض كلام القوم فَهِمَ أنَّ مرادهم أنَّ الموجبة تقتضي وجود الموضوع في الخارج، فاعترض عليهم بأنَّ الحقَّ التَّفصيل في القضايا، وقد علمت أنَّ التحقيق خلافه، فلا محلّ للاعتراض، وسبق السَّنوسي لهذا الاعتراض على المناطقة الفخرُ الرَّازِيُّ، وأبو عثمان العقباني. وكلامُ السَّعد في شرح المقاصد» يدلُّ على أنَّ التَّحقيق في الموجبة المعدولة أنَّها تقتضي وجود الموضوع؛ لأنَّ محمولها ثبوتيّ فالمحكوم به في نحو زيد لا عالم هو ثبوت نسبة ضد العلم الذي هو الجهل ، فـ لا عالم في قوَّة : جاهل، بخلاف السَّالبة المحصلة فالمحكوم به فيها هو العلم من غير التفات إلى ثبوت ضده، وهذا هو الحق.
His saying: 'And the negative proposition has an advantage... etc.' He follows al-Sanusi in it. Our master al-Tayyib objected to him: that the verification is that the establishment of some attribute for a subject necessitates the existence of that subject; for if it were not established, it would not be established for it. So affirmation requires the existence of the subject in the mind insofar as it is a judgment, for one must conceive of the subject of judgment. And the truth of the predicate's establishment for the subject also requires its existence, because the predicate's establishment for it is a branch of its own existence. The difference between these two existences is that the first, which the judgment necessitates, is only considered at the moment of judgment, i.e., to the extent that the judge judges the predicate of the subject, like a moment for example. As for the existence that the predicate's establishment for the subject necessitates, it is according to the establishment of that predicate: if permanent then permanent, if temporary then temporary, if external then external, if mental then mental. So the negative shares with the affirmative in requiring the first existence but not the second. It is not the intent of the logicians that the affirmative requires the existence of the subject externally, such that one might object: the truth is a division of propositions. The conclusion is that presence in the mind is necessary in both; for the non-existence of a thing does not entail the impossibility of conceiving it for judgment. Therefore they said that judgment of a thing is a branch of its conception. What is common between them is existence in the mind at the moment of judgment, i.e., its occurrence. As for existence at the time of considering the judgment, only the affirmative requires it, not the negative. So the removal of the predicate from the subject does not require its existence, while its establishment for the subject requires its existence. It appears that al-Sanusi, when he objected to the logicians, understood that they meant the affirmative requires the subject's existence externally, so he objected: the truth is a division of propositions. And you have learned that the verification is contrary to that, so there is no place for objection. Al-Sanusi was preceded in this objection against the logicians by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and Abu 'Uthman al-'Uqbani. And the words of al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid indicate that the verification regarding the affirmative privative (mujaba ma'dula) is that it requires the existence of the subject, because its predicate is positive, so what is judged in 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable' is the establishment of the relation of the opposite of knowledge, which is ignorance, so 'not-knowledgeable' is equivalent to 'ignorant', unlike the simple negative (saliba muhassila) where what is judged is knowledge without regard to the establishment of its opposite. And this is the truth.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) where the commentator first notes that the earlier verification (tahqiq) on the negative proposition's truth advantage follows al-Sanusi. Then an objection by Shaykh al-Tayyib is presented: he argues that any affirmation of a predicate for a subject requires the subject's existence, because if the subject did not exist, nothing could be predicated. The response distinguishes two types of existence: first, the subject's presence in the mind at the moment of judgment (which all propositions require), and second, the subject's actual existence (external or mental) commensurate with the predicate's duration (which only affirmatives require). The negative does not require even the second existence, because negating a predicate does not imply the subject's existence (e.g., 'The phoenix is not blind' is true even if the phoenix doesn't exist, as long as it is conceived). Thus the negative shares with the affirmative only the first existence (mental presence). The commentator then explains that al-Sanusi misunderstood the logicians as requiring external existence, so his objection is invalid. He adds that even some scholars like al-Razi and al-'Uqbani made similar objections. Finally, al-Sa'd's view is cited: for the privative affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable'), the predicate 'not-knowledgeable' is positive in the sense of affirming ignorance, so it requires the subject's existence, unlike the simple negative.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الموجبة المعدولة
السالبة المحصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to al-Tayyib's objection, what does a negative proposition (saliba) require regarding the subject?
A) External existence.
B) Mental existence at the moment of judgment only.
C) Neither mental nor external existence.
D) Both mental and external existence.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between mental existence (required for all judgments) and actual existence (required only when a positive predicate is affirmed) is essential for understanding the logical force of affirmative and negative propositions in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The Jinn are not bound by Islamic law.' Using the two-existence distinction, explain why this negative proposition is meaningful even if Jinn are not physically present. What kind of existence is required?
?
Examine the proposition 'This transaction is not riba' (simple negative) vs 'This transaction is riba-free' (privative affirmative). Apply the distinction to determine what existence of the subject is required in each case, and explain how this impacts a fatwa.

The Proper Placement of Quantifiers
Author explains that quantifiers only enter upon universal subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a quantifier is correctly placed on a universal subject or misplaced on a particular subject or predicate.

Core Idea
Quantifiers belong only on universal subjects because they indicate scope over individuals.

Quantifiers like 'kull' (all) and 'ba‘ḍ' (some) specify how many individuals the subject refers to. The classical logicians emphasize that quantifiers should only attach to terms that actually have individuals—namely universal subjects. Placing them elsewhere leads to logical deviance. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Locate the word that indicates universal or particular quantity (e.g., kull, ba‘ḍ). In Arabic, the quantifier typically precedes the subject.
If no quantifier, the proposition is indeterminate.
2
Check what term the quantifier attaches to
Determine whether the quantifier modifies the subject or the predicate. In a standard proposition, it should modify the subject.
3
Determine if the subject is universal or particular
A universal subject can have multiple individuals (e.g., 'insān' – man). A particular subject refers to a single individual (e.g., 'Zayd'). Only universal subjects are eligible for quantifiers.
Even if the subject is modified by negation, like 'lā ḥayy' (not-animal), it can still be universal if it applies to many things that are not animals.
4
Verdict: correct or deviant
If the quantifier is on a universal subject, it is correctly placed. If on a particular subject or on the predicate, the proposition is deviant (munḥarifa).
The predicate's purpose is to convey a concept, not to enumerate individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها بالعُدُولِ ؛ كَمَا يُقَالُ: «زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ كَاتِباً فِي الصَّالِبَةِ، وَ زَيْدٌ لَا كَاتِبَ أَوْ غَيْرَ كَاتِبٍ فِي المَعْدُولَةِ. اهـ (1) وَأَمَّا العُدُولُ فِي المَوْضُوعِ فَهُوَ بِأَنْ يُقَدَّمَ السُّوْرُ عَلَى حَرْفِ السَّلْبِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ لَا حَيَّ جَمَادٌ». التَّنْبِيهُ الرَّابِعُ : تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ السُّوْرَ هُوَ : مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمِ الْأَفْرَادِ، أَوْ تَبْعِيضِهَا»، وَأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنْ مَوْضُوع القَضِيَّةِ مَصْدُوقُهُ ، وَمِنْ [ج/ ٢٤] مَحْمُولِهَا مَنْهُومُهُ ، وَلِذَا كَانَ مِنْ حَقِّ السُّورِ أَنْ لَا يَدْخُلَ إِلَّا عَلَى مَا لَهُ أَفْرَادٌ يُقْصَدُ الحُكْمُ عَلَيْهَا تَعْمِيماً أَوْ تَبْعِيضاً ، وَهُوَ المَوْضُوعُ الكُلّيُّ لَا الجُزْئِيُّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا أَفْرَادَ لَهُ ، وَلَا المَحْمُولُ مُطلَقاً ، لأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنْهُ مَفْهُومُهُ ، لَا أَفْرَادُهُ، فَإِذَا دَخَلَ السُّورُ عَلَى المَوْضُوعِ الجُزْئِيِّ أَوْ عَلَى المَحْمُولِ، كُلِّيَّا كَانَ أَوْ جُزْئِيّاً، فَقَدِ انْحَرَفَ عَنْ مَوْضِعِهِ الَّذِي يَسْتَحِقُهُ، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : «مُنْحَرِفَةٌ»، وَقَدْ بَلَغُوا عَدَدَ صُورِ المُنْحَرِفَاتِ ... قوله في التنبيه الرَّابع: (إِلَّا عَلَى مَا لَهُ أَفْرَادُ أي : سواءٌ كانت خارجيَّةٌ، أو حقيقيَّة، أو ذهنيّة . قوله : (يُقْصَدُ الحُكْمُ عَلَيْهَا إِنَّما قال: «يقصد ... إلخ » ؛ إشارةً إلى أنَّ الموضوع لا يقصد منه الأفراد دائماً ؛ إذ قد يقصد منه المفهوم فقط، كما في الطَّبيعيَّات. قوله : (لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنْهُ مَنْهُومُهُ) أي: الحكم بحقيقة المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع، ولهذا قالوا : المحمول لا يكون موجوداً عيناً، وإنَّما يكون كلّيّا، وما ورد من نحو: «هذا زيد» فمؤوّل بصاحب هذا الاسم. قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : «مُنْحَرِفَةٌ» من باب تسمية الكلِّ باسم البعض؛ لأنَّ المنحرف : في الحقيقة إنَّما هو السُّور ; قال شيخنا سيدي حمدون بن الحاج : وَإِنْ أَتَتْ أَسْوَارُهَا مُنْحَرِفَه عَنْ حَقِّهَا، فَهَذِهِ مُنْحَرِفَه وَقال صاحب القادرية»: وس رِفَةٌ إِذَا عَلَى جُزْنِي أَوْ ذِي الحَمْلِ سُورٌ دَخَلَا نْحَرِفَةٌ
Chapter on propositions and their rulings: with modification, as is said: 'Zayd is not a writer' in the negative, and 'Zayd is not-writer' or 'other than writer' in the modified. End. As for modification in the subject, it is by advancing the quantifier before the particle of negation; e.g., 'Every not-animal is an inanimate object'. The fourth note: It has preceded that the quantifier is what indicates universalization of individuals or their particularization; and that the intended from the subject of the proposition is its instances (maṣdūquhu), and from its predicate its concept (mafhūmuhu); therefore it is proper for the quantifier not to enter except upon what has individuals upon which judgment is intended universally or particularly, and that is the universal subject, not the particular; because it has no individuals, nor the predicate at all; because what is intended from it is its concept, not its individuals. So if the quantifier enters upon the particular subject or upon the predicate, whether universal or particular, then it has deviated from its proper place, and the proposition is then called 'deviant' (munḥarifa). And they have enumerated the number of forms of deviants... His words in the fourth note: 'except upon what has individuals' i.e., whether external, real, or mental. His words: 'upon which judgment is intended' — he only said 'intended' to indicate that the subject is not always intended for its individuals; sometimes only the concept is intended, as in natural universals. His words: 'because what is intended from it is its concept' i.e., judgment is made of the reality of the predicate upon what the subject applies to; hence they said that the predicate does not exist as a particular entity, but is only universal, and what occurs like 'this is Zayd' is interpreted as 'the possessor of this name'. His words: 'and the proposition is then called deviant' — this is naming the whole by the name of the part, for in reality the deviant is only the quantifier. Our teacher Sayyid Ḥamdūn ibn al-Ḥājj said: And if its quantifiers come deviated from their proper place, then these are deviant. And the author of al-Qādiriyya said: And it is named deviant when upon a particular or upon the bearer of predication a quantifier enters.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of quantifiers (aswār) and deviant propositions (al-munḥarifāt) in propositional logic. It begins with a heading 'Chapter on propositions and their rulings', giving examples of modified propositions: the simple negative 'Zayd is not a writer' and the modified 'Zayd is not-writer' or 'Zayd is other than a writer'. Then it describes modification in the subject: when the quantifier precedes the negation particle, e.g., 'Every not-animal is an inanimate object'. The main focus is the fourth note: the quantifier indicates whether the judgment covers all or some individuals. The intended referent of the subject is its instances (maṣdūq), while the intended referent of the predicate is its concept (mafhūm). Therefore, the quantifier should only attach to the universal subject, which has individuals, not to the particular subject (which has no individuals) nor to the predicate (whose function is conceptual). If the quantifier attaches to a particular subject or to the predicate (whether universal or particular), the proposition becomes 'deviant' (munḥarifa). The commentary elaborates: 'has individuals' includes external, real, or mental individuals. 'Intended' indicates that sometimes only the concept of the subject is meant (as in natural universals). For the predicate, its concept is what matters, not its individuals; predicates are universal as concepts, and even statements like 'This is Zayd' are interpreted as 'This is the possessor of the name Zayd'. Finally, it notes that calling the whole proposition 'deviant' is naming the whole by the part, since the actual deviation is in the quantifier, not the entire proposition. Verses from Shaykh Ḥamdūn and the Qādiriyya are cited to reinforce the classification.

السور
الموضوع الكلي
الموضوع الجزئي
مصدوق
مفهوم

B) بعض الحيوان ناطق (Some animal is rational)
C) كل كاتب موجود (Every writer exists) - where 'writer' is intended as a concept
D) زيد ليس كل كاتب (Zayd is not every writer)

Key Takeaway
A quantifier must always attach to a universal subject, because only universal subjects have multiple individuals to quantify over.

Understanding Deviant Propositions
Author defines deviant propositions as those with misplace quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a deviant proposition and explain why it is deviant in terms of quantifier misplacement.

Core Idea
A deviant proposition is one where the quantifier has left its proper place on a universal subject.

When a quantifier attaches to a particular subject or to the predicate (whether universal or particular), the proposition is called 'deviant' (munḥarifa). The deviance is actually in the quantifier's position, but the whole proposition is named after it. This lesson clarifies the definition and the naming convention.

Guided Path
1
Find the quantifier
Look for a word indicating all or some (kull, ba‘ḍ, etc.). If none, the proposition is not deviant.
2
Check the term the quantifier modifies
Is it attached to the subject or the predicate? If it is attached to the predicate, it is deviant.
3
If attached to subject, check if subject is universal or particular
If the subject is particular (e.g., a proper name or a singular referent), it is deviant because particular subjects have no multiplicity. Only universal subjects are proper for quantifiers.
Even a modified subject like 'lā ḥayy' (not-animal) can be universal if it applies to many.
4
Classify as deviant or not
If the quantifier is on a particular subject or on the predicate (any), the proposition is deviant. Otherwise, it is correct.
The commentary notes that calling the proposition deviant is naming the whole after the part, since the actual deviation is the quantifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Quantifier's Proper Place in a Proposition
Author explains the proper placement of the quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the quantifier belongs only on a universal subject and not on a particular subject or the predicate.

Core Idea
The quantifier quantifies individuals; the predicate is conceptual.

Before defining deviant propositions, you must understand the correct placement of the quantifier. This lesson clarifies that the subject is taken by its extension (individuals) and the predicate by its intension (concept). Hence, the quantifier – which indicates generality or particularity of individuals – can only appropriately attach to a universal subject.

Guided Path
1
Recall the function of the quantifier
The quantifier (sur) indicates whether the judgment applies to all or some individuals of the subject. So it must attach to a term that has individuals to quantify.
Quantifiers are 'all' (kull) and 'some' (ba‘ḍ).
2
Distinguish subject and predicate roles
The subject is taken by its extension (maṣduq) – the set of individuals it refers to. The predicate is taken by its intension (mafhūm) – its concept or meaning.
Extension = individuals; intension = concept.
3
Apply the rule: quantifier only on universal subject
Since the predicate is taken by its concept, it has no individuals to quantify. A particular subject (e.g., 'some Zayd' if Zayd is a particular) also has no individuals because it is already particular. Therefore, the quantifier is correctly placed only before a universal subject.
Example: 'Every human is mortal' – 'human' is universal, so quantification is proper.
4
Identify what happens when the rule is violated
If the quantifier is placed before a particular subject or before the predicate, it 'deviates' (yanḥarif) from its proper place, producing a deviant proposition.
This deviation is the focus of the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبیه الرابع: تقدم أن السور هو ما دل على تعميم الأفراد أو تبعيضها، وأن المقصود من موضوع القضية مصدوقه، ومن محمولها مفهومه، ولذا كان من حق السور أن لا يدخل إلا على ما له أفراد يقصد الحكم عليها تعميما أو تبعيضا، وهو الموضوع الكلي لا الجزئي؛ لأنه لا أفراد له، ولا المحمول مطلقا، لأن المقصود منه مفهومه، لا أفراده، فإذا دخل السور على الموضوع الجزئي أو على المحمول، كليا كان أو جزئيا، فقد انحرف عن موضعه الذي يستحقه، وتسمى القضية حينئذ: «منحرفة»، وقد بلغوا عدد صور المنحرفات إلى مئة واثنتي عشرة قضية، انظرها في المختصر. قوله في التنبيه الرابع: (إلا على ما له أفراد) أي: سواء كانت خارجية، أو حقيقية، أو ذهنية. قوله: (يقصد الحكم عليها) إنما قال: «يقصد ... إلخ»؛ إشارة إلى أن الموضوع لا يقصد منه الأفراد دائما؛ إذ قد يقصد منه المفهوم فقط، كما في الطبيعيات. قوله: (لأن المقصود منه مفهومه) أي: الحكم بحقيقة المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع، ولهذا قالوا: المحمول لا يكون موجودا عينا، وإنما يكون كليا، وما ورد من نحو: «هذا زيد» فمؤول بصاحب هذا الاسم. قوله: (وتسمى القضية حينئذ: «منحرفة») من باب تسمية الكل باسم البعض؛ لأن المنحرف في الحقيقة إنما هو السور؛ قال شيخنا سيدي حمدون بن الحاج: وإن أتت أسوارها منحرفه عن حقها، فهذه منحرفه. وقال صاحب القادرية: منحرفة إذا على جزئي أو ذي الحمل سور دخلا. قوله: (إلى مئة واثنتي عشرة قضية) بيانها: أن السور إن دخل على المحمول: فإما أن يكون كليا؛ نحو: زيد كل إنسان، أو جزئيا نحو: زيد كل عمرو. والسور أيضا: إما كلي أو جزئي؛ كقولك في المثالين بدل كل: بعض؛ فهذه أربعة أحوال في المحمول. والموضوع مع كل واحد منها: إما كلي أو جزئي، وكل: إما مسور بالسور الكلي أو الجزئي أو مهمل، فهذه ستة أقسام، وأمثلتها على النشر المرتب: كل إنسان كل زيد، و: كل زيد كل عمرو، و: بعض الإنسان كل زيد، و: بعض زيد كل عمرو، و: الإنسان كل زيد، فإذا ضربت أحوال الموضوع السنة في أربعة أحوال المحمول؛ يخرج أربعة وعشرون. ثم الطرفان في جميعها: إما أن يقترنا بحرف السلب معا، أو لا يقترنان، أو يقترن الموضوع فقط، أو المحمول فقط، فهذه أربع حالات تضربها في الأربع والعشرين؛ بستة وتسعين؛ أربع وعشرون في حمل الكلي على الكلي، ومثله في حمل الجزئي على الجزئي، ومثليها في حمل الكلي على الجزئي، وحمل الجزئي على الكلي. وقد يكون الانحراف بدخول السور على الموضوع الجزئي فقط، ولا يدخل على المحمول أصلا؛ نحو: «كل زيد إنسان»، وحينئذ فالسور: إما كلي أو جزئي؛ نحو: «بعض زيد إنسان»، فهذه حالتان في الموضوع والمحمول مع كل منهما؛ إما كلي أو جزئي؛ نحو: «كل زيد عمرو»، و: «بعض زيد عمرو»، فهذه أربع؛ من ضرب اثنين في اثنين وكل منها: إما أن تقترن الطرفان فيه بحرف السلب أو لا، أو الموضوع فقط، أو المحمول فقط؛ أربعة في أربعة بست عشرة تضم إلى السنة والتسعين، يجتمع مئة واثنتا عشرة قضية.
The Fourth Notice: It has preceded that the quantifier is that which indicates generalization of individuals or particularization of them, and that the intended meaning from the subject of a proposition is its extension, and from its predicate is its intension. Therefore, it is fitting for the quantifier to enter only upon that which has individuals intended to be judged upon it, generally or particularly, i.e., the universal subject, not the particular, because it has no individuals, nor upon the predicate absolutely, because the intended from it is its concept, not its individuals. So if the quantifier enters upon the particular subject or upon the predicate, whether universal or particular, it has deviated from its proper place, and the proposition is then called 'deviant'. They have reached the number of forms of deviant propositions to one hundred and twelve propositions; see it in al-Mukhtasar. His statement in the Fourth Notice: ('except upon that which has individuals') i.e., whether they are external, true, or mental. His statement: ('intended to be judged upon them') He only said 'intended...' etc. as an indication that the subject is not always intended for its individuals; for sometimes its concept is intended, as in natural propositions. His statement: ('because the intended from it is its concept') i.e., the judgment of the reality of the predicate upon what the subject applies to; hence they said: the predicate does not exist as an entity, but is universal, and what is reported like 'This is Zayd' is interpreted as 'the owner of this name'. His statement: ('and the proposition is then called 'deviant'') is from the category of naming the whole by the name of a part, because the deviant in reality is only the quantifier; our master Sayyidi Hamdun ibn al-Hajj said: 'And if its quantifiers come deviated from their right, then it is deviant.' And the author of al-Qadiriyya said: 'Deviant when on a particular or the object of predication a quantifier enters.' His statement: ('to one hundred and twelve propositions') Its explanation: The quantifier if it enters upon the predicate: either it is universal, e.g., 'Zayd is every human', or particular, e.g., 'Zayd is every Amr'. And the quantifier also: either universal or particular, as if you say in the two examples instead of 'every' the word 'some'; so these are four states concerning the predicate. The subject with each of them: either universal or particular, and each: either quantified with a universal quantifier or a particular quantifier or unquantified, so these are six divisions. Their examples in orderly enumeration: 'Every human is every Zayd', 'Every Zayd is every Amr', 'Some human is every Zayd', 'Some Zayd is every Amr', 'The human is every Zayd'. So if you multiply the six states of the subject by the four states of the predicate, it yields twenty-four. Then the two terms in all of them: either they are both accompanied by the particle of negation, or not accompanied, or the subject alone is accompanied, or the predicate alone; so these are four states which you multiply into the twenty-four, yielding ninety-six: twenty-four for the predication of universal upon universal, likewise for the predication of particular upon particular, and double thereof for the predication of universal upon particular and the predication of particular upon universal. And deviation may be by the entry of the quantifier upon the particular subject only, without entering upon the predicate at all, e.g., 'Every Zayd is human'; then the quantifier: either universal or particular, e.g., 'Some Zayd is human'; so these are two states concerning the subject and the predicate with each of them: either universal or particular, e.g., 'Every Zayd is Amr', 'Some Zayd is Amr'; so these are four, from multiplying two by two. And each of them: either the two terms are accompanied by the particle of negation or not, or the subject alone, or the predicate alone; four by four yields sixteen, added to the ninety-six, totals one hundred and twelve propositions.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is both definitional and enumerative. It defines the deviant proposition (munharifa) and then calculates its 112 forms. The author begins by recalling that the quantifier (sur) indicates generalization or particularization. The subject of a proposition is considered by its extension (maṣduq), i.e., the individuals it applies to, while the predicate is considered by its intension (mafhūm), i.e., its concept. Therefore, the quantifier is appropriately placed only before a universal subject, because a particular subject has no individuals to quantify over, and it is never placed before the predicate because the predicate is judged by its concept, not its individuals. If the quantifier is placed before a particular subject or before the predicate (whether universal or particular), it deviates from its proper place, and such a proposition is called 'deviant'. The author then states that scholars have enumerated 112 forms of deviant propositions, referring to al-Mukhtasar for details. The commentary (the second part, introduced by 'قوله') expands on the definition: the subject's individuals can be external, true, or mental; the subject may be intended for its concept rather than its individuals (as in natural propositions); the predicate is always universal, and proper names like 'Zayd' are interpreted as 'the owner of this name'. It also clarifies that the term 'deviant' is applied to the whole proposition, though strictly only the quantifier deviates, as illustrated by a couplet from Hamdun ibn al-Hajj. Finally, the commentary provides a detailed breakdown of the enumeration: first, cases where the quantifier enters on the predicate (4 states of the quantifier on predicate times 6 states of the subject = 24, then multiplied by 4 states of negation = 96), and second, cases where the quantifier enters only on the particular subject (2 states of subject times 2 states of predicate = 4, then multiplied by 4 states of negation = 16), totaling 112. This meticulous counting demonstrates the author's thoroughness in classifying all possible deviations.

موضوع
محمول
منحرفة

Checkpoint
Why does the quantifier not enter upon the predicate?
A) The predicate is always particular.
B) The predicate is considered by its intension, not its extension.
C) The quantifier would make the proposition false.
D) The quantifier only applies to proper names.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier properly attaches only to a universal subject because it quantifies individuals, and the predicate is conceptual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the proposition 'Every scholar is knowledgeable' follows the rules of proper quantifier placement. Justify your answer by stating the subject, its extension, and the predicate's intension.
?
Construct a proposition where the quantifier is incorrectly placed on the predicate, and explain why it is deviant.

Defining and Identifying Deviant Propositions
Author defines deviant proposition and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a deviant proposition and identify when a quantifier has deviated.

Core Idea
A deviant proposition is one where the quantifier is misplaced.

Now that you know the correct place of the quantifier, we formally define the deviant proposition (al-qadiyya al-munharifa). This lesson explains the term and shows by example what deviation looks like.

Guided Path
1
Recall the correct quantifier placement
The quantifier should only be on a universal subject.
2
Define deviant proposition
A proposition is called 'deviant' (munharifa) when the quantifier enters upon a particular subject or upon the predicate (whether universal or particular).
The term 'deviant' is applied to the whole proposition, though really only the quantifier deviates.
3
Examine examples of deviation
Examples from the text: 'Zayd is every human' (quantifier on predicate), 'Every Zayd is human' (quantifier on particular subject). In both cases, the quantifier is not on a universal subject.
The text also mentions that proper names like Zayd are interpreted as 'the owner of the name', so they are particular.
4
Understand the naming convention
The commentary notes that the term 'deviant' is metonymic: the whole proposition is called deviant because its quantifier has deviated. A couplet from Hamdun ibn al-Hajj confirms this.

Propositions

Counting the 112 Forms of Deviant Propositions
Author enumerates the 112 forms via combinatorial multiplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the enumeration of 112 forms of deviant propositions using the combinatorial method described in the text.

Core Idea
The 112 forms arise from multiplying cases of quantifier placement, subject/predicate types, and negation.

The author states that scholars have enumerated 112 forms of deviant propositions. This lesson unpacks the combinatorial calculation step by step, showing how 24 forms multiply by 4 negation states, and then adding 16 more for a special case, totaling 112.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two major categories of deviation
First category: the quantifier enters on the predicate (with subject any kind). Second category: the quantifier enters only on the particular subject (predicate not quantified).
These two categories are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
2
Calculate the first category (96 forms)
For the predicate: the quantifier can be universal or particular (2 states). The predicate itself can be universal or particular (2 states). So 2×2=4 states for the predicate. For the subject: subject can be universal, particular, and each can be quantified universally, quantified particularly, or unquantified (3×2=6 states). Multiply 4 predicate states × 6 subject states = 24. Then there are 4 negation possibilities: both terms negated, neither negated, only subject negated, only predicate negated. 24×4=96.
The text accounts for universal/particular predication arrangements (kullī ‘alā kullī, etc.) but the multiplication is the same.
3
Calculate the second category (16 forms)
Here the quantifier is only on the particular subject. The subject quantifier can be universal or particular (2 states). The subject is particular. The predicate can be universal or particular (2 states). So 2×2=4. Then apply the 4 negation states: 4×4=16.
The subject's particular nature is fixed in this category.
4
Add the totals to get 112
96 + 16 = 112. This is the number of possible deviant propositions.
The text notes that this counting is from al-Mukhtasar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبیه الرابع: تقدم أن السور هو ما دل على تعميم الأفراد أو تبعيضها، وأن المقصود من موضوع القضية مصدوقه، ومن محمولها مفهومه، ولذا كان من حق السور أن لا يدخل إلا على ما له أفراد يقصد الحكم عليها تعميما أو تبعيضا، وهو الموضوع الكلي لا الجزئي؛ لأنه لا أفراد له، ولا المحمول مطلقا، لأن المقصود منه مفهومه، لا أفراده، فإذا دخل السور على الموضوع الجزئي أو على المحمول، كليا كان أو جزئيا، فقد انحرف عن موضعه الذي يستحقه، وتسمى القضية حينئذ: «منحرفة»، وقد بلغوا عدد صور المنحرفات إلى مئة واثنتي عشرة قضية، انظرها في المختصر. قوله في التنبيه الرابع: (إلا على ما له أفراد) أي: سواء كانت خارجية، أو حقيقية، أو ذهنية. قوله: (يقصد الحكم عليها) إنما قال: «يقصد ... إلخ»؛ إشارة إلى أن الموضوع لا يقصد منه الأفراد دائما؛ إذ قد يقصد منه المفهوم فقط، كما في الطبيعيات. قوله: (لأن المقصود منه مفهومه) أي: الحكم بحقيقة المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع، ولهذا قالوا: المحمول لا يكون موجودا عينا، وإنما يكون كليا، وما ورد من نحو: «هذا زيد» فمؤول بصاحب هذا الاسم. قوله: (وتسمى القضية حينئذ: «منحرفة») من باب تسمية الكل باسم البعض؛ لأن المنحرف في الحقيقة إنما هو السور؛ قال شيخنا سيدي حمدون بن الحاج: وإن أتت أسوارها منحرفه عن حقها، فهذه منحرفه. وقال صاحب القادرية: منحرفة إذا على جزئي أو ذي الحمل سور دخلا. قوله: (إلى مئة واثنتي عشرة قضية) بيانها: أن السور إن دخل على المحمول: فإما أن يكون كليا؛ نحو: زيد كل إنسان، أو جزئيا نحو: زيد كل عمرو. والسور أيضا: إما كلي أو جزئي؛ كقولك في المثالين بدل كل: بعض؛ فهذه أربعة أحوال في المحمول. والموضوع مع كل واحد منها: إما كلي أو جزئي، وكل: إما مسور بالسور الكلي أو الجزئي أو مهمل، فهذه ستة أقسام، وأمثلتها على النشر المرتب: كل إنسان كل زيد، و: كل زيد كل عمرو، و: بعض الإنسان كل زيد، و: بعض زيد كل عمرو، و: الإنسان كل زيد، فإذا ضربت أحوال الموضوع السنة في أربعة أحوال المحمول؛ يخرج أربعة وعشرون. ثم الطرفان في جميعها: إما أن يقترنا بحرف السلب معا، أو لا يقترنان، أو يقترن الموضوع فقط، أو المحمول فقط، فهذه أربع حالات تضربها في الأربع والعشرين؛ بستة وتسعين؛ أربع وعشرون في حمل الكلي على الكلي، ومثله في حمل الجزئي على الجزئي، ومثليها في حمل الكلي على الجزئي، وحمل الجزئي على الكلي. وقد يكون الانحراف بدخول السور على الموضوع الجزئي فقط، ولا يدخل على المحمول أصلا؛ نحو: «كل زيد إنسان»، وحينئذ فالسور: إما كلي أو جزئي؛ نحو: «بعض زيد إنسان»، فهذه حالتان في الموضوع والمحمول مع كل منهما؛ إما كلي أو جزئي؛ نحو: «كل زيد عمرو»، و: «بعض زيد عمرو»، فهذه أربع؛ من ضرب اثنين في اثنين وكل منها: إما أن تقترن الطرفان فيه بحرف السلب أو لا، أو الموضوع فقط، أو المحمول فقط؛ أربعة في أربعة بست عشرة تضم إلى السنة والتسعين، يجتمع مئة واثنتا عشرة قضية.
The Fourth Notice: It has preceded that the quantifier is that which indicates generalization of individuals or particularization of them, and that the intended meaning from the subject of a proposition is its extension, and from its predicate is its intension. Therefore, it is fitting for the quantifier to enter only upon that which has individuals intended to be judged upon it, generally or particularly, i.e., the universal subject, not the particular, because it has no individuals, nor upon the predicate absolutely, because the intended from it is its concept, not its individuals. So if the quantifier enters upon the particular subject or upon the predicate, whether universal or particular, it has deviated from its proper place, and the proposition is then called 'deviant'. They have reached the number of forms of deviant propositions to one hundred and twelve propositions; see it in al-Mukhtasar. His statement in the Fourth Notice: ('except upon that which has individuals') i.e., whether they are external, true, or mental. His statement: ('intended to be judged upon them') He only said 'intended...' etc. as an indication that the subject is not always intended for its individuals; for sometimes its concept is intended, as in natural propositions. His statement: ('because the intended from it is its concept') i.e., the judgment of the reality of the predicate upon what the subject applies to; hence they said: the predicate does not exist as an entity, but is universal, and what is reported like 'This is Zayd' is interpreted as 'the owner of this name'. His statement: ('and the proposition is then called 'deviant'') is from the category of naming the whole by the name of a part, because the deviant in reality is only the quantifier; our master Sayyidi Hamdun ibn al-Hajj said: 'And if its quantifiers come deviated from their right, then it is deviant.' And the author of al-Qadiriyya said: 'Deviant when on a particular or the object of predication a quantifier enters.' His statement: ('to one hundred and twelve propositions') Its explanation: The quantifier if it enters upon the predicate: either it is universal, e.g., 'Zayd is every human', or particular, e.g., 'Zayd is every Amr'. And the quantifier also: either universal or particular, as if you say in the two examples instead of 'every' the word 'some'; so these are four states concerning the predicate. The subject with each of them: either universal or particular, and each: either quantified with a universal quantifier or a particular quantifier or unquantified, so these are six divisions. Their examples in orderly enumeration: 'Every human is every Zayd', 'Every Zayd is every Amr', 'Some human is every Zayd', 'Some Zayd is every Amr', 'The human is every Zayd'. So if you multiply the six states of the subject by the four states of the predicate, it yields twenty-four. Then the two terms in all of them: either they are both accompanied by the particle of negation, or not accompanied, or the subject alone is accompanied, or the predicate alone; so these are four states which you multiply into the twenty-four, yielding ninety-six: twenty-four for the predication of universal upon universal, likewise for the predication of particular upon particular, and double thereof for the predication of universal upon particular and the predication of particular upon universal. And deviation may be by the entry of the quantifier upon the particular subject only, without entering upon the predicate at all, e.g., 'Every Zayd is human'; then the quantifier: either universal or particular, e.g., 'Some Zayd is human'; so these are two states concerning the subject and the predicate with each of them: either universal or particular, e.g., 'Every Zayd is Amr', 'Some Zayd is Amr'; so these are four, from multiplying two by two. And each of them: either the two terms are accompanied by the particle of negation or not, or the subject alone, or the predicate alone; four by four yields sixteen, added to the ninety-six, totals one hundred and twelve propositions.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is both definitional and enumerative. It defines the deviant proposition (munharifa) and then calculates its 112 forms. The author begins by recalling that the quantifier (sur) indicates generalization or particularization. The subject of a proposition is considered by its extension (maṣduq), i.e., the individuals it applies to, while the predicate is considered by its intension (mafhūm), i.e., its concept. Therefore, the quantifier is appropriately placed only before a universal subject, because a particular subject has no individuals to quantify over, and it is never placed before the predicate because the predicate is judged by its concept, not its individuals. If the quantifier is placed before a particular subject or before the predicate (whether universal or particular), it deviates from its proper place, and such a proposition is called 'deviant'. The author then states that scholars have enumerated 112 forms of deviant propositions, referring to al-Mukhtasar for details. The commentary (the second part, introduced by 'قوله') expands on the definition: the subject's individuals can be external, true, or mental; the subject may be intended for its concept rather than its individuals (as in natural propositions); the predicate is always universal, and proper names like 'Zayd' are interpreted as 'the owner of this name'. It also clarifies that the term 'deviant' is applied to the whole proposition, though strictly only the quantifier deviates, as illustrated by a couplet from Hamdun ibn al-Hajj. Finally, the commentary provides a detailed breakdown of the enumeration: first, cases where the quantifier enters on the predicate (4 states of the quantifier on predicate times 6 states of the subject = 24, then multiplied by 4 states of negation = 96), and second, cases where the quantifier enters only on the particular subject (2 states of subject times 2 states of predicate = 4, then multiplied by 4 states of negation = 16), totaling 112. This meticulous counting demonstrates the author's thoroughness in classifying all possible deviations.

سور
موضوع
محمول
سلب

The Criterion for Falsehood of Deviant Propositions
Author states the condition under which deviant propositions are necessarily false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a deviant proposition is necessarily false based on the two conditions given by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
A deviant proposition is false only if it affirms multiplicity for a single subject or combines multiple subjects into one.

In this passage, al-Bajuri sets out a precise criterion (ḍābiṭ) to determine when a deviant proposition — one that violates the normal quantifier-subject relationship — is necessarily false. He gives two specific conditions. This rule is essential for evaluating the truth-value of propositions in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with quantifiers applied to particulars.

Guided Path
1
Understand deviant propositions
A deviant proposition is one where the quantifier is misplaced. Al-Bajuri's examples: 'Every Zayd is knowledgeable' and 'Zayd is every knowledgeable person'.
Deviant propositions often arise from unnatural quantifier placement.
2
Identify the first condition
The proposition affirms individuals (shi'ā') for a particular subject (juznī). For instance, 'Every Zayd is a man' is false because Zayd is a single individual and cannot have multiple instances.
This condition applies when a universal quantifier is used with a singular term.
3
Identify the second condition
The proposition judges that multiple individuals are combined into a single individual. Example: 'Zayd is every knowledgeable person' claims Zayd is identical to the entire set, which is impossible.
This condition applies when the predicate is a universal quantifier over individuals.
4
Apply the criterion
Given any deviant proposition, check whether it meets either condition. If yes, it is necessarily false. If neither, it may be true or false like any other proposition.
The criterion gives a clear rule for assessing deviant quantifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضابط الكاذب بسبب الانحراف: أن كل قضية أثبتت للجزني أفراداً؛ نحو: «كل زيد عالم»، أو حكمت باجتماع أفراد في فرد واحد؛ نحو: «زيد كل عالم»، فهي كاذبة، وإلا يكن فيها أحد الأمرين، فهي كغيرها في الصدق والكذب. قوله: (وضابط الكاذب) قد أشار إلى هذا الضابط شيخنا سيدي حمدون بقوله: تكذب إن تثبت لجزني شياع، وعكس ذا أيضا يكون في ضياع
And the criterion for falsehood due to deviation: every proposition that affirms individuals for a particular [subject], e.g., 'Every Zayd is knowledgeable', or judges the conjunction of individuals in a single individual, e.g., 'Zayd is every knowledgeable person', is false. If it does not contain either of the two [cases], it is like other propositions in truth and falsehood. His statement: ('And the criterion for falsehood') Our master Sayyidi Hamdun alluded to this criterion by his saying: 'It becomes false if you affirm for a particular a generality, and the opposite of that also becomes lost.'
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage provides the rule for when a deviant proposition is necessarily false. The author states two conditions: (1) if the proposition affirms individuals for a particular subject (e.g., 'Every Zayd is knowledgeable' – since Zayd is a particular individual, he cannot have multiple individuals, so the quantifier 'every' falsely implies multiple Zayds), and (2) if the proposition judges that multiple individuals are combined into a single individual (e.g., 'Zayd is every knowledgeable person' – meaning Zayd himself is identical to every knowledgeable person, which is impossible). In both cases, the deviation causes falsehood. If neither condition applies, the deviant proposition may be true or false like any other proposition. The commentary cites a poetic line from Hamdun ibn al-Hajj that encapsulates the rule: 'It becomes false if you affirm for a particular a generality' (i.e., the first condition) 'and the opposite of that also becomes lost' (i.e., affirming a particular for a general also fails). This rule is important for evaluating the truth of propositions where the quantifier is misplaced, as it clarifies that not all deviant propositions are false – only those that entail a contradiction of individual counts.

ضابط

Key Takeaway
A deviant proposition is necessarily false if it wrongfully attributes multiplicity to a particular subject or collapses multiple individuals into one.

Definition and Division of Conditional Propositions
Author defines conditional proposition and divides it into connected and disconnected with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disconnected conditionals and identify their parts (antecedent, consequent).

Core Idea
Conditional propositions link two claims: either by suspension (connected) or opposition (disconnected).

This lesson introduces the conditional proposition (shartiyya), which forms the basis of conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic. After categorical propositions, the text now turns to conditionals, defining them and dividing them into two fundamental types: connected and disconnected. Understanding this division is essential for analyzing arguments in kalam, fiqh, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of a conditional
Every conditional has two original propositions: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali). Locate these in the examples given.
The linking particle (harf al-shart or harf al-'inad) connects them.
2
Understand the definition of the connected conditional
In a connected conditional (muttasila), the judgment suspends the obtaining of one proposition's content on the obtaining of another, or negates that. Example: 'If the sun rises, day exists.'
The suspension (ta'liq) is literal here.
3
Understand the definition of the disconnected conditional
In a disconnected conditional (munfasila), the judgment asserts opposition ('inad) between the contents of two propositions, or negates that. Example: 'Either the number is even or it is odd.'
The opposition means they cannot both be true or both false simultaneously (depending on type).
4
Distinguish the two types using the examples
Compare the examples: connected uses 'in' (if) and expresses dependence; disconnected uses 'imma' (either) and expresses mutual exclusivity. Practice classifying new examples.
The negation examples show how the same structure can be negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ (٥٨) أَيْضاً : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ، وَمِثْلِهَا شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَ (٥٩) جُزَآهُمَا: مُقَدَّمُ، وَتَالِي لَمَّا تَكَلَّمَ عَلَى مَا قَصَدَهُ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّاتِ، ذَكَرَ هُنَا مُقَابِلَهَا ، وَهُوَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ، وَتَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهَا : القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ مِنْ قَضِيّتَيْنِ فِي الأَصْلِ، وَرَبَطَ بَيْنَهُمَا حَرْفُ الشَّرْطِ أَوِ العِنَادِ»، هي : وَعَنْ هَذَا الرَّبْطِ الشَّامِلِ لِلأَمْرَيْنِ عَبَّرَ هُنَا بِـ: «التَّعْلِيقِ»؛ بِدَلِيلِ أَنَّهُ قَسَّمَهَا : - إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِتَعْلِيقِ حُصُولِ مَضْمُونِ قَضِيَّةٍ عَلَى حُصُولِ مَضْمُونِ أُخْرَى ، أَوْ بِنَفْي ذَلِكَ»؛ نَحْوُ : «إِنْ طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ وُجِدَ النَّهَارُ، أَوْ: «لَيْسَ إِنْ طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ وُجِدَ اللَّيْلُ . - وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِبُوتِ العِنَادِ بَيْنَ مَضْمُونَي قَضِيَّتَيْنِ، أَوْ سَلْبٍ ذَلِكَ»؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً» ، أَوْ لَيْسَ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَيَوَاناً».
His saying: (57) 'And if upon the suspension in it a judgment has been made, then it is conditional' and it divides (58) also into connected conditional and similarly disconnected conditional (59) their two parts: antecedent and consequent. When he spoke about what he intended from categoricals, he mentioned here their counterpart, which is the conditional. It was previously stated that it is the proposition that is composed of two propositions originally, and a particle of condition or opposition links them. And from this linking that encompasses both matters he expressed here with 'suspension' as evidence that he divided it: into a connected conditional, which is that in which a judgment is made suspending the obtaining of the content of one proposition on the obtaining of the content of another, or a negation of that; such as: 'If the sun rises, day exists' or 'It is not that if the sun rises, night exists.' And into a disconnected conditional, which is that in which a judgment is made of the existence of opposition between the contents of two propositions, or a negation of that; such as: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' or 'It is not that either this is a human or it is an animal.'
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage defines and divides conditional propositions. The author begins by referencing the matn line, then explains that after discussing categorical propositions, the text now turns to their counterpart: the conditional. He recalls the earlier definition that a conditional is composed of two original propositions linked by a conditional (shart) or adversative (inad) particle. The linking is termed 'ta'liq' (suspension) because it connects the two parts. The division is into two types: (1) connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila), where the judgment suspends the obtaining of one proposition's content on the obtaining of another, e.g., 'If the sun rises, day exists' (affirmative) or 'It is not that if the sun rises, night exists' (negative); (2) disconnected conditional (shartiyya munfasila), where the judgment asserts opposition between the contents, e.g., 'Either the number is even or it is odd' (affirmative) or 'It is not that either this is a human or it is an animal' (negative). This classification is foundational for understanding conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

D) It is not that either this is a human or it is an animal.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is defined by a linking particle (condition or opposition) that creates either suspension (connected) or opposition (disconnected) between its two constituent propositions.

Identifying Antecedent and Consequent in Conditionals
Explains metaphorical usage and defines antecedent and consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in any conditional proposition and determine whether the order is based on wording or meaning.

Core Idea
In conditionals, the antecedent is the followed, the consequent is the follower, but ordering rules differ by type.

Conditional propositions are the backbone of logical inference in Islamic sciences, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh. The source passage clarifies the essential parts of both connected and disconnected conditionals, and how the terms 'antecedent' and 'consequent' are defined and applied. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze conditional arguments correctly.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish literal and metaphorical 'ta'liq'
The author states that the term 'ta'liq' (suspension) is used metaphorically for the linking in disconnected conditionals by resemblance to the literal linking in connected ones. This shows how the same term can span both literal and figurative uses.
Note the author's comment that such mixed usage is permissible according to the correct view.
2
Identify antecedent and consequent
The first part is called muqaddam (antecedent) because it is the sought-after followed, and the second is called tali (consequent) because it is the follower. This definition applies to both connected and disconnected conditionals.
Memorize these terms: antecedent is the dependent-upon, consequent is the dependent.
3
Apply the meaning-order rule to connected conditionals
In connected conditionals, the antecedent is determined by meaning, not by word order. For example, in 'Day exists if the sun rises', 'Day exists' is the consequent even though it appears first, because its existence depends on the sun rising. Word order may be reversed for rhetorical or technical reasons.
The example shows that the consequent can precede the antecedent in wording without changing its logical role.

However, the application of 'suspension' to this linking is metaphorical, likening it to the linking of the connected, so it involves using the word in both its literal and metaphorical senses, by indication of what follows, and that is permissible according to the correct view. Similarly, naming the disconnected 'conditional' is also metaphorical due to its resemblance to the connected in what it is composed of. Then the first of the two extremes in each of them is called 'antecedent' because it is the sought-after followed, and their second is called 'consequent' because it is the sought-after follower. Then this in the disconnected proposition runs only according to the order of wording, and in the connected according to the order of meaning, even if the consequent is presented in wording; such as: 'Day exists if the sun rises,' and its precedence does not remove it from being a consequent. As for the estimation of the answer after the condition in analysis, it is only for the sake of that art, because the particle
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage clarifies the metaphorical usage in conditional terminology. The author first notes that applying 'ta'liq' (suspension) to the linking in disconnected conditionals is metaphorical, since it resembles the literal linking in connected conditionals. This involves using the term in both literal and metaphorical senses simultaneously, which is permissible. Similarly, calling the disconnected type 'shartiyya' (conditional) is metaphorical due to its composition resembling the connected type. Then the author defines the parts: the first extreme is called muqaddam (antecedent) because it is the sought-after followed, and the second is called tali (consequent) as the follower. In disconnected conditionals, this designation is based on the order of wording. In connected conditionals, it is based on the order of meaning, so if the consequent is mentioned first (e.g., 'Day exists if the sun rises'), it remains the consequent because meaning-wise it depends on the antecedent. The placement of the answer after the particle in analysis is merely a technical convention in the craft of logic.

Key Takeaway
In conditional propositions, the antecedent is the depended-upon part and the consequent is the dependent part, and in connected conditionals this is determined by meaning order, not word order.

The Disjunctive Conditional: Definition and Examples
Defines the disjunctive conditional proposition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive conditional proposition and distinguish its affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
The disjunctive conditional judges mutual exclusion between two propositions, not genuine conditionality.

We now move from connected conditionals to the disjunctive type. This passage defines the disjunctive conditional precisely, highlighting its core feature: mutual exclusion. You will learn how to recognize both affirmative and negative forms and understand why the term 'conditioning' is used metaphorically here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-shartiyya al-munfasila' (disjunctive conditional). Note that it is a type of compound proposition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is a compound proposition. The differentia is that it judges mutual exclusion ('al-'inad) between the contents of two propositions — either affirmatively or negatively.
3
Distinguish affirmative and negative forms
The definition explicitly includes both 'bi-thubut al-'inad' (affirming mutual exclusion) and 'salbi dhalik' (negating it). This gives two subtypes: affirmative and negative disjunctives.
4
Analyze the examples
The affirmative example: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' — both cannot be true and cannot be false together. The negative example: 'It is not the case that either this is a human or it is an animal' — denies the mutual exclusion (since it is both).
5
Understand the metaphorical use of 'ta'liq'
The word 'ta'liq' (conditioning) is applied metaphorically to disjunctives because there is no genuine antecedent-consequent relation; it is merely a resemblance to connected conditionals. The author notes this is a permissible combined use of literal and metaphorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِبُوتِ العِنَادِ بَيْنَ مَضْمُونَي قَضِيَّتَيْنِ، أَوْ سَلْبٍ ذَلِكَ»؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً» ، أَوْ لَيْسَ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَيَوَاناً».
غَيْرَ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقَ التَّعْلِيقِ عَلَى هَذَا الرَّبْطِ مَجَازٌ ؛ تَشْبِيهَا لَهُ بِرَبْطِ المُتَّصِلَةِ، فَفِيهِ اسْتِعْمَالُ اللَّفْظِ فِي حَقِيقَتِهِ وَمَجَازِهِ؛ بِقَرِينَةِ مَا بَعْدَهُ، وَهُوَ جَائِزٌ عَلَى الصَّحِيحِ .
And to a disjunctive conditional, which is: that in which is judged the affirmation of mutual exclusion between the contents of two propositions, or the negation of that; such as: 'Either the number is even, or it is odd,' or 'It is not the case that either this is a human or it is an animal.' However, the application of 'conditioning' to this linkage is metaphorical; as a resemblance to the linkage of the connected [conditional]. Thus it involves using the word in its literal and metaphorical sense together, by the indication of what follows it, and that is permissible according to the correct [view].
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional proposition (al-shartiyya al-munfasila). It begins with the definition: a proposition that rules on the mutual exclusion (al-‘inād) between the contents of two propositions, either affirmatively (with ‘bi-thubūt’) or negatively (with ‘aw salbi dhālika’). Two examples are given: the affirmative example says a number is either even or odd—this is a true disjunction where both cannot be true simultaneously and both cannot be false simultaneously. The negative example denies the disjunction, i.e., it is not the case that this is either a human or an animal (which would be false because it is both). Then the commentary explains that calling this linkage 'conditioning' (ta‘līq) is metaphorical, because in a genuine conditional the antecedent conditions the consequent, but in a disjunction the parts are merely linked by mutual exclusion. This usage analogizes it to the connected conditional, so the word is used in both its literal and metaphorical sense together, which is permissible on the correct view.

Key Terms

العناد
التعليق

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive conditional is defined by the judgment of mutual exclusion between two propositions, and the term 'conditioning' is used metaphorically.

Nomenclature of Antecedent and Consequent in Conditionals
Explains the naming of parts in conditionals and the difference between connected and disjunctive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the basis of naming muqaddam and tāli in connected versus disjunctive conditionals, and identify the two scholarly positions on the scope of these terms.

Core Idea
Muqaddam and tāli reflect logical order only in connected conditionals; in disjunctives, they are merely verbal.

Conditional propositions have two parts: muqaddam (antecedent) and tāli (consequent). But these terms are not applied uniformly. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies that in connected conditionals, the naming follows the logical order of meaning, while in disjunctives it follows only verbal order. A disagreement among logicians further nuances the usage. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional
In any conditional proposition, there are two parts: the first is called muqaddam and the second tāli. This is a general rule, but the basis for this naming differs between connected and disjunctive conditionals.
2
Compare the basis in connected vs. disjunctive
In a connected conditional (e.g., 'If A then B'), the naming follows the logical order: the muqaddam is the logical antecedent (the condition) and the tāli is the logical consequent (the result). In a disjunctive conditional (e.g., 'Either A or B'), the naming follows only the verbal order (which part is spoken first), because logically the two parts are interchangeable.
The passage explicitly states that in disjunctives it is 'according to the order of the words only'.
3
Analyze the example given
The example 'The daytime exists if the sun has risen' is a connected conditional where the tāli ('the daytime exists') comes before the muqaddam ('if the sun has risen') in verbal order. Despite this, it remains the tāli because logically it is the consequent. This shows that in connected conditionals, logical order overrides verbal order.
The passage says: 'Its being advanced does not remove it from being a consequent.'
4
Contrast logicians' and grammarians' approaches
Grammarians (nuḥāt) insist on placing the conditional particle first, so they mentally estimate the answer after the condition even if words are rearranged. Logicians disregard this because they consider only meaning, not syntax.
Logicians' focus is purely on logical import, hence no need for taqdīr al-jawāb.
5
Examine the disagreement over generalizing the terms
Some logicians (like the versifier) apply muqaddam and tāli to both connected and disjunctive conditionals. Others (like al-Mukhtasar) restrict these terms to connected conditionals only, arguing that in disjunctives there is no real order of meaning—the speaker can arbitrarily choose which part to state first.
This disagreement is presented as a critical refinement: understanding why al-Mukhtasar restricts the terms clarifies the nature of disjunctive propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا يُسَمَّى : مُقَدَّماً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الطَّالِبُ المَتْبُوعُ، وَثَانِيهِمَا يُسَمَّى: «تَالِياً»؛ لأَنَّهُ المَطْلُوبُ التَّابِعُ . ثُمَّ هَذَا فِي القَضِيَّةِ المُنْفَصِلَةِ جَارٍ عَلَى حَسَبٍ تَرْتِيبِ اللَّفْظِ فَقَطْ، وَفِي المُتَّصِلَةِ عَلَى حَسَبِ تَرْتِيبِ المَعْنَى ، وَإِنْ قُدِّمَ التَّالِي لَفْظاً؛ نَحْوُ : «النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ إِنْ طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ، وَلَا يُخْرِجُهُ تَقَدُّمُهُ عَنْ كَوْنِهِ تَالِياً . وَأَمَّا تَقْدِيرُ الجَوَابِ بَعْدَ الشَّرْطِ عِنْدَ التَّحَاةِ، فَلِمُرَاعَاةِ تِلْكَ الصِّنَاعَةِ فَقَطْ ; لِأَنَّ الأَدَاةَ لَهَا الصَّدْرُ، وَلَا حَاجَةَ إِلَى ذَلِكَ عِنْدَ المَنْطِقِيّ ; لأَنَّ مَلْحُوظَهُ المَعْنَى فَقَطْ، وَتَعْمِيمُ التَّسْمِيَةِ فِي طَرَفَي المُتَّصِلَةِ وَالمُنْفَصِلَةِ، كَمَا فَعَلَ النَّاظِمُ طَرِيقَةٌ لِبَعْضِهِمْ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَصَرَهَا عَلَى طَرَفَي المُتَّصِلَةِ قَالَ : لأَنَّ طَرَفَي المُنْفَصِلَةِ لَا تَرْتِيبَ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي المَعْنَى، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ اخْتِيَارُ المُتَكَلَّم يُقَدِّمُ أَيُّهُمَا شَاءَ، وَالمَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ، وَهَذَا صَنِيعُ المُخْتَصَرِ».
Then the first of the two parts in each of them is called 'muqaddam' (antecedent) because it is the seeker followed, and the second of them is called 'tali' (consequent) because it is the sought follower. Then this in the disjunctive proposition runs according to the order of the words only, while in the connected [conditional] it is according to the order of meaning, even if the consequent is advanced in words; such as: 'The daytime exists if the sun has risen.' Its being advanced does not remove it from being a consequent. As for the estimation of the answer after the condition in [the usage of] the grammarians, it is solely to observe that discipline; because the particle has the initial position, and there is no need for that in the view of the logician, because his consideration is of meaning only. And generalizing the nomenclature to the two parts of the connected and the disjunctive, as the versifier did, is a method of some of them, and some of them restricted it to the two parts of the connected. He said: Because the two parts of the disjunctive have no order in meaning; rather, it is the choice of the speaker—he may advance whichever he wishes, and the meaning is one. And this is the practice of al-Mukhtasar.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage discusses the naming of the parts of conditional propositions. It states that the first part is called muqaddam (antecedent) because it is the seeker (al-ṭālib) and followed (al-matbū‘), and the second is called tāli (consequent) because it is the sought (al-maṭlūb) and follower (al-tābi‘). However, there is an important distinction: in a disjunctive conditional, this naming is purely based on the verbal order, whereas in a connected conditional it is based on the order of meaning (i.e., logical order). An example is given: 'The daytime exists if the sun has risen'—here the consequent 'the daytime exists' is placed before the antecedent 'if the sun has risen', yet it remains the consequent because logically it follows from the antecedent. The passage then addresses the practice of grammarians (nuḥāt) who estimate the answer (jawāb) after the condition because the conditional particle (in Arabic) must come first, but logicians disregard this as they consider meaning only. Finally, there is a disagreement: some (like the versifier) generalize the terms muqaddam and tāli to both connected and disjunctive conditionals, while others restrict them to connected conditionals on the grounds that disjunctives lack a real order of meaning—the speaker can arbitrarily choose which part comes first without affecting the truth conditions. This latter view is attributed to al-Mukhtasar.

Key Terms
مقدم (muqaddam): The first part of a conditional proposition; in connected conditionals it is the logical antecedent; in disjunctives it is the first part verbally. [Note: Some texts (e.g., al-Mukhtasar) restrict this term to connected conditionals; others apply it to disjunctives as well.]
تالي (tāli): The second part of a conditional proposition; the logical consequent in connected conditionals. [Note: Same as muqaddam; usage depends on the author.]
تقدير الجواب (taqdīr al-jawāb): The grammarians' practice of mentally placing the answer after the condition even if words are rearranged. [Note: No significant shift; it is a grammar-specific concept.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The naming of muqaddam and tāli reflects logical order only in connected conditionals; in disjunctives, it is merely verbal order, and some logicians restrict the terms to connected conditionals for this reason.

Critique of the Definition of the Connected Conditional
Presents the standard definition of the connected conditional and criticizes it as not comprehensive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error in defining the connected conditional solely by entailment and apply the corrected definition to Islamic conditional statements.

Core Idea
A connected conditional judges companionship or its negation, not necessarily entailment.

This passage presents the standard definition of the connected conditional as requiring entailment (talāzum) between its two parts. However, the commentator shows that this definition fails to include coincidental and negative conditionals. We will examine the critique and learn the correct definition that encompasses all types of connected conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definition
Find the Arabic phrase defining the connected conditional: 'that which necessitates entailment between its two parts.' Write it down.
The definition uses 'awjabat al-talāzum'.
2
Locate the critique
The commentator says this definition is 'ghayru jāmi‘' (not comprehensive). Why? Because it excludes two types: the coincidental (ittifāqiyya) and the negative (sāliba).
3
Understand the excluded types
The coincidental conditional is where the two parts always coincide without necessary connection (e.g., 'Whenever something is a human, it is a reader' if all humans happened to be readers). The negative conditional negates companionship (e.g., 'Not: whenever something is an animal, it is a human').
These are valid connected conditionals but do not involve entailment.
4
Recognize the corrected definition
The correct definition: the connected conditional judges companionship (al-ṣuḥba) or its negation between the two parts. The text gives an affirmative example: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' (companionship), and a negative example: 'Not: whenever something is an animal, it is a human' (negation of companionship).
This definition includes both affirmative and negative, and both necessary and coincidental cases.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the statement: 'If the moon is sighted, then fasting is obligatory.' This is a connected conditional. Is it based on entailment or mere companionship? In usul al-fiqh, such statements often rely on a shar‘i connection, not natural entailment. The corrected definition accommodates this.
The example shows the practical relevance in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ بَيَّنَ المُتَّصِلَةِ فَقَالَ:
(٥٩)
أَمَّا بَيَانُ ذَاتِ الان يَانُ ذَاتِ الِانّصَالِ :
(٦٠) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَلَازُمَ الجُرْأَيْنِ يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ المُتَّصِلَةَ هِيَ : الَّتِي أَوْجَبَتْ - أَيْ : أَثْبَتَتْ - الثَّلَازُمَ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا» وَهَذَا التَّعْرِيفُ غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِهِ لِلاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ وَالسَّالِبَةِ) .
وَالصَّوَابُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «المُتَّصِلَةُ هِي : الَّتِي حَكَمَتْ بِالصُّحْبَةِ، أَوْ سَلْبِهَا بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ»؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، وَ لَيْسَ هُوَ - أَي : الشَّيْءُ - كُلَّمَا كَانَ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ إِنْسَاناً».
Then he explained the connected [conditional] and said: (59) 'As for the explanation of what pertains to connectedness: (60) What necessitates the entailment of the two parts' means: the connected conditional is that which necessitates—i.e., affirms—entailment between its two parts.' And this definition is not comprehensive because it does not include the coincidental and the negative. The correct is to say: 'The connected is that which judges companionship, or its negation, between the two parts'; such as: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal,' and 'Not: whenever something is an animal, it is a human.'
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage presents and then critiques the definition of the connected conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The initial definition says it is 'that which necessitates entailment (talāzum) between its two parts.' The commentator points out that this definition is not comprehensive (jāmi‘) because it fails to include two types: the 'coincidental' (ittifāqiyya) and the negative (sāliba). The 'coincidental' refers to conditionals where the antecedent and consequent always happen together without necessary entailment (e.g., 'Whenever something is a human, it is a reader' is not always true, but if they always coincide, it is still a valid conditional). The negative (sāliba) is the negation of companionship, e.g., 'It is not the case that whenever something is an animal, it is a human.' The correct definition, therefore, is that the connected conditional judges either the companionship (al-ṣuḥba) or its negation between the two parts. Two examples are given: the affirmative 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' (which asserts companionship between being human and being animal) and the negative 'Not: whenever something is an animal, it is a human' (which negates companionship). Thus the corrected definition captures both affirmative and negative as well as coincidental and necessary cases.

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة
التلازم
الاتفاقية

Key Takeaway
The definition of the connected conditional must include both affirmative and negative judgments of companionship, not just entailment.
