Definition and Division of the Conjunctive Conditional
Definition and division of the conjunctive conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the correct definition of the conjunctive conditional from an incomplete one and classify its two subtypes (implicative and accidental).

Core Idea
The conjunctive conditional is about companionship, not necessarily entailment.

The conjunctive conditional (al-muttasila) is a type of conditional proposition that expresses a relation between its antecedent and consequent. The passage starts by presenting a common but flawed definition, then corrects it, and finally divides the conditional into two kinds. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, where conditionals are frequently used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the incomplete definition
Locate the definition 'what necessitates entailment between its two parts.' What is its limitation according to the author?
This definition fails to include accidental (ittifaqiyya) and negative (saliba) conditionals.
2
Extract the correct definition
Find the corrected definition: 'that which judges companionship or its negation between the two sides.' Note that 'companionship' (subha) is broader than 'entailment' (talazum).
3
Understand the division into two types
The passage then divides the conjunctive into implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). The implicative has a cause for the companionship; the accidental does not.
The passage does not give an explicit accidental example, but the implicative examples are provided.
4
Recognize the four causes of implication
For the implicative type, the companionship can be due to reason ('aql), habit ('ada), law (shar'), or correlativity (tadayuf). Identify the example for each cause.
These correspond to the examples: rational (human to animal), habitual (sunrise to light), legal (sun decline to prayer), correlative (e.g., fatherhood to childhood).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بَيَانُ ذَاتِ الاتِّصَالِ : «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَلَازُمَ الجُزْأَيْنِ» يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ المُتَّصِلَةَ هِيَ : الَّتِي أَوْجَبَتْ - أَيْ : أَثْبَتَتْ - التَّلَازُمَ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا . وَهَذَا التَّعْرِيفُ غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِهِ لِلِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ وَالسَّالِبَةِ . وَالصَّوَابُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «المُتَّصِلَةُ هِيَ : الَّتِي حَكَمَتْ بِالصُّحْبَةِ، أَوْ سَلْبِهَا بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ»؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً، وَلَيْسَ هُوَ كُلَّمَا كَانَ حَيَوَاناً كَانَ إِنْسَاناً. قوله : (وَلَا حَاجَةَ إِلَى ذَلِكَ عِنْدَ المَنْطِقِي ... إلخ) أشار بهذا لرد قول ابن مرزوق : التحقيق أنَّ المقدَّم لا يزال مقدَّماً في اللفظ ؛ لأنَّ جواب الشرط أبداً متأخّر، ولو تقدم شيءٌ فهو دليل الجزاء لا عينه. ووجه الرَّدِّ : أنَّ ملحوظ المناطقة المعنى فقط، وإذا كان كذلك فلا حاجة إلى تقدير الجواب، مع تقدم ما يصلح له بحسب المعنى. ثُمَّ هِيَ تَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لُزُومِيَّةٍ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي تَكُونُ الصُّحْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِمُوجِبٍ»؛ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنْهُمَا سَبَباً لِلثَّانِي، أَوْ عَكْسَهُ عَقْلاً أَوْ عَادَةً أَوْ شَرْعاً، أَوْ يَشْتَرِكُ الطَّرَفَانِ فِي سَبَبٍ، أَوْ يَكُونَا مُتَضَايِفَيْنِ. فَالعَقْلِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً». وَالعَادِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ وُجِدَ الضَّوْءُ». وَالشَّرْعِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلَّمَا مَالَتِ الشَّمْسُ وَجَبَتْ صَلَاةُ الظُّهْرِ». وَيَكُونُ الأَوَّلُ مُسَبَّباً عَنِ الثَّانِي فِي عَكْسِ هَذِهِ الأَمْثِلَةِ.
As for the clarification of the nature of the conjunctive: "What necessitates the entailment of the two parts" means: the conjunctive conditional is that which necessitates—i.e., establishes—entailment between its two sides. And this definition is not comprehensive, due to its not including the accidental and the negative. The correct thing is to say: "The conjunctive conditional is that which judges companionship, or its negation, between the two sides"; such as: "Whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal," and "It is not that whenever a thing is an animal, it is a human." His statement: (And there is no need for that according to the logician... etc.) He pointed by this to refute the saying of Ibn Marzuq: The fact is that the antecedent always remains antecedent in wording because the response of the conditional is always delayed, and if something precedes it, it is an indication of the apodosis, not the apodosis itself. The reason for the refutation is that the logician's observation is only of the meaning, and when that is the case, there is no need to estimate the apodosis, with the presence of what is suitable for it in terms of meaning. Then it is divided into: implicative, which is "that in which the companionship between its two sides is due to a cause"; such that the first of them is a cause of the second, or its opposite, by reason, habit, or law, or the two sides share a cause, or they are correlatives. The rational: such as "Whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal." The habitual: such as "Whenever the sun rises, light exists." The legal: such as "Whenever the sun declines, the noon prayer becomes obligatory." And the first is caused by the second in the opposite of these examples.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage defines the conjunctive conditional (al-muttasila) and then divides it. The author begins by presenting a common definition: 'What necessitates the entailment of the two parts' i.e., a conditional that establishes entailment (talazum) between its antecedent and consequent. However, this definition is criticized as not comprehensive because it fails to include two types: the accidental conjunctive (ittifaqiyya) where there is mere companionship without entailment, and the negative conjunctive (saliba). The correct definition, according to the author, is 'that which judges companionship or its negation between the two parts.' The companion or negation is what is affirmed or denied. An example of affirmative companionship is 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal.' An example of negative is 'It is not that whenever something is an animal, it is human.' The author then mentions a side discussion: Ibn Marzuq argued that the antecedent must remain as antecedent in wording, but logicians only care about meaning, so there is no need for such formal constraints. After this, the passage moves to the division of the conditional into implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). The implicative is defined as one where the companionship between its two sides has a cause (mujib). This cause can be logical (such as in 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' – because animality is part of the definition of human), habitual (like 'Whenever the sun rises, light exists' – a regular occurrence by habit), legal (like 'Whenever the sun declines, the noon prayer becomes obligatory' – by divine law), or the two sides may be correlatives (mutadayifan). Each example illustrates a different basis for the implication.

اللُّزُومِيَّة
التَّلَازُم
الصُّحْبَة

Key Takeaway

Implicative Conditionals: Shared Cause and Correlative
Author defines luzumiyya conditionals with two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of luzumiyya conditionals (shared cause and correlative) and distinguish them from coincidental conditionals.

Core Idea
Luzumiyya conditionals have a real necessary connection; ittifaqiyya do not.

In Islamic logic, not every 'if...then' statement implies a genuine causal link. Some conditionals, like 'Whenever light exists, the stars are hidden,' share a common cause, while others, like 'If Zaid is the father of Amr, then Amr is his son,' involve correlatives that necessarily accompany each other. This lesson equips you to recognize these two types of necessary conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Read the conditional and label the part after 'if' (or 'whenever') as the antecedent, and the part after 'then' as the consequent.
In Arabic, the conditional particle is often 'كلما' or 'إن'.
2
Check for a real connection
Determine whether the antecedent and consequent are linked by a rational, legal, or customary necessity. If yes, it is luzumiyya.
If the connection is merely coincidental, it is ittifaqiyya (next lesson).
3
Classify the connection type
If luzumiyya, decide whether the connection is because of a shared cause (e.g., light and stars being hidden both result from the sun's position) or mutual dependence (e.g., father and son imply each other).
Shared cause: both are effects of a common cause. Correlative: each entails the other.
4
Apply to new examples
Given a new conditional, practise identifying whether it is luzumiyya and, if so, which subtype.
Use examples from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator' (shared cause? no, but necessary implication).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وَالمُشْتَرِكَانِ فِي سَبَبٍ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الضَّوْءُ مَوْجُوداً، فَالكَوَاكِبُ خَفِيَّةٌ .
والمُتَضَايفَانِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ أَباً لِعَمْرٍو، فَعَمْرُو ابْنٌ لَهُ .
- وَإِلَى اتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي تَكُونُ الصُّحْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِغَيْرِ مُوجِبٍ؛ بِأَنِ اتَّفَقَ فَقَطْ صِدْقُهُمَا فِي الوُجُودِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ، فَالإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانٌ، فَإِنَّ أَحَداً مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يَقْتَضِي الآخَرَ ، لَا عَقْلاً وَلَا شَرْعاً وَلَا عَادَةٌ .
قوله : (لِغَيْرِ مُوجِب أي بأن لا يقتضي صدق أحدهما الآخر بوجه.
قوله : ( كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ... إلخ) أي : فإنَّ طلوع الشَّمس لا إشعار له بحيوانية الإنسان، ولا يقتضيها لا عقلاً، ولا شرعاً، ولا عادةً.
ونظير المثال : إن كانتِ السَّماء فوق الأرض، فالعسل حلو.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. The two [types] that share a cause: e.g., 'Whenever light exists, the stars are hidden.' And the two correlatives: e.g., 'Whenever Zaid is the father of Amr, then Amr is his son.' And [the third type] is the coincidental, which is: the companionship between its two sides is without an imperative; i.e., they merely happen to be true in existence; e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal,' for one of the two sides does not necessitate the other, not rationally, not legally, and not customarily. His statement: (without an imperative) meaning that the truth of one does not necessitate the truth of the other in any way. His statement: (Whenever the sun rises... etc.) meaning: the rising of the sun gives no indication of man's animality, nor does it necessitate it rationally, legally, or customarily. And an analogous example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.'
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of conditional propositions (muttasila): luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (coincidental). It begins by giving two subtypes of luzumiyya: those sharing a cause (e.g., light causes stars to be hidden) and correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship). Then it defines ittifaqiyya as a conditional where the connection is without any necessary tie; the antecedent and consequent merely happen to be true together, as in 'whenever the sun rises, man is an animal.' The commentary explains that the example shows no rational, legal, or customary necessitation. Another example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.' This lesson establishes the basic classification for conditional propositions used in Islamic logic.

Coincidental Conditionals: The Illusion of Connection
Author defines ittifaqiyya conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize ittifaqiyya conditionals and explain why they lack logical necessity.

Core Idea
Ittifaqiyya conditionals are mere coincidences; no necessity ties the two parts together.

Sometimes two true statements happen to occur together without any cause-and-effect relationship. For instance, 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal' is true but the sun's rising has nothing to do with humanity's animal nature. This type is called ittifaqiyya (coincidental). Master this concept to avoid mistaking accidental co-occurrence for logical implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional and locate the antecedent and consequent
As before, identify the two parts. For ittifaqiyya, the connection is not necessary.
Example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.' Both are true but unrelated.
2
Test for necessity
Check if the antecedent logically, legally, or customarily necessitates the consequent. If not, it is ittifaqiyya.
The author mentions three types of necessity: rational ('aql), legal (shar'), and customary ('ada).
3
Confirm it is not luzumiyya
Verify that there is no shared cause or correlativity. If the connection is purely coincidental, it is ittifaqiyya.
In the sun-man example, no shared cause links sunrise to being human.
4
Practice with new examples
Given a conditional, determine whether it is ittifaqiyya and state why.
Use examples like 'If it is Friday, then water is wet.'

Broader Ittifaqiyya and the Example of 'Law lam yakhaf Allah'
Author expands definition of ittifaqiyya and discusses the authenticity of a famous example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the broader interpretation of the coincidental conditional and critically assess the authenticity of its common example.

Core Idea
The broader ittifaqiyya removes imagined contradictions, not logical ones.

We have seen the standard definition of the coincidental conditional. Now the author expands it to a broader sense where the consequent is simply true. This serves a unique purpose: to dissolve an apparent conflict between two facts. The famous example, 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him,' is examined critically for its authenticity, showing that even widely used examples require careful sourcing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the broader ittifaqiyya definition
The text says the broader coincidental is when the consequent is true, and its truth is not incompatible with the truth of the antecedent. This differs from the earlier definition where the antecedent is false. Notice that here the consequent is true independently.
2
Identify the purpose
The phrase 'to remove an imagined conflict' is key. The example shows that the lack of fear is imagined to conflict with lack of disobedience, but the conditional removes that imagination.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him' appears to imply that fearlessness would lead to obedience, which is counterintuitive. The broader ittifaqiyya shows this is a coincidental conditional, not a causal one.
4
Examine the critical authenticity discussion
The author reports that some scholars considered it a hadith, but others like al-Subki, al-Iraqi, and al-Suyuti found no evidence. The only trace is a weak chain in al-Hilya. This highlights the importance of verifying sources even for logical examples.

وَقَدْ تُفَسَّرُ «الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ) بِأَعَمَّ مِمَّا مَرَّ ، وَهِيَ : أَنْ يَكُونَ تَالِيهَا صَادِقاً، وَلَا يُنَافِي في صِدْقِهِ صِدْقَ المُقَدَّم»، وَفَائِدَتُهَا : رَفْعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ التَّنَافُرِ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ؛ نَحْوُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ« ، قَدْ تُتَوَهَّمُ مُنَافَاةُ عَدَمِ الخَوْفِ لِعَدَمِ المَعْصِيَةِ، وَأَنَّ مَنْ لَمْ يَخَفْ يَعْصِي، فَرَفَعَ ذَلِكَ التَّوَهُمَ.
قوله: (نَحْوُ : «لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ» جعله السَّنوسي تبعاً للعقباني حديثاً، ومثله في المطول.
وقيل : هو أثر عن عُمر بن الخطاب رضي الله تعالى عنه .
وذكر بهاء الدين السبكي في شرح التلخيص، وكذا العراقي ووالده ابن زرعة والسيوطي : يوجد في شيء من كتب الحديث حديثاً، ولا أثراً مع الفحص عنه .
نعم؛ في الحلية» عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما : أنَّ النَّبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قال في سالم مولى أبي حذيفة : أنَّ سالماً شديد الحب الله ، لو كان لا يخافُ الله ما عصاه، وفي إسناده: ابن لهيعة، وهو ضعيف .
And the coincidental may be interpreted more broadly than what preceded: that its consequent is true, and the truth of the antecedent does not contradict its truth. Its benefit is to remove an imagined conflict between two matters; e.g., 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' There may be an imagined conflict between lack of fear and lack of disobedience, and that whoever does not fear disobeys, so it removes that imagination. His statement: (e.g., 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him'): al-Sanusi, following al-Uqbabi, considered it a hadith; and similarly in al-Mutawwal. It is said it is an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab. Bahauddin al-Subki in his commentary on al-Talkhis, as well as al-Iraqi and his father Ibn Zar'a and al-Suyuti, mentioned: it is not found in any book of hadith as a hadith or athar despite searching. However, in al-Hilya from Ibn Umar: the Prophet said about Salim, the freed slave of Abu Hudhayfa: 'Salim intensely loves Allah. If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' But its chain includes Ibn Lahia, who is weak.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage expands the definition of the coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) to include cases where the consequent is known to be true, and the purpose is to remove an apparent contradiction. The famous example 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him' is given, and then the commentary critically examines its authenticity. Various scholars such as al-Sanusi, al-Uqbabi, and the author of al-Mutawwal considered it a hadith, but others like al-Subki, al-Iraqi, and al-Suyuti found no trace of it in hadith books. A weak narration exists in al-Hilya with a weak chain. This lesson refines the understanding of the example: though widely used, it is not authentically prophetic.

Al-Sa'd's Linguistic Approach to Quranic Conditionals
Author provides Quranic examples and explains al-Sa'd's view on a key verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical pitfalls of interpreting Quranic conditionals as syllogisms and apply al-Sa'd's linguistic approach to similar verses.

Core Idea
Quranic conditionals follow linguistic usage, not syllogistic form.

In this lesson, we examine al-Sa'd's analysis of a Quranic verse that might appear to be a logical syllogism. He shows that interpreting it as such leads to false conclusions and that it is actually an implicative conditional based on language. This lesson refines our understanding of how Quranic conditionals work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent syllogism
The verse 'If Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear' seems like a first figure categorical syllogism with middle term 'He would have made them hear'. But note that the conclusion 'they would have turned away' is not explicitly stated; it is derived by omitting the middle term. This reveals a hidden assumption.
Al-Sa'd refutes this interpretation by showing the major premise lacks universality.
2
Recognize the failure of the syllogistic reading
If we treat the verse as a categorical syllogism, the major premise 'Whoever Allah makes hear, turns away' is not universal. Just as 'Man is an animal, an animal is a horse' yields a false conclusion due to a particular major, this syllogism fails. Moreover, if Allah knew good in them, they would not turn away but accept.
The invalidity arises from the absence of a universal major premise in the first figure.
3
Understand al-Sa'd's alternative
Al-Sa'd classifies the verse as an implicative conditional (لزومية) based on linguistic usage (قاعدة اللغة). This means the conditional indicates a necessary link in the context of speech, not a logical deduction. The verse's purpose is to emphasize that the people's turning away is not due to Allah's lack of knowledge but their own bad nature.
This interpretation avoids attributing ignorance or foolishness to Allah.
4
Apply the distinction to other Quranic examples
The passage also cites verses like 'If all the trees on earth were pens...' and 'If the sea were ink...'. These are examples of ittifaqiyya (coincidental) conditionals that remove imagined conflicts. They are not logical syllogisms but linguistic constructions to emphasize a point.
Recognizing the linguistic basis protects against misinterpreting Allah's words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمِثْلُهُ: ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ مِن شَجَرَةٍ أَفَلَامُ [لقمان: ۲۷] الآيَةَ، ولو كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَتِ رَبِّي ﴾ [الكهف: ۱۰۹] ،الآيَةَ قُل لَّوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٥٤] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا وَهُم مُعْرِضُونَ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي الكَلَامِ كَثِيرٌ . وَأَمَّا : ﴿ وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيل اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ.
قوله : (وَمِثْلُهُ: ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ .... إلخ) أي : فإنَّه ربَّما يُتوهَّمُ التَّنافر بين كون الأشجار أقلاماً ، وعدم نفاد كلمات الله تعالى فرفع هذا التّوهُم وصار المعنى: أنَّ كلمات الله تعالى لا نفاد لها، مع كون الأشجار أقلاماً، فكيف مع عدم ذلك؟!
قوله: (وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ ) يعني: أنَّ التَّولّي لازم عـلـى تـقـديـر الإسماع، فكيف على تقدير عدم الإسماع؟! فهو دائم الوجود.
وهذا إن فسِّر «التَّولّي» بـ : «عدم الانتفاع» كما في «الكشاف»، وأمَّا لو فسر بـ: «الإعراض» كما في السَّعد، فهي وارده على الاستعمال المشهور ; أي: من قبيل استعمال «لو» الامتناعيَّة; نحو: «لو جئتني لأكرمتك».
قوله : (فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ: إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ) ذكر ذلك في «المطول» بعد أن زيف قول من قال : إنَّ الآية على صورة قياس اقتراني، والحد الوسط هو : «لأسمعهم»، فإذا حذف عند الإنتاج، قيل : ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لتولُّوا»، وهذا محال; لأنه على تقدير علم الخير فيهم لا يحصل منهم التّولّي، بل الإذعان والقبول، وإنَّما جاء البطلان من فقد كلّية الكبرى في الشكل الأوَّل ; لأنَّها هنا مهملةٌ ; كقولك : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان فرس» فالنتيجة باطلة. وكيف يقال : إنَّ الله تعالى أتى بقياس ليس فيه شرط الإنتاج ; لأنَّه إن لم يعلمه فهو جهل، وإن علمه وأتى به على خلاف ذلك فهو سفه، فالحقُ : أنَّها واردة على قاعدة اللُّغة ; يعني : أنَّ سبب ...
His statement: (And similar to it: 'If all the trees on earth were pens...' the verse, and 'If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord...' the verse, 'Say: Even if you had been in your houses, those destined to be killed would have come out to their resting places,' and similar to that: 'And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away while they were averse,' and many such in speech. As for 'If Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear,' al-Sa'd said: it is of the implicative type, occurring on the basis of language. His statement: (And similar to it: 'If all the trees on earth...' etc.) meaning: there may be imagined conflict between the trees being pens and the non-exhaustion of Allah's words, so this removes that imagination, and the meaning becomes: Allah's words are never exhausted, even if trees were pens, so what about without that? His statement: (And similar to that: 'And if He had made them hear') means: turning away is necessary on the assumption of hearing, so how about on the assumption of not hearing? So it is always existent. This is if 'turning away' is interpreted as 'not benefiting' as in al-Kashshaf. But if interpreted as 'aversion' as in al-Sa'd, then it occurs on the common usage, i.e., from the category of using 'law' of negation, like 'If you had come to me, I would have honored you.' His statement: (Al-Sa'd said: It is of the implicative type, occurring on the basis of language) He mentioned that in al-Mutawwal after refuting the claim that the verse is in the form of a categorical syllogism with the middle term 'He would have made them hear', and when omitted in the production, it is said: 'If Allah had known any good in them, they would have turned away' — but this is impossible because on the assumption of known good, they do not turn away but rather submit and accept. The invalidity arises from the lack of universality of the major in the first figure, because it is here particular, like saying 'Man is an animal, and an animal is a horse' — the conclusion is false. How can it be said that Allah brought a syllogism lacking the condition of productivity? For if He did not know it, it is ignorance; and if He knew it and brought it contrary, it is foolishness. The truth: it occurs on the basis of language, meaning that the reason ...

Commentary
This passage provides several Quranic examples of the broader coincidental conditional, explaining how they remove imagined conflicts. For instance, the verse about trees and ink removes the notion that Allah's words could be exhausted; the verse about those destined for death shows that fate is unavoidable. It then discusses al-Sa'd's analysis of the verse 'If Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear' as an implicative conditional based on linguistic usage, not as a logical syllogism. The commentary refutes a mistaken attempt to interpret it as a categorical syllogism, pointing out the error in the major premise's universality. This lesson critically refines the understanding of Quranic conditionals.
